Big Health-Care Ideas
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Big Health-Care Ideas
1,021 words
21 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Washington denizens who say the Bush Administration is out of domestic ideas haven't been paying
attention. The more we look at the fine print in the health-care reforms President Bush is now stumping for,
the more we see the potential for the most sweeping and beneficial changes in half a century.
One way to think about the Bush reforms is as HillaryCare in reverse. The former first lady sought to mandate
employer-based coverage and then hold down costs by brute government force ("managed care"). Mr. Bush
is instead attempting to revitalize the private market for individual health insurance, so employees are
ultimately less dependent on their bosses' coverage and can ultimately buy the kind of insurance that makes
better sense for them. Along the way, Americans would also become wiser consumers of health-care
services.
The reason most Americans get their health insurance from their employers is an artifact of history and the
tax code. Companies subject to wage and price controls during World War II used health insurance to attract
workers, and Congress later made employer-provided insurance tax deductible. Employers also have another
huge advantage if they are large enough to create their own risk pool (or "self-insure"), since they are then
allowed to evade expensive state insurance regulations and abide instead by a more rational federal scheme
known by the acronym Erisa.
These tax and regulatory advantages have worked well for those lucky enough to be inside the employer
system, but they have also made it cheaper for employers to offer gold-plated health plans. That means
consumers, in turn, have had little incentive to pay attention to prices, contributing to double-digit annual
increases in health-care inflation. That burden is now starting to affect the bottom line of employers, who are
passing along more of those costs to workers in the form of higher premiums, or less overtly in smaller wage
increases.
For those who don't work for large employers, meanwhile, the current system is simply unfair. Individuals and
small businesses wanting to buy insurance must comply with expensive state regulations. And individuals
must do it with after-tax dollars. This is what Mr. Bush, to his credit, wants to correct.
---
The centerpiece of his strategy is the Health Savings Account, which combines an insurance policy with a
deductible of $1,050 or more with a tax-free savings account to help people pay pre-deductible expenses.
Critics say the relatively high deductible makes HSAs work only for the "healthy and wealthy."
But in fact HSAs are what health insurance would have looked like all along if the employer-insurance tax
exemption never existed. That is, insurance for catastrophic illness to prevent destitution but not for routine
care. (Think of it this way: What's the deductible on your car insurance?) And with more than three million
HSA policies already in existence, there is plenty of data showing high customer satisfaction among policy
holders of all ages and incomes.
Mr. Bush is proposing to supercharge HSAs in several important ways. He'd allow HSA premiums to be paid
from the tax-free savings account, which have the potential to rise in value every year. And he'd give
individual purchasers a credit for the 15% Social Security and Medicare payroll taxes that they and their
employers have already paid on those dollars. That creative idea would fully equalize the tax treatment for all
insurance purchases.
The Bush proposals would also make HSA policies fully portable and easier to buy. For individuals, this
means breaking down the current barrier to purchasing insurance across state lines, an archaic system that
makes no sense in an age where interstate banking is commonplace. And if the purchaser is an employer,
Mr. Bush wants to change the current self-insurance rules to allow companies to buy portable policies that
could follow employees much like their 401(k) retirement accounts.

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The economic benefits could be profound. No longer would individuals have "job lock," meaning that they fear
leaving a job they otherwise dislike because they're afraid to lose their insurance. Especially for someone with
an illness in the family, this would be liberating and provide more health-care security. Genuine insurance
portability would also help the overall economy, by freeing employees to move to jobs where they would both
be happier and better utilized. Those who dismiss HSAs as another "tax shelter for the rich" are ignoring the
benefits to moderately paid workers.
On HSAs, finally, Mr. Bush also wants to increase the allowable contribution limits. Thus contributions up to
the out-of-pocket maximum (including all co-pays), not just the deductible, would be allowed for most people.
This could be as much as $10,500 a year for families, half that for individuals. The White House proposals
would also allow employers to make larger deposits to the HSA accounts of individuals with chronic
conditions such as diabetes, in order to make the accounts attractive for them as well.
---
Another proposal goes by the name of Association Health Plans, which were originally proposed as a way of
allowing small businesses to band together for greater purchasing power when they buy insurance. It's a
good idea, and the President wants to make it better by allowing not just businesses but voluntary
associations like churches to participate too. He's also still pushing to rein in outrageous malpractice
insurance costs. And he wants to facilitate the consumer-driven system he's touting by encouraging doctors
and insurers to be more transparent about prices.
We might quibble with some other details, especially the "refundable" tax credit. But all things considered, Mr.
Bush has assembled an ambitious and mutually reinforcing set of health reforms. The key principles are
equal tax treatment and portability. These ideas give Republicans in Congress a chance to pass reforms that
address genuine problems and are consistent with their free-market principles. Let's hope they understand
their historic opportunity.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: HSAs Won't Benefit Those Who Need Insurance Most" -- WSJ Mar.
7, 2006)

Stocks Greet Bernanke's Testimony With Dow's Highest Close Since 2001
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Greet Bernanke's Testimony With Dow's Highest Close Since 2001
By E.S. Browning
575 words
16 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average pushed up to its highest close since 2001, as investors displayed
measured enthusiasm for a Greenspan-esque Ben Bernanke.
In his first congressional testimony as Federal Reserve chairman, Mr. Bernanke sounded much like his
predecessor Alan Greenspan -- albeit more comprehensible. He said the Fed is likely to keep raising interest
rates for a while to prevent inflation, but investors have gotten used to that prospect, seeing the economy as
strong enough to grow anyway. Mr. Bernanke also signaled an intention to base policies on economic data,
as did Mr. Greenspan.
The market's reaction was exuberant, but not irrationally so. After surging 136 points on Tuesday, the Dow
tacked on another 30.58 points, or 0.28%, to finish at 11058.97. It's up 3.2% this year, the index's highest
level since June 2001, three months before the 9/11 attacks. Other major indexes also rose, though they
remained below their January highs.
"People see the economy as pretty strong" after Tuesday's report of surprisingly powerful retail sales, said
Tim Heekin, director of trading at San Francisco brokerage firm Thomas Weisel Partners. "They understand
that the Fed has got a responsibility to keep the economy and inflation in check, and they have gotten
comfortable with slightly higher rates."
Investors also welcomed the continuing slump in oil prices, which left crude futures down 3.2% at $57.65 in
New York, the year's biggest percentage decline and the lowest finish in almost two months. That benefited
airlines and trucking companies, sending the Dow Jones Transportation Average up 0.78% to 4442.29,
another in a recent string of record finishes.
Since June 2001, when stocks were falling amid concerns that stock prices were too high compared with
profits, the situation has improved. In 2001, the Dow industrials traded at 23.5 times the component
companies' profits for the past 12 months. Today, they trade at a less-expensive 21.6 times. The historical
average, however, is in the teens.
Financial stocks were among the day's leaders, amid hopes that a strong economy and limited rate increases
will benefit their business. Investors said they feel more comfortable with financial stocks leading the way
than with oil producers, since rising oil prices are bad for many other companies.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.35%, or 4.47 points, to 1280.00, up 2.5% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 0.63%, or 14.26 points, to 2276.43, ahead 3.2% for 2006. Gold fell,
although it remains up 4% this year.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.01%, or 0.03 point, to 205.15.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.73 billion shares traded, 2,092 stocks rose and 1,201 fell.
Bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 4/32, or $1.25 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 4.604%. The 30-year bond was up 9/32 to yield 4.577%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 117.86 yen, up from 117.40, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.1887 from $1.1918.

Common Sense: Yield-Curve Puzzle Deepens on Desire For 30-Year Bond
Personal Finance
Common Sense: Yield-Curve Puzzle Deepens on Desire For 30-Year Bond
By James B. Stewart SmartMoney
865 words
15 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
D4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TALK ABOUT REVIVALS. Last week the Treasury brought back the long-dormant 30-year bond, and it was
greeted by investors like the return of the Rolling Stones.
Buyers fell all over themselves to hand over their money for three decades in exchange for what? A measly
4.48% in annual interest. That was not only lower than the 10-year rate of 4.53%, but also prevailing
short-term rates of 4.64%, which means the yield curve was inverted across the full length of the maturity
spectrum, known as a perfect inversion. I last wrote about the implications of a flattening yield curve when it
briefly inverted in late November; it can no longer be dismissed as an aberration.
Although I've been happily parking my fixed-income assets along the short end of the maturity spectrum,
reaping superior rates for minimal risk and commitment of time, this situation does give me pause. An
inverted yield curve has preceded every recession since the mid-1960s. Add a "humped" yield curve to the
mix, in which the longer end of the curve is inverted, and such curves have preceded every recession since
the mid-1950s, and possibly much earlier (the data are incomplete). While the inverted curve of 1966-67
didn't result in a recession, it was followed by one quarter of modest economic contraction.
It's hard to dismiss such a track record as sheer coincidence, and there's a wealth of academic research
exploring the predictive capacity of the yield curve. The keen level of current interest in the topic was evident
when I went to Google and searched for "inverted yield curve."
High among the results was a 2004 Auburn University dissertation by Paul Francis Cwik, hardly a household
name, even among economists. I found it a useful (if highly technical) overview, and if you find the subject
fascinating, you may want to peruse it. As I understood it, Mr. Cwik argues the inverted curve is indeed a
harbinger of recession, but why that would be the case remains the subject of considerable academic
head-scratching.
The most prevalent explanation is that buyers of the long bond accept the lower yield in anticipation of a
recession, which is likely to drive long rates even lower. In this theory, such expectations in the markets
become self-fulfilling. But I have yet to see any empirical data to support this notion.
In any event, inverted yield curves rarely last long. At this juncture, either long rates will rise, short rates will
fall, or some combination of the two will occur, restoring the normal upward slope. You don't want to own
long-term bonds when rates are rising.
To me, this makes it even more paradoxical that investors were so keen for those low-yielding 30-year bonds.
It wouldn't take many cuts in short-term rates for the Fed to restore the slope of the yield curve. But how
much lower can long-term rates fall? True, rates in deflationary Japan got close to zero, but the possibility of
deflation in the U.S. seems a distant memory with gold trading at well over $500 an ounce. The likelihood
that long-term rates will go above 4.48% over the next 30 years strikes me as close to a certainty.
And what if the yield curve turns out to be wrong this time, and there isn't any recession? Not only will
economists return to the drawing board, but that means economic growth will continue, putting more upward
pressure on long-term rates.
This seems yet another argument for investing in shorter maturities at today's relatively high rates. Short
maturities, like the one- to three-year bank certificates of deposit I've recommended, have little risk of
principal erosion even if rates rise. Meanwhile, you can collect close to 5%, and if the Fed continues its
rate-rising campaign, you'll soon be able to earn even more.
The situation is more worrisome for stock investors, since a recession typically takes a harsher toll on stock
prices. But the stock market seems to be shrugging off the yield curve, and the stock market is also an

--- Page 16 ---
Page 16 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
economic indicator of recessions, though a far less reliable one than the yield curve. Moreover, if the Fed is
indeed close to the end of its campaign to raise rates, this has historically been a positive factor for stocks.
I don't see the inverted yield curve to be cause for alarm. It may reflect worrisome forces that economists are
still trying to figure out, but it isn't going to affect whether consumers go shopping, workers boost their
productivity or businesses invest in new technology. If it was really a certainty that an inverted yield curve
causes a recession, then we'd already be in one.
---
James B. Stewart is a columnist for SmartMoney magazine and SmartMoney.com. Unlike Dow Jones staff
reporters, he may have positions in the stocks he writes about. For his past columns, see:
www.smartmoney.com/wsj_common .

further signs that the central bank will continue its campaign to fend off inflation.
Traders raised concerns about the Treasury-bond yield curve, which remains "inverted," meaning rates on
short-term government debt remain higher than those on securities with longer maturities. Such a scenario
has in the past preceded an economic -- and stock-market -- downturn.
"The inverted yield curve is definitely bearish for the market," said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at
brokerage firm SG Cowen. "We could come out of it, but it's not a very good sign."

oil falling 60 cents to $61.24 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Gold prices, which had hit a
25-year high earlier this month, shed $11.20, or 2%, to $539 a troy ounce.

Web-Based Bonds For the Rest of Us --- How Uncle Sam and Others Are Helping Small Investors Trade Debt With Online Ease
Web-Based Bonds For the Rest of Us --- How Uncle Sam and Others Are Helping Small Investors
Trade Debt With Online Ease
By Serena Ng
1,466 words
11 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IT'S GETTING EASIER to load up on debt. Traditionally, people who wanted to invest in bonds found it rough
going, aside from using mutual funds. Investors interested in bonds quickly encountered a murky world
designed to cater to Wall Street brokers and banking titans, not the little guy.
That is starting to change. Aging baby boomers have an appetite for bonds, which are less risky than stocks
and usually pay regular interest. In response, investment firms are offering new tools for investors to compare
prices and trade corporate, municipal and government debt. Fidelity Investments started offering a new online
service at fidelity.com about a year ago that lets individuals buy and sell bonds much like stocks.
Just last fall, the U.S. government got into the act, making it possible for people to buy a range of government
debt online without fees or paperwork. Since then, the site treasurydirect.gov has sold more than $2 billion in
Treasurys to individual investors.
Smaller financial firms are also pushing into the business. "We will do more bond trades this month than
equity trades," says W. David Hemingway of Zions Bancorp., a small Salt Lake City bank that sells bonds
online at bondsforless.com.
For individual investors, bond investing remains more challenging than stock trading. For one thing, most
bonds don't trade on an exchange, and thus investors often aren't able to get the best price available when
they buy a bond through a broker. And while a company like General Motors might have a single stock that
investors can trade, that same firm can have all kinds of different bonds sporting different yields and risk
levels.
"The bond market is still a fairly opaque environment for individual investors," says Stuart Snyder, a former
stockbroker from Pennsylvania who now manages his own money.
For investors with the least tolerance for risk, Treasury securities remain the gold standard. Since they are
issued by the government, there is no risk of default unless the government itself fails. Interest rates are
lower, but investments like these are particularly well-suited for older investors looking to protect their nest
eggs. One catch: If you don't hold these bonds to maturity, you have to pay a commission to sell them.
To reduce risk even more, investors can use a strategy called laddering. This involves buying securities with
different maturities and yields, and it gives investors added flexibility if interest rates change.
Investinginbonds.com has a laddering primer.
The easiest way is to buy Treasurys at treasurydirect.gov. After opening an account there, it is possible to
buy a broad array of bonds, notes and bills with maturity dates that range from four weeks to 30 years and
can be purchased in lots as small as $1,000.
All of this is a big improvement over the old system. The Treasury has sold securities to individuals for
decades, but that process used to involve a good deal of old-fashioned paperwork. Now, however, everything
can be done online, and interest payments are automatically deposited into the investor's bank account.
Treasurys may be safe, but other bonds -- which are more risky -- offer better payouts. The yield on
investment-grade U.S. corporate bonds is currently about one percentage point higher than on a comparable
10-year Treasury note. (On a $10,000 investment, that is an extra $100 per year.) So-called junk bonds,
which are the riskiest kind of bond, pay still-higher yields.
For investors looking for better returns but not a lot of hassle, mutual funds or exchange-traded funds are an
option. Bond funds come with annual fees -- typically about 1.25% of the funds' assets, according to
investment-research firm Morningstar Inc. For ETFs (which are funds that trade on a stock exchange),

--- Page 23 ---
Page 23 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
management fees and expenses are much smaller, typically about 0.2% of assets, but brokers charge
commissions when they are bought and sold, the way they do with a stock.
Funds let investors spread out their risk, since a fund holds a broad array of investments, not just a few. They
also offer a chance at capital gains, because the value of the bonds in the fund might appreciate.
The downside: The income generated by funds can fluctuate as the makeup of bonds in the portfolio shifts
and as fund managers buy and sell securities. In addition, the underlying value of the funds -- which is based
on the prices of the bonds they hold -- can also fluctuate and can be particularly volatile in periods of rapidly
rising or falling interest rates. These changing "net asset values" mean bond-fund returns aren't a sure thing.
Last year was a case in point. The average total return on bond mutual funds was 2.26%, according to data
from Morningstar. That was less than the 2.81% return of Treasurys, as measured by Merrill Lynch's U.S.
Treasury Master Index, which covers securities with maturities of more than one year.
Anyone wanting to chase the potentially lofty returns of junk bonds are best advised to do it by investing in
funds. In a large, diversified mutual-fund portfolio, one default wouldn't wipe you out.
For investors who want full control over the timing and size of their payments -- but want higher returns than
Treasurys offer -- a portfolio of corporate bonds or municipal bonds could work. Typically, bonds pay out
"coupons" (or interest payments) at fixed intervals and also promise to pay back the amount borrowed at a
specified date in the future.
Individuals saving for specific events -- like a child's college education -- or who want a precise monthly
income, can invest in a collection of bonds whose payment schedules meet these objectives. For example,
an investor who wants monthly income of $3,000 could buy six bonds that each pay out coupons of $3,000
twice a year, but during different months.
This can get tricky. Financial advisers generally say that this approach only makes sense for households that
have at least $50,000 to invest in this way. The reasons: Bonds like these are usually sold in minimum
amounts of $5,000, and you probably want to have at least 10 bonds to be sufficiently diversified.
On top of that, this kind of investment requires much closer monitoring than, say, a mutual fund. (For
instance, you might want to get out of one of those bonds if, say, the company started go get weaker.)
Most retail brokerage firms will take individual orders for bonds. But the lack of transparency in the market
works against the individual. Bond prices quoted by brokers often contain an undisclosed "markup," which is
a fee typically ranging from 1% to 3% of the bond's face value. In many cases, the smaller the purchase, the
higher the markup.
A small number of brokerage firms, including Fidelity and Zions Direct, have recently begun "un-bundling" the
markup on bonds. Instead, they are charging investors flat fees. Fidelity lists fees ranging from 50 cents to $5
a bond, subject to a minimum of $19.95 and a maximum of $500. Zions has a flat fee of $10.95 a trade,
regardless of size.
Moreover, more Web sites are popping up listing bond prices, such as investinginbonds.com, and
broker-dealer JB Hanauer &Co. (bondsearch123.com). Some sites, including those run by BondsOnline
Group Inc. (bondsonline.com) and Fidelity also offer tools that allow investors to compare the yields and risks
of different securities.
One of the biggest issues in bonds is taxes. Bond mutual funds can distribute unexpected capital gains
because they also pay out profits from their bond trades. That can lead to unexpected taxes at year end.
Investors living in high-tax states may prefer to buy tax-exempt municipal bonds to shield themselves from
state and federal income taxes. Individuals investing from 401(k) plans or Individual Retirement Accounts
don't have to pay taxes on the income these accounts generate until they begin withdrawing money from the
accounts.
Another issue, which is growing in significance, is the alternative minimum tax. Interest paid out by some
municipal bonds falls under the category of "private activity" and may be included in the computation of the
AMT, a tax designed for wealthy individuals but which is affecting an increasing number of middle-class
Americans.
"Investors should always check with their brokers and advisers to see if any of their bonds are subject to AMT
treatment," says Jill Schlesinger of StrategicPoint Investment Advisors. Municipal bonds in this category may
include securities issued for airports, certain housing agencies, and not-for-profit organizations.

Page 24 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Review / Books: Prophet With the Voice of Gold
Review / Books:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Review / Books: Prophet With the Voice of Gold
By James Grant
1,039 words
10 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
W3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A GODLY HERO
By Michael Kazin
(Knopf, 374 pages, $30)
WILLIAM JENNINGS Bryan (1860-1925) was the Paul Bunyan of the larynx. The 6,000 speeches he
delivered over the course of 30 years made him wealthy. Their effect on the country he loved was slightly
different.
Bryan -- he of the Scopes trial and the Cross of Gold speech; orator, journalist, evangelist and perennial
Democratic presidential hopeful -- tirelessly worked to take from the rich to give to the poor. Or, rather, to take
from the other rich. If you were wondering whom to thank for the federal income tax, you may begin by
thanking Bryan. His voice was the wind in the sails of what became the 16th Amendment to the Constitution.
His latest biographer, Michael Kazin, professor of history at Georgetown University, does thank Bryan: "In his
advocacy of a stronger, more interventionist state, Bryan calmed his party's ancestral dread of federal power.
Every Democratic president from Woodrow Wilson to Lyndon Johnson would reap the rewards of his
apostasy."
This is a fine book, never mind that both the author and his hero could have profited from a little closer
reading of Adam Smith. Mr. Kazin succeeds in rescuing Bryan's reputation from H.L. Mencken's mocking
laughter. To the Sage of Baltimore, the orator from Nebraska was an idiot. Not so, Mr. Kazin is able to
demonstrate. Glib, sentimental and bull-headed, yes. But the unreflective Bryan was the herald of the modern
American mega-state.
For which achievement he was correctly excoriated by the conservative Democrats of his time. One of this
now nearly extinct tribe, the journalist Frank Kent, writing in 1928, arraigned Bryan for leading the party of
Thomas Jefferson into electoral oblivion by turning it away from its small-government, libertarian principles.
Bryan ran for president on the Democratic ticket in 1896, 1900 and 1908. But the U.S. economy grew and
grew, and he lost and lost.
As a biographical judge and jury, Mr. Kazin is admirably even-handed. He stands up for Bryan but is
clear-eyed about his faults and confusions. A racist, Bryan was also a devout Presbyterian. A pacifist, he
volunteered for service during the Spanish-American War (and later directed that his bones be interred
among the nonpacifists in the Arlington National Cemetery). A leftist, his writ was Scripture, not Marx.
The critical weakness in Mr. Kazin's biography is that Bryan's class enemies never get to take the stand in
their own defense. The Commoner detested the money changers and Pharisees, among whom he lumped
the railroad builders, bankers, corporate entrepreneurs and stockbrokers. Against these supposed parasites,
Bryan held up for veneration the farmers, professionals and small businessmen -- the "producers," he styled
them. Exactly what were the sins of the enterprising and successful rich? How could the American standard
of living ever have been raised without the profit-seeking work of free individuals? On these critical questions
Mr. Kazin and his hero are silent.
"God grant that I may use it wisely," prayed Bryan as he realized the power of his own speaking voice. On the
authority of Mary, his whiney wife, Bryan's voice carried for three city blocks. He earned $2,000 a week on the
summertime Chautauqua circuit, holding forth on religious and political topics to small-town audiences across
America. "He invested nearly all of it in government bonds and real estate," Mr. Kazin relates of these
plutocratic honoraria. "Speculating in the stock market was, of course, out of the question."

--- Page 25 ---
Page 25 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Bryan, and everybody else in turn-of-the-century America, was paid in dollars as good as gold; you could
exchange $20.67 for an ounce of bullion (now an ounce will set you back by $570). Bryan hated hard money.
How much better, he mused, if the "producing classes" could discharge their debts in the cheaper, more
inflation-prone paper kind. "Having behind us the producing masses of this nation and the world," he
famously thundered at the 1896 Democratic National Convention in Chicago, "supported by the commercial
interests, the laboring interests and the toilers everywhere, we will answer their demand for a gold standard
by saying to them: You shall not press down upon the brow of labor this crown of thorns, you shall not crucify
mankind upon a cross of gold." After the crazed delegates regained their composure, Gov. John Altgeld of
Illinois turned to his friend Clarence Darrow and said: "I have been thinking over Bryan's speech. What did he
say, anyhow?"
Bryan's political program was self-explanatory. The power of the federal government should be enlarged and
the liberty of the individual consequently diminished, said the Commoner time and again. Conservatives like
Grover Cleveland discounted these cries as radical ravings, little suspecting how soon they would enter the
public-policy mainstream. A graduated income tax? An "elastic" currency? Federal deposit insurance?
Federal crop subsidies? Bryan and his fellow populists were there long before Franklin D. Roosevelt.
In 1925, in Dayton, Tenn., Darrow cross-examined Bryan in the trial of John Scopes, a science teacher who
had run afoul of the Tennessee law prohibiting the teaching of Darwinian evolution. "Do you believe a whale
swallowed Jonah?" Darrow demanded of the man who did, indeed, profess to believe the literal truth of the
Bible. "Do you believe Joshua made the sun stand still?" Bryan, near death, stumbled and stammered.
What he wanted to say, and -- as Mr. Kazin relates -- did say, over and over from countless lecterns, was that
there could be no morality without religion. On this point, at least, Bryan could claim an impeccable
intellectual lineage. A century and a half earlier, John Adams and Sam Adams, among other Founders, had
said the very same thing. Ahead of his time on secular matters, Bryan was a reactionary in spiritual ones.
God and he fit easily enough into the Democratic Party of the late 19th and early 20th centuries. They would
be lost in the 21st-century version.
---
Mr. Grant, the editor of Grant's Interest Rate Observer, is the author, most recently, of "John Adams: Party of
One."

Page 26 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Yen Advances on Profit-Taking; Treasury Yields Support Dollar
Currency Trading
Yen Advances on Profit-Taking; Treasury Yields Support Dollar
By Steven Vames and Laurence Norman
710 words
8 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar fell against the yen yesterday, as profit-taking on yen-bearish bets sparked broader buying of the
Japanese currency.
Trading in the yen was mostly driven by technical factors, with investors inclined to buy yen after the currency
failed overnight to revisit the eight-week low it touched last Friday. The yen gained even more sharply versus
the euro, rising to its best levels in about two weeks in a move that brought the euro down about 1% versus
the Japanese currency yesterday.
However, the dollar had a subdued performance versus the euro and most other currencies, as a lack of
news and economic data gave markets little on which to trade. The dollar fell marginally versus the euro and
Swiss franc, and made slight gains versus the pound and the Australian and New Zealand dollars. The U.S.
currency advanced against the Canadian dollar.
Though it fell against the yen, the dollar was supported versus other major currencies, in part because of a
rise in long-term Treasury yields which brought the 10-year note's yield up about 0.03 percentage point to
near 4.58%. Falling gold prices and a decline in crude-oil prices to their lowest levels since early January
also helped the dollar hold its ground.
Versus the yen, the dollar fell to 118.09 yen from 119.02 yen late Monday.
The euro was at $1.1969, up slightly from $1.1960 late Monday.
The yen's broad advance began overnight as short-term speculative investors took profits on recent
yen-negative bets. The yen has performed poorly in recent weeks, in part because investors borrowed the
Japanese currency to fund purchases of higher-yielding foreign currencies.
Though the move to reverse bets against the yen has been seen as largely technical, some in the market
pointed at coming events as triggers for buying.
Some say they expect repatriation of overseas profits by Japanese firms to come in the weeks ahead, as the
March 31 end of the Japanese fiscal year approaches. Also, some said the Japanese currency may be
getting boosted ahead of the Bank of Japan's monetary-policy meeting today and tomorrow.
The bank isn't expected to make any changes to its zero interest-rate policy or to quantitative easing -- the
pumping of extra liquidity into the financial system. However, the BOJ has said repeatedly it hopes to be able
to begin paring back quantitative easing in the new fiscal year.
In that context, any hawkish talk from BOJ Gov. Toshihiko Fukui could give further fuel to the yen gains,
analysts said, yet few expect to see any sort of consensus for tighter policy out of the BOJ.
Versus the euro, pressure on the dollar was offset by more disappointing economic data, with a drop in
German industrial-output data in December, the second consecutive monthly decline. This followed Monday's
weak German December manufacturing-orders data.
However, analysts said that with European Central Bank officials continuing to signal that a March rate
increase is likely and with other recent euro-zone data proving stronger than expected, the euro didn't prove
overly sensitive to the data.
That wasn't true for sterling, which followed up a sharp decline Monday with a modest drop yesterday.

--- Page 27 ---
Page 27 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The pound's decline came after the British Retail Consortium's monthly survey showed that same-store sales
in January were up 0.2% on the year, making it the weakest start to a year since the survey was first
conducted in 1995.
The increase in sales was significantly weaker than the 1.5% consensus expectation.
The Bank of England is widely expected to keep rates on hold at 4.50% when it meets this week. However, in
a research note yesterday, Steven Saywell, senior currency strategist at Citigroup in London, said that while
the retail "data is not sufficiently weak for fixed income markets to price in a rate cut . . . further weak UK data
will prove the catalyst for markets to price in lower base rates."
Elsewhere, the Australian dollar was little changed at $0.7414, ahead of a Reserve Bank of Australia policy
meeting today.

Gold Prices, After Historic Rally, Fall Hard, Settle at $551 an Ounce
Commodities Report
Gold Prices, After Historic Rally, Fall Hard, Settle at $551 an Ounce
By Allen Sykora
412 words
8 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The gold rally paused, as liquidation of longer-term contracts sent futures on all precious metals sharply
lower in New York. Copper and crude oil also fell.
The nearby-month February contract lost $19.20 an ounce to $551 on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange. Most-active April gold slid $19.50 to $554.80. Most-active March silver lost 34.8 cents
to $9.41 an ounce.
Gold hit 25-year highs last week because of worries about inflation and Iran's nuclear intentions. "There's
been a lot of selling and profit-taking -- across all asset classes, actually," said Bernard Hunter, director of
precious metals at Scotia Mocatta. "Oil is weaker. Gold, silver, platinum and palladium are weaker, and base
metals are weaker. It just fed on itself."
Gold turned back after spot metal wasn't able to rise above $575 last week, Mr. Hunter said.
Tom O'Brien, editor of the Gold Report, cited speculative liquidation. There were reports of some
central-bank selling, but he expressed doubt that this spooked the market much. "Gold had gone too far for
too long without a correction," Mr. O'Brien said. From the low of $496.50 set Dec. 21 to Thursday's contract
high of $579.50, April gold hadn't experienced a significant downturn. "To me, it's going to be a natural
retracement in a bull-market move," said Mr. O'Brien. Speculative profit-taking was reported in silver and
copper. Most-active March copper fell from an overnight peak of $2.3390 a pound to settle with a 5.20-cent
loss to $2.2780.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE: Prices on the Nymex slid 3% amid some concern about easing demand. The Department of Energy
revised first-quarter U.S. oil demand, paring the growth rate to 0.19% from the 1.26% rate in a forecast last
month. Also weighing on prices were expectations that government data due today will show increases in
crude-oil and gasoline inventories. The March contract fell $2.02 to $63.09 a barrel.
COCOA: Prices on the New York Board of Trade settled higher as the market took out important technical
chart points amid renewed violence in top-producer Ivory Coast, where 11 people were killed and four people
were kidnapped Monday. March gained $41 to $1,517 a ton.

GM and Google Fall, Disney Shines --- Alcoa, Exxon, Caterpillar Are Among Session's Losers; Earnings Help Some Shares
Abreast of the Market
GM and Google Fall, Disney Shines --- Alcoa, Exxon, Caterpillar Are Among Session's Losers;
Earnings Help Some Shares
By Alex Davidson
933 words
8 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks fell as weakness from General Motors and Google trumped a strong showing by Walt Disney.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which includes GM and Disney, declined 48.51 points, or 0.45%, to
10749.76. The Nasdaq Composite Index, which includes Google, lost 13.84, or 0.61%, to 2244.96, and the
S&P 500-stock index fell 10.24, or 0.81%, to 1254.78.
Clark Yingst, chief market analyst at Joseph Gunnar, said the market's recent behavior is likely to remain as
investors juggle the possibility of higher interest rates, geopolitical concerns and remnants of a lukewarm
earnings season.
"I think this [volatility] lasts for a while," Mr. Yingst said. He said a lot of volatility stems from investors'
awaiting clarity on how new Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke will direct the body on interest rates.
"It is a transition; it is an unknown," he said.
Google declined $17.18, or 4. 5%, to $367.92 as concerns about the Internet company's potential growth and
increased competition from Microsoft and others weighed on sentiment, furthering the stock's recent declines.
GM slid 53 cents, or 2.3%, to 22. 81 as the auto maker's directors voted to halve the company's $2 annual
dividend and slash Chief Executive Rick Wagoner's compensation by 50%.
Disney climbed 1.74, or 7%, to 26.70 after the entertainment titan's fiscal first-quarter net income rose, as
strong performances from its U.S. theme parks and its ABC television network offset another tough quarter
for the company's movie studio.
Other movers among the 30 Dow components included Alcoa, down 1.16, or 3.6%, to 30.87; Exxon Mobil,
down 1.42, or 2.3%, to 60.55; and Caterpillar, off 1.54, or 2.2%, to 67.16.
Toll Brothers fell 1.73 cents, or 5.5%, to 29.47. The luxury home builder reported a steep decline in fiscal
first-quarter orders and said it continues to be constrained by long delivery times at many of its properties.
Other movers on the news included Meritage Homes, down 2.27, or 3.8%, to 58.13; KB Home, down 2.64, or
3.8%, to 67.50; and D.R. Horton, off 1.28, or 3.7%, to 33.67.
Boston Scientific gained 64 cents, or 3%, to 22.26. The medical-device maker's fourth-quarter earnings rose,
though the results took a back seat to analysts' questions about the effects of a recent warning the company
received from the Food and Drug Administration and a pending takeover of Guidant Corp.
American depositary shares of BP fell 2.47, or 3.5%, to 67.50. The oil titan's fourth-quarter earnings fell shy of
analysts' estimates, in part because of changes to international accounting rules, and its oil and gas
production fell 2% in the quarter.
Gold stocks slid as the price of the commodity fell. Meridian Gold lost 2.23, or 8.4%, to 24.43, while
Newmont Mining retreated 4.21, or 7%, to 56.27.
CNET Networks (Nasdaq) advanced 1.09, or 7.9%, to 14.94. Needham &Co. upgraded its rating of the media
company as CNET said it would sell its Computer Shopper magazine business for undisclosed terms.
Emerson Electric advanced 2.09, or 2.7%, to 78.96. The electronics-products manufacturer reported a strong
first quarter and raised its earnings guidance for fiscal 2006.
Urban Outfitters (Nasdaq) rose 1.01, or 3.9%, to 26.81. The retailer said fourth-quarter same-store sales rose
8%, on increases at its Urban Outfitters, Free People and Anthropologie brands.

--- Page 31 ---
Page 31 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Under Armour (Nasdaq) lost 8.26, or 21%, to 30.94. The athletic-wear company, one of the most successful
initial public offerings last year, forecast 2006 revenue short of Wall Street's view.
Polo Ralph Lauren declined 3.30, or 5.7%, to 54.17. The clothier lowered its full-year profit outlook and said
the next fiscal year's earnings will fall below Wall Street's views.
Activision (Nasdaq) lost 80 cents, or 5.6%, to 13.56. The videogame publisher's third-quarter results missed
Wall Street's expectations, and the company issued a lower-than-expected fourth-quarter outlook, now
expecting a loss in the period.
American depositary shares of Royal KPN climbed 64 cents, or 6.9%, to 9.95. The telecommunications
company predicted core earnings will stabilize in 2006 after a year of decline and said it will buy back another
1 billion euros ($1.2 billion) of its own shares.
Church &Dwight fell 2.88, or 8%, to 33.30. The maker of Arm &Hammer products posted a strong
fourth-quarter profit, helped by solid sales of Arm &Hammer and Xtra liquid laundry detergents.
Benchmark Electronics rose 1.36, or 3.9%, to 37.37. The contract-manufacturing provider expects
first-quarter and full-year 2006 earnings, excluding charges, ahead of Wall Street's average estimates.
Mentor Corp. fell 1.18, or 2.7%, to 42.72. The medical-products supplier dropped its bid to acquire Medicis
Pharmaceutical Group. Medicis lost 1.05, or 3.4%, to 30.05.
Hillenbrand Industries advanced 3.30, or 6.7%, to 52.90. The maker of health-care equipment and funeral
caskets saw an 12% increase in its fiscal first-quarter net income and said it was making progress on
streamlining business and cutting costs.

Silver Would Benefit From Fund
Commodities Report
Silver Would Benefit From Fund
By Allen Sykora
342 words
6 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A pending exchange-traded fund for silver, if approved, may boost demand for the metal, mirroring the
scenario in gold, which has a number of popular, relatively new ETFs, analysts say.
The possibility of increased investment demand has kept the silver market frothy lately as traders ponder an
ETF proposal from Barclays PLC's Barclays Global Investors.
Most-active March silver on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange set a record of $9.95
an ounce last week, the highest level for Comex silver since 1984. On Friday, March silver fell 11.7 cents to
$9.760, while spot February fell 11.4 cents to $9.730.
Silver has been steadily rising in recent months with gold, which hit 25-year records amid inflation worries,
geopolitical tensions and general investment demand for commodities.
Barclays' original silver ETF application was submitted this past summer, but interest among silver traders
peaked during the last two weeks after the Securities and Exchange Commission published a notice to solicit
comments on Barclays' iShares Silver Trust. An SEC spokesman said there isn't a timetable on when a
decision could be rendered.Barclays said it can't discuss the ETF because of a "quiet period" during the SEC
review.
While a gold or silver ETF allows investors to buy or sell shares similar to a stock, only the ETFs closely track
day-to-day movement of the commodity. The objective is for the share value to reflect the price of the silver
held by the trust, minus expenses and liabilities, according to the Barclays prospectus submitted to the SEC.
The ETF shares are backed by silver stored on behalf of the trust.
"This type of investment vehicle makes investing in silver more convenient," said Michael DiRienzo, executive
director of the Silver Institute, an international association of miners, refiners, fabricators and wholesalers of
silver and silver products.
Barclays said trading volume in its gold ETF has increased 33% over the prior month for the last three
months.
Document J000000020060206e22600025

Page 37 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Monthly Mutual Funds Review --- One Big Step Into the Unknown --- Five Top Fund Managers Search for the Market's Direction
Monthly Mutual Funds Review --- One Big Step Into the Unknown --- Five Top Fund Managers Search
for the Market's Direction
By Craig Karmin
2,434 words
6 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WHEN PICKING INVESTMENTS, it is still about the economy if you don't want to look stupid.
Forecasting U.S. economic expansion this year looks particularly tricky: Will Iran's nuclear standoff send oil
prices skyrocketing -- or will rising oil stockpiles keep prices steady? If consumers' appetite for toys --
flat-panel TVs, expensive handbags -- wanes, will spending by businesses be enough to keep the economy
humming?
For millions of small investors, answers to these questions can be crucial as they try to figure out whether to
put extra cash this year into their stock funds, or stuff it into a money-market account instead. Even the pros
are struggling to read the tea leaves.
We asked five top-rated mutual-fund managers to take the pulse of the economy. They represent different
money-management styles and are based in different regions of the country. Each has run a fund for at least
10 years, witnessing both stock-market booms and busts, and all have racked up returns superior to their
peers.
While they don't agree about how well the economy will perform this year, all see consumer spending
dropping. Most think businesses will pick up some of that slack, though they don't agree by how much. These
managers see the economy expanding at a faster clip in the first half of the year than the second, with
year-end expansion totaling as little as 2% to as much as 3.5%.
Their forecast is a bit more bearish than the consensus among economists, who see expansion of about
3.25%, according to The Wall Street Journal's annual economic-forecasting survey, conducted in December.
Commerce Department figures showed that the U.S. economy expanded 1.1% in the fourth quarter, hurt by
slower consumer spending, but some economists are predicting a sharp rebound.
These fund managers tend to see the stock market muddling through with another year of single-digit returns.
They see energy companies, commodity producers and technology firms faring the best. Consumer-goods
makers, retail stores and financial-service businesses, especially those tied to the mortgage market, look
more vulnerable.
But, as Ron Muhlenkamp, manager of the $3.2 billion-in-assets Muhlenkamp Fund, puts it, "in economics,
what everybody fears rarely happens." Instead, they get some wild card like Iran.
The other managers interviewed were Manu Daftary, manager of the $900 million Quaker Strategic Growth
Fund; William Frels, who runs the $2.6 billion Mairs &Power Growth Fund; William Nasgovitz, manager of
$1.6 billion Heartland Value Fund; and Robert Rodriguez, in charge of FPA Capital Fund, a $2.1 billion
portfolio.
Here are developments they are watching for signs of what is ahead:
OVER A BARREL: Aside from the obvious benefits to major oil companies, most of the fund managers agree
that high energy costs represent perhaps the biggest threat to the country's economic expansion.
But how high is too high? Despite oil prices that surged above $70 a barrel last summer, the economy
continued to chug along, defying the expectation of some economists who recalled the toxic mix of inflation
and slower expansion, known as stagflation, unleashed when prices spiked in the 1970s.
With strong demand for energy from China and fear of potential supply disruptions from volatile oil-producing
nations like Iran and Nigeria, many oil experts predict energy prices could set new records this year.

--- Page 38 ---
Page 38 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
If the price of oil, at $65.37 a barrel, is still above $60 by the summer, Mr. Frels says he would reassess the
prospects for the economy this year. "That would dampen consumer spending," he says. "The longer you
have high oil prices, the greater the effect on the inflation rate, and greater the likelihood that the Fed will
continue to raise interest rates" to ward off higher inflation.
Mr. Muhlenkamp fears the combination of higher oil prices and steeper interest rates but says chances of
recession this year are less than 20%, even if oil prices and rates continue going up. "That would slow things
down," he says. "But it would take an outside surprise to bring about recession."
While high energy prices are translating into higher heating costs, Mr. Daftary sees the price at the gasoline
pump as a critical variable. If families start paying $4 a gallon for gasoline, "that could have a debilitating
effect."
THE CONSUMER'S CHOICES: Economists say that consumer spending barely increased in the fourth
quarter, and the five fund managers are bracing for it to fall off sometime this year. That decline may be
increasingly evident as higher energy costs mean more expensive heating costs for much of the country.
Mr. Muhlenkamp says surveys indicate that eating out, going to the movies and spending on clothes are
some of the main areas susceptible to cutbacks. In particular, he is keeping an eye on retailers as they report
their fourth-quarter results. "We're not confident in being able to predict which areas will be most affected," he
says. "So we're watching the data."
Many of the managers look beyond financial statements for signs that consumers may be tightening their
belts. Mr. Frels keeps an eye on how full restaurants are when he is dining out. He isn't seeing a lot of empty
tables.
Mr. Rodriguez notes that the rate of increase in personal income fell below the inflation rate by the end of
last year, a trend that he expects to continue this year and to cause the consumer to be more finicky. He says
that residential investment as a percentage of gross domestic product is at its highest level ever.
"Consumers have a large percentage of their assets in a category that has inflated," he says. "Any kind of
correction would have big implications for spending." The managers note that the mortgage-refinancing boom
is coming to an end: Many homeowners have refinanced at low rates and increases in interest rates make
additional refinancings less appealing, so a big source of money to buoy the economy is drying up.
Mr. Daftary counts "For Sale" signs in his suburban Boston neighborhood to detect whether one of the
country's hotter housing markets is cooling down. He has seen more signs -- and more of them sticking
around for a longer period. "That tells me housing prices are too high," he says.
BEYOND THE BOTTOM LINE: Corporate earnings are going through a golden age. Operating earnings per
share for the companies in the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index have risen 90% during the past four years,
according to Citigroup Inc. (Operating earnings exclude some gains and losses that are part of net income as
calculated under generally accepted accounting principles.) The consensus forecast calls for earnings this
year to jump 13% from 2005, according to Thomson Financial. That would represent an unprecedented fourth
straight year of double-digit profit increases.
Rather than zeroing in on the bottom line, Mr. Muhlenkamp focuses on the top line. Revenue expansion,
unlike profits, can't be aided by cost cutting and is a more accurate gauge of the state of the economy, he
says. He predicts revenue increases for blue-chip companies this year to average about 3.5%, which
indicates GDP expansion of about the same.
Some of the managers are focused on the amount of money that companies spend on factories, information
systems and other investments. As consumers feel less confident, business spending will have to pick up the
slack to offset any drop in the consumer's appetite. Mr. Frels suggests looking at what big diversified
companies like General Electric Co. or 3M Co. say about their capital expenditures. He sees their moves as a
window into what many U.S. companies are doing.
Despite GE's disappointing earnings released last month, he remains relatively upbeat. "There was a lot of
noise in the fourth quarter," he says. "But basic business is doing well, especially those sectors tied to the
economy."
THE DOLLAR'S DIRECTION: Last year, the U.S. currency enjoyed its biggest gains since 2001, rising 15%
against the euro and the yen. While a stronger dollar helps keep a lid on inflation in the U.S. by encouraging
the importation of cheaper goods from abroad, it also makes U.S. products less competitive abroad, by
making them more expensive relative to European or Japanese goods.

--- Page 39 ---
Page 39 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A sudden and rapid decline in the dollar would be bad for the U.S. economy and capital markets, because the
shock of it likely would provoke foreign investors to pull back from dollar-denominated securities. But a repeat
of the gradual retreat the dollar experienced between 2002 and 2004 would benefit many U.S. exporters.
In that case, Mr. Daftary says, "you may see higher earnings estimates from agricultural companies like
Archer-Daniels-Midland (Co.) and commodity producers."
Mr. Nasgovitz notes that the price of gold climbed to a 25-year high recently. Although gold and the dollar
both rallied last year, a sharp spike in the price of the precious metal historically has coincided with a weaker
U.S. currency and inflation. "It's negative for stocks when the price of gold goes through the roof," he says.
ASIA'S APPETITE: Commodity prices took off again in 2005: The Dow Jones-AIG Index of 20 commodities
jumped 18%. Much of that rise was fueled by exploding demand in China and India for oil and natural
resources to supply their rapidly expanding economies.
Mr. Frels thinks this demand will continue to lift the prices of a variety of commodities and raw materials, a
trend that could be costly to U.S. companies. That could mean more inflation -- and it may put pressure on
the Fed to continue raising interest rates.
At the same time, he is keeping an eye on Asian appetite for U.S. debt. Japan is the largest foreign holder of
U.S. Treasury bonds, and while Tokyo's buying of this debt fell off dramatically last year -- in part because the
Bank of Japan wasn't intervening to weaken its currency by buying dollars and selling yen -- China helped
pick up the slack.
Chinese net purchases of U.S. Treasury debt stood at $35.3 billion through the first 11 months of 2005,
according to the latest U.S. government figures, making 2005 China's biggest year for these purchases.
Economists say Asian demand for U.S. Treasury debt is a big reason why long-term rates have remained
low, and some investors worry that any significant diversification into other countries' debt would result in
higher U.S. bond yields, which, in turn, could slow the economy.
YIELDING DIRECTION: In the Treasury-bond market, the difference in the yield for short-term debt and
long-term debt has become quite small, a phenomenon known as a flat yield curve. In December, the yield for
the two-year note briefly exceeded that of the 10-year note. That development is known as a yield-curve
inversion, and it often indicates that an economic downturn, or even recession, is looming. That is because
investors usually demand more payment from governments and companies to tie up their money over longer
periods. So if they are willing to accept a lower yield, it suggests they believe the Fed, which raised its target
short-term rate to 4.5% last week, will soon have to bring those rates back down to ward off a recession.
Some of the managers, like Mr. Frels, say a yield-curve inversion matters less when interest rates are at
relatively low levels. In this case, the curve flattens because long-term rates have come down faster than
short-term ones have risen. This is more benign than when both long- and short-term rates are rising,
pressuring the economy to slow.
Mr. Rodriguez thinks this flattening yield curve signals slower expansion ahead for the entire economy, and it
is particularly worrisome for the financial sector. There, it isn't merely a signal, but a hindrance to core lending
activities. When short-term and long-term rates are almost the same, these companies can't borrow at low
short-term rates and lend at higher long-term rates, pocketing the difference as profit. Instead, they may have
to make riskier loans to less credit-worthy borrowers to get a decent spread.
"We're likely to see a number of financial-services firms with difficult earnings," Mr. Rodriguez says.
WASHINGTON WORRIES: This is an election year, with all 435 House seats and one-third of the Senate
seats in play. Republicans are expected to retain their majorities in both chambers. But lobbying scandals
that caused House Majority Leader Tom DeLay to give up his leadership post have led some political pundits
to suggest that those majorities may be thinner after the November election.
That matters to some of the managers because they think pro-business legislation would be less likely to
pass if the Republican majority is whittled down. Mr. Nasgovitz says uncertainty in Washington is a head wind
for the stock market, and he thinks the Bush Administration's tax cuts may not be extended under the current
political climate. "That would be a drag on the economy," he says.
Mr. Daftary thinks the less Congress does, the better, and that too much legislation tends to make matters
worse. A divided Congress would suit him fine. Still, he thinks some sign from the White House that the U.S.
is moving closer to bringing troops back from Iraq would offer a psychological lift to the market and could rev
up consumer confidence.
OTHER TEA LEAVES: During the 1990s, Mr. Muhlenkamp recalls, comments like Fed Chairman Alan
Greenspan's famous warning that the stock market was responding to "irrational exuberance" gradually gave

--- Page 40 ---
Page 40 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
way to pundit claims that the market would keep going up because "this time is different." These assurances
began to peak about the same time that stock prices did in 2000.
For now, analysts and financial journalists are writing furiously about a housing-market bubble, and what
would happen if it bursts. Mr. Muhlenkamp is scanning headlines to find pundits who argue, instead, that
conditions have changed, and prices can keep going higher.
"When guys making the warnings get tired of being shouted down and start keeping mum," he says, "that's
when it's over."

Despite Oil Drop, Dow Falls 101.97 As Investors Fret
Thursday's Markets
Despite Oil Drop, Dow Falls 101.97 As Investors Fret
By E.S. Browning
498 words
3 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A MEASURE of volatility returned to the stock market, as investors struggled with conflicting information on
economic strength, the interest-rate outlook, corporate profits and even world security.
After jumping 89 points Wednesday, the Dow Jones Industrial Average sagged 101.97 points, or 0.93%, to
10851.98, still up 1.3% this year. Trading again was heavy.
Traders blamed a Labor Department report showing lower fourth-quarter worker productivity -- output per
worker -- and higher labor costs.
"Productivity is one of the things that [former Federal Reserve Chairman Alan] Greenspan has been saying
for years has been able to contain inflation," said Todd Clark, director of stock trading at San Francisco
money-management and brokerage firm Nollenberger Capital Partners. "The fact that the productivity figure
was down instead of up got people spooked."
Gold, which investors buy in times of worry, hit another 25-year high in New York, with gold futures up $3.10
at $572.50 an ounce.
St. Paul Travelers, a big U.S. commercial-insurance company, cable provider Comcast and manufacturer
Tyco all reported disappointing profit news. Then came a false rumor that the Homeland Security Department
was raising the terror alert, which contributed to the jitters.
But at the same time, there was plenty of positive news, which left some traders wondering why stocks had
fallen so heavily. Monthly sales reports from big retail chains were stronger than expected. Despite the
unfounded terror rumor and continuing anxiety about Iran's nuclear program, crude-oil futures fell $1.88 to
$64.68 a barrel in New York. Natural gas, heating fuel and gasoline futures also fell.
Investors got more unsettling news after regular trading hours, as Amazon.com announced disappointing
quarterly sales. The stock fell 8.2% in after-hours trading.
Investors will be anxiously awaiting January numbers on employment levels, due this morning, which often
are taken as an indicator of future inflation risks.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.91%, or 11.62 points, to 1270.84, up 1.8% in 2006. The Nasdaq
Composite Index declined 1.25%, or 28.99 points, to 2281.57, up 3.5% this year.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.57%, or 1.20 points, to 209.53.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.90 billion shares traded, 986 stocks rose and 2,340 fell.
Bond prices were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note was unchanged, continuing to yield 4.565%. The
30-year bond was up 7/32, or $2.19 for every $1,000 invested, pushing the yield down to 4.698%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 118.50 yen, up from 118.02, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.2098 from $1.2061.

The Greenspan Story: A Hard Landing?
The Greenspan Story: A Hard Landing?
By Michael T. Darda
1,120 words
2 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Alan Greenspan's last FOMC meeting Tuesday went off without a hitch -- but the first year of the
post-Greenspan era may prove much more challenging. Ben Bernanke takes the helm with the 10-year
Treasury note near 4.5% and core inflation near 2%, lower on both counts than anyone could have imagined
18 years ago.
But the road to price stability hasn't been without serious setbacks and potentially menacing risks ahead. Mr.
Greenspan assumed the Fed chairmanship as the Louvre Accord was unraveling. A weakening commitment
to dollar stability coincided with a 40% rise in the statutory capital gains tax rate, a casualty of the 1986 tax
reform compromise. Rising inflation expectations and a higher tax wedge on capital helped to set the stage
for the October 1987 crash, a monetary baptism by fire for a new Fed chairman if there ever was one. In
some ways, it seems like history is repeating itself as a newly minted chairman takes the helm while precious
metals prices push to levels not seen in two decades.
Some chalk up $570 an oz. gold prices to geopolitical uncertainty and investor fear. However, these fears are
not showing up in emerging market credit spreads, which hover near record lows. Nor can they be seen in the
ratio of the S&P 600 small-capitalization equity index to the S&P 500, which reached all-time highs recently.
These indicators are highly sensitive to the supply/demand balance for monetary liquidity.
Many see the flat yield curve and the still-low level of long-term bond yields as an "all clear" signal on the
inflationary front. However, bond yields usually move with (and sometimes after) inflation. Conversely,
sustained movements in precious metals prices, especially gold, tend to lead movements in other
commodities, monetary velocity and inflation. The yield curve is an important indicator, but it is not infallible.
Many believe globalization will create a firebreak against inflation, but trade barriers have been in a steady
descent for more than 70 years with no consistent bearing on U.S. inflation rates. Others argue that inflation
can't happen here because labor has no pricing power with India and China -- both named explicitly as
"competitor" nations by the president in his State of the Union address. But the late 1990s witnessed the
tightest labor markets since the 1960s, which raised real wages for low-end workers, despite competition
from India and China.
To be sure, inflation and deflation are monetary phenomena, not labor market issues. This is why profit
margins collapsed during the 1997-2001 period. The labor market tightened, but firms had no ability to pass
on costs with the tight liquidity conditions that prevailed: Weak gold, low commodities, falling monetary
velocity, a high dollar and high real short-term interest rates all signaled a stringent monetary environment.
Today, the labor market is tightening, but these same sensitive indicators signal an ongoing mismatch
between the supply of Fed liquidity and the demand for it in the real economy. It is true that the dollar rallied
in 2005, but this was due to the yen and the euro falling against commodities faster than the dollar did. In
short, the yen and euro weakened; the dollar didn't strengthen.
Containing core inflation in a tight 1-to-2 percentage point band will not be possible without a set of sensitive
intermediate indicators that allow the Fed to react before excess liquidity ignites the inflation process or
deficient liquidity dampens it excessively. Stated differently, the Fed needs to know if the volume of money
entering the system is excessive or deficient before a movement in actual prices is afoot.
Keynes got it right in his 1930 "Treatise on Money" when he said, "movements in the Bank rate have so often
represented a belated and inadequate effort to follow the natural rate . . . the true natural rate of interest is
that at which the value of money is unchanged."
The Asian and Latin monetary crises, frequently blamed on the vicissitudes of the free market, actually
occurred in the wake of incredible instability in the value of money. Tumbling gold prices, weak spot
commodity indices, a sustained ascent in the dollar's foreign exchange value, falling monetary velocity and

--- Page 49 ---
Page 49 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
high real short-term interest rates set the stage for the global monetary turbulence that characterized the late
1990s.
By 2001, virtually every country that had kept its monetary policy harnessed to the U.S. dollar had
experienced outright price-level deflation. The Fed took drastic action after the equity bubble began to
collapse, eventually cutting rates to an incredible 1% in 2003 and holding them there for a "considerable
period" to lean against the risk of deflation. Ironically, sensitive indicators in the gold, commodity and the
forex markets were telling the Fed that deflation risk had abated by 2003. The indicators the Fed was
following missed the shift in price-level risks. We know this because the eight-quarter average of the quarterly
annualized change in the core PCE deflator has risen to 2.1%. This is the top of the Fed's tolerance zone and
also the highest eight-quarter average since June 1996.
No one can claim that the current level of core inflation is high, but the trends are clear -- and troubling.
Moreover, headline inflation rates tend to lead core rates by six-to-12 months and sometimes more, meaning
all the sanguinity about the lack of pass-through from high oil to core prices probably is misplaced.
So Ben Bernanke begins where Alan Greenspan leaves off, against the backdrop of an eerily similar set of
monetary and fiscal circumstances that Mr. Greenspan stepped into 18 years ago. Mr. Bernanke will have no
control over Congress and whether it allows crucial tax cuts on capital to expire (a tax hike), but he does have
control over the Fed and the indicators it chooses to heed. If he elevates sensitive, real-time indicators and
downplays lagging information, he will inspire confidence and limit the potential fallout from the Fed holding
short rates too low for too long. If not, and the Fed pauses too soon, the dollar's value would be likely to slip
further against sensitive price-level indicators which would elevate monetary velocity and boost inflation risk.
This likely would require much more tightening down the road with a greater potential to seriously disrupt the
economy. A premature pause wouldn't prevent a hard landing -- it would virtually ensure it.
---
Mr. Darda is chief economist and director of research for MKM Partners. This is the fourth in a short series
this week on Mr. Greenspan.

A STRONG profit report from Boeing and an afternoon dip in oil prices helped stocks shake off Tuesday's

The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which includes the airplane maker among its 30 component stocks, rose
89.09 points, or 0.82%, to 10953.95. It now is up 2.2% for 2006, less than 50 points short of the 11000

Other indexes, less influenced by Boeing, put in smaller gains.
Boeing jumped almost 5% after the company announced a doubling of fourth-quarter profits and boosted its

program.

"Energy prices went down and the market kind of found itself," said Robert Harrington, head of U.S. stock
trading at UBS's brokerage arm.
Some investors also felt Tuesday's decline had been an overreaction to the Federal Reserve's statement
after its policy meeting, which said "some" further interest-rate increases "may be needed" to stave off
inflation. While higher rates are bad for stocks, that is a much milder statement than previous Fed comments
on interest rates, which indicated that more rate increases were likely. It suggests the Fed still is moving
toward an end to rate increases in the coming months.
Gold pulled back $1.40 from Tuesday's 25-year high, finishing at $569.40 an ounce.
Google, which had fallen 12% in after-hours trading Tuesday after its quarterly profit missed Wall Street
expectations, recovered some of that ground. But it still finished 7% below Tuesday's regular-hours close.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.19%, or 2.38 points, to 1282.46, up 2.7% so far this year.
The Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 0.21%, or 4.74 points, to 2310.56, up 4.8% on the year.
