The Greenspan Story: Oracle of the Fed
The Greenspan Story: Oracle of the Fed
By Steve Forbes
1,371 words
1 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Never in history has a central banker received such a cacophony of hosannas as has Alan Greenspan, who
officially stepped down yesterday.
Here and abroad, government officials, politicians, economists, executives, entrepreneurs and journalists
have all heaped praise for his "unparalleled skills" in guiding American monetary policy. No man other than
William McChesney Martin -- and who remembers him? -- has served longer as Federal Reserve Board
chairman, and Mr. Greenspan's tenure was hardly a quiet one. It was loaded with crises, from the 1987 stock
market crash (equity markets suffered their most catastrophic one-day decline ever, the Dow Jones
Industrials down 23%) to the spectacular popping of the high-tech bubble a few years ago. Through it all, Mr.
Greenspan exuded an owlish calm that seemed borne of wisdom and the innate ability to constructively,
decisively, matter-of-factly deal with the matter at hand, no matter the magnitude or the urgency.
Though he served as President Ford's chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers, and though he was
originally appointed to head the Fed by Ronald Reagan, Mr. Greenspan won the trust and admiration of both
sides of the aisle on Capitol Hill as well as the business and financial communities. His Delphic, ambiguous,
often impenetrable prose many times had listeners scratching their heads in bewilderment. But no one
believed this meant Mr. Greenspan didn't know what he was talking about. It meant his audience lacked the
smarts to figure it out, or that Mr. Greenspan, a la President Eisenhower's meandering syntax, was
deliberately muddying the waters. In fact, millions of investors took repeated and perverse delight in watching
Mr. Greenspan deftly run verbal circles around his usually befuddled congressional interlocutors.
Mr. Greenspan's record? Officially measured rates of inflation are lower today than when the Maestro took
office. So is unemployment. The banking system seems sound. So, too, do the financial markets. Problems
abound, of course, but these can mostly be laid at the steps of Congress: most notoriously, Washington
politicians' unrestrained spending. Mr. Greenspan was sound on trade, particularly helpful in recent years
when destructive protectionist pressures were surfacing, and usually constructive on tax cuts.
He was also brilliant in managing the media and in guiding the financial markets. Any changes in policy and
interest rates were well signaled in advance. No nasty surprises. With ample warning of what was coming,
markets could calmly adjust to changing circumstances. Mr. Greenspan's 15 quarter-point increases in the
Federal funds rate is the supreme example.
---
So what's not to like? Just this -- and, alas, it's a big "this." Mr. Greenspan has left no institutional legacy that
can guide his successors, that can be a lasting model for other central bankers here and around the world. In
short, Mr. Greenspan operated by his gut, on hunches. He was similar to a pilot who flies without instruments.
His instincts are usually right on, but not always.
True, he never moved precipitously. He painstakingly examined incalculable amounts of data before making
a decision. He dominated the Fed by ever-patiently taking the time to bring his colleagues and his
bureaucracy around to his own way of thinking on a particular policy. But this raises the question: What
guided the Fed's -- i.e., Mr. Greenspan's -- actions? How did the Fed know on a day-to-day, or even
month-to-month, basis whether it was doing its job right or wrong? What were its equivalents of earnings, of
market share, of sales figures, of balance sheets that guide CEOs? What was the Fed equivalent of the
speedometer that lets a driver know whether he is going too slow or too fast?
Mr. Greenspan never clearly and repeatedly articulated -- and, therefore, never educated Capitol Hill and the
country -- as to what was the paramount job of the Fed (to keep the dollar sound and stable) and how it
should go about that job.

--- Page 53 ---
Page 53 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Not spelling out the Fed's core mission is consequential. There are many in Congress and elsewhere who
think the Fed -- and other central banks -- can guide economies. European politicos are pressuring the
European Central Bank, for instance, not to increase interest rates even though inflation may be brewing,
lest such an act abort a still-anemic recovery.
Many policymakers and observers believe the Fed can fine-tune the economy: print more money, for
instance, to "stimulate" it when activity is lackluster, or raise interest rates if the economy appears
"overheated." Mr. Greenspan himself in the early 1990s seemed enamored of the so-called Phillips Curve,
which postulates that there is trade-off between inflation and unemployment. Sometimes he seemed to key
monetary policy to the price of gold; other times to labor market conditions; then other times to some sort of
inflation index, and then -- who knows what else?
This lack of a time-tested, durable compass is why Mr. Greenspan committed his biggest blunder. In 1997,
the Fed inadvertently began tightening up monetary policy, ceasing to supply enough credit to the markets.
The result was a deflation that hurt us and hit other countries harder than ours.
This deflation helped create the Asian crisis. It collapsed commodity prices, damaging developing countries
like Brazil. I remember as a presidential candidate seeing firsthand the distress of Iowa farmers over the
precipitous slide in their crop prices in 1999. That collapse had political repercussions. It gutted U.S. efforts to
wean farmers off subsidies and led to that horrific agriculture bill President Bush signed early in his
presidency. The plunge in steel prices led to the administration's flirtation -- thankfully brief -- with
protectionism around the same time.
The deflation also had another absolutely unappreciated impact -- by devastating traditional businesses such
as steel and agriculture, it artificially diverted capital into high technology, thus enlarging the bubble. The
deflation also damaged debtors, particularly in telecommunications, which played a role in bringing on the
contractions and bankruptcies that ended up costing the economy and investors hundreds of billions of
dollars.
And now Mr. Greenspan leaves his successor with another blunder -- the Fed is re-stoking the fires of
inflation. In 2002, it wisely began to ease its over-tight monetary policy. The stock market rebounded.
Combined with the tax cuts of May 2003, this enabled the economy to come vigorously back to life. But the
Fed overshot the mark. It printed -- and continues to print -- too much money. We see one manifestation of
this new inflation daily at the gas pump.
Think of monetary policy this way. If you don't have enough fuel for your car, the engine will stall. If you have
too much fuel, you will flood the engine. But if the fuel injector is working right, you have a chance to ride
smoothly.
Most economists hate to hear this, but for a variety of reasons, gold is to monetary policy what the North
Star is to determining location. Gold's intrinsic value hardly changes. Its price fluctuation in dollars reflects not
a change in the value of gold but a change in the real worth of the greenback. This is absolutely basic.
Gold is the best barometer we have for monetary disturbances. It zoomed in the 1970s, signaling the horrible
inflation we experienced in that era. The yellow metal's dollar price plunged in the late 1990s. And now it has
soared since 2004. The Fed needn't fix a price for gold -- a range of say, $350 to $380 (which is above the
average price over the past 10 to 12 years) will suffice.
Like almost all economists, though, Ben Bernanke, Mr. Greenspan's successor, disdains gold as both an
indicator and guide to monetary policy. Alan Greenspan's failure to leave a gold-based legacy means poor
Ben Bernanke won't be prepared for what will be hitting him -- the most serious resurgence of inflation in 25
years.
---
Mr. Forbes is president and CEO of Forbes, Inc. and editor-in-chief of Forbes magazine. This is the third in a
short series this week on Mr. Greenspan.

Abreast of the Market
Altria, Major Indexes Retreat; Alcoa, Newmont Mining Rise
By Alex Davidson Dow Jones Newswires
554 words
1 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks fell as markets digested the Federal Reserve's quarter-percentage-point increase in short-term
interest rates, a host of economic data and earnings from several Dow components.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average retreated 35.06 points, or 0.32%, to 10864.86. The Nasdaq Composite
Index lost 0.96, or 0.04%, to 2305.82, and the S&P 500 fell 5.12, or 0.4%, to 1280.08.
Donald Coxe, chairman of Harris Investment Management, said high prices for commodities such as oil and
gold, which hit $570.80 an ounce yesterday, indicate the Fed is unlikely to stop raising rates.
"You don't get gold setting all-time highs the day the Fed stops tightening," said Coxe, arguing that gold
prices ease when inflation fears are tamed. "It's a little difficult to make a conclusive point that the Fed's work
is done."
Dow industrial Altria Group slid $1.57, or 2.1%, to $72.34 as the company reiterated its breakup plan and said
any potential share buyback probably won't occur before then.
Kraft Foods, in which Altria owns an 87% stake, slid 56 cents, or 1.9%, to 29.44. The maker of Oreo cookies
and Oscar Mayer meats plans to cut 8,000 jobs, or about 8% of its staff, and close 20 production plants
world-wide.
Several gold stocks rose along with the commodity's price increase. Newmont Mining increased 89 cents, or
1.5%, to 61.80; Barrick Gold added 1.15, or 3.8%, to 31.46; and Glamis Gold climbed 92 cents, or 3%, to
31.97.
Dow industrial Alcoa rose 97 cents, or 3.2%, to 31.50 after investment firm UBS upgraded its rating on the
largest aluminum company in the world by production to "buy" from "neutral."
Dow component Merck rose four cents, or 0.1%, to 34.50. The pharmaceutical company posted a slight
increase in fourth-quarter earnings, helped by a gain in sales of its asthma medication and income from its
cholesterol-drug partnership with Schering-Plough.
Nasdaq Stock Market (Nasdaq) lost 4.79, or 10%, to 41.91, following a weaker-than-expected outlook for
2006 and a run-up Monday.
U.S. Steel climbed 1.11, or 1.9%, to 59.75. The steel company said it will buy back as many as eight million
shares after reporting a 76% drop in fourth-quarter earnings.
Chicago Mercantile Exchange advanced 19.25, or 4.8%, to 423.25, hitting an intraday high as the parent
company of the nation's largest futures exchange posted strong fourth-quarter results thanks to expanded
trading volume.
Goodyear Tire &Rubber retreated 3.12, or 17%, to 15.64. The tire maker provided a fourth-quarter forecast
below Street expectations, due partly to higher-than-anticipated raw-material prices.
Archer-Daniels-Midland rose 2.76, or 9.6%, to 31.50. The grains processor's fiscal second-quarter results
beat expectations, and the company said the outlook for its corn business is encouraging.
Wyeth lost 68 cents, or 1.5%, to 46.25. The drug company's fourth-quarter profit missed analysts' estimates,
as weak sales and higher research-and-development costs weighed on results.

Stocks Climb In Last Month For Greenspan
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Climb In Last Month For Greenspan
By E.S. Browning
900 words
1 February 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS FELL on January's last day amid disappointment about Alan Greenspan's final meeting as Federal
Reserve chairman, but still finished the bellwether month with clear gains.
Despite yesterday's decline of 35.06 points, or 0.32%, to 10864.86, the Dow Jones Industrial Average
finished the year's first month with a gain of 1.4% -- the biggest January percentage increase since 1999.
Although there are plenty of exceptions, the year's first month historically has often been a sign of things to
come, with stocks more likely to rise in the ensuing 11 months if they go up in January.
Yesterday's declines were mild and limited. Small stocks posted gains, and on the New York Stock
Exchange, more stocks rose than fell. The Russell 2000 small-stock index closed at a record, up 2.33 points,
or 0.32%, at 733.20.
The problem was that, as Mr. Greenspan prepared to step aside today and be succeeded by Ben Bernanke
at the Fed's helm, the news out of the Fed's regular January policy meeting wasn't entirely what investors
had hoped.
Fed officials boosted their target short-term interest rate by another quarter point, to 4.5%, the 14th
consecutive meeting at which they have increased rates since they started the process in June 2004. Higher
rates make life more expensive for businesses and consumers, slowing the economy, and are bad for stocks.
The rate increase wasn't the real problem, because it was anticipated.
What disappointed investors was the Fed's postmeeting statement, in which it warned that further rate
increases "may be needed" to prevent inflation. Some investors had hoped that, with a new Fed chief, policy
makers would avoid foreshadowing future rate increases, leaving Mr. Bernanke with a clean slate.
"There was an expectation that because it was Mr. Greenspan's last day in his position, that the decks would
be cleared for Ben Bernanke. If there was any disappointment, it was that the decks certainly weren't
cleared," said Matthew Johnson, head of stock syndication at New York brokerage firm Lehman Brothers
Holdings Inc. "There is plenty of room for the Fed to continue doing what they were doing."
The Fed's indication that it may have to keep raising rates came on top of general unease in the stock market
about the transition from the known-and-trusted Mr. Greenspan to the less-known Mr. Bernanke. Investors
were nervous when Mr. Greenspan succeeded Paul Volcker in 1987, and it could take some time for them to
adjust to a new Fed chairman.
"The changing of the guard at the Fed and the uncertainty about the interest-rate outlook -- which we get right
out of the gate with the new Fed chairman -- are going to create a lot of noise and potential volatility," said
Leo Grohowski, chief-investment officer at U.S. Trust.
On top of that, investors got an additional jolt after regular trading hours, as Google Inc.'s quarterly profit fell
well short of investor expectations. The stock plunged 12% in after-hours trading.
Gold futures, which investors turn to in times of uncertainty, hit a 25-year high in New York, rising $5 to
$570.80 an ounce, up 10% in January. Crude-oil futures fell 43 cents, but remained high at $67.92 a barrel.
Despite the continuing interest-rate uncertainty, investors were optimistic about fourth-quarter results, about
the 2006 profit outlook, and about the likelihood that the Fed will stop raising rates.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index finished the month with a 2.5% gain, despite a decline yesterday of
0.40%, or 5.12 points, to 1280.08. The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 4.6% in January, after edging down
0.04%, or 0.96 point, to 2305.82.

--- Page 56 ---
Page 56 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Historically, a strong January often has been good news for the rest of the year. In years when the Dow
industrials have risen in January, the next 11 months have shown an above-average gain 61% of the time,
according to a study done for The Wall Street Journal by Ned Davis Research. After a January decline, the
rest of the year is above average only 34% of the time. Stocks fell in January 2005, and the rest of the year
was disappointing.
Following yesterday's increase in the Fed's short-term rate target, banks including Bank of America Corp.,
J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. and Wachovia Corp. raised the prime lending rate, the benchmark for many
consumer and business loans, to 7.5% from 7.25%. That is among the ways that the Fed's rate increases
slow the economy and hold down inflation. They raise the cost of borrowing, restricting consumer spending
and business activity.
In major U.S. market action:
Major stock indexes retreated. But on the Big Board, where 1.97 billion shares traded, 1,797 stocks rose and
1,528 fell.
Bond prices rose. The 10-year Treasury note rose 3/32, or 94 cents for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 4.523%. The 30-year bond was up 10/32 to yield 4.684%.
The dollar weakened. It traded at 117.30 yen, down from 117.62; the euro rose on the dollar to $1.2152 from
$1.2085.

The Chairman's Mystique
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Chairman's Mystique
1,045 words
31 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The paradox of Alan Greenspan's achievement as he leaves office today after 18 years as Chairman of the
Federal Reserve is that nearly everyone is praising his performance but no one seems to know exactly how
he did it. He bequeaths to successor Ben Bernanke a record but not a method -- which means we all have to
see if the new guy has the same juggling skills.
As no less an authority than Milton Friedman notes nearby, this monetary eclecticism hasn't detracted from
Mr. Greenspan's historical accomplishment. With a blip or two along the way, he has continued the progress
toward price stability begun at the Fed under Paul Volcker. He also steered the economy through or around
more than one major crisis, including the stock market crash of 1987 and the savings and loan debacle of a
couple of years later.
We'd give Mr. Greenspan less credit than the conventional wisdom does for his handling of the
foreign-exchange crises of the 1990s. His willingness to join Robert Rubin in bailing out Mexico's foreign-debt
holders in 1994 accentuated the moral hazard that led to the Asian and Russian crises later that decade. The
Bush Administration's change of policy in refusing to bail out such global deadbeats as Argentina has done
more to limit currency crises than those episodes did.
On the other hand, Mr. Greenspan deserves more praise than he typically receives for his stewardship over
financial markets. He has tried to demystify the widespread use of credit derivatives, which have helped
financial institutions diversify their risk. One happy result is fewer bank failures than in previous recessions or
periods of rising interest rates. This Greenspan contribution will be especially missed, since no one else has
the same stature to counter the misguided fears tossed around by Warren Buffett and other media favorites.
Another misguided critique of the Greenspan era is the recent preoccupation with "global imbalances." This is
an updated version of the 1980s' alarums over the U.S. "twin deficits," in the trade account and federal
budget. The fear now as then is that the trade deficit in particular is "unsustainable," and that it will lead to a
crash in the dollar and thus in the global economy. The conventional wisdom's proffered cure, now as then, is
a major U.S. tax increase.
But these imbalances are not Mr. Greenspan's creation; they result from policy and investment choices made
around the world. The U.S. continues to be a mecca for global investment, thanks to fluid capital markets,
inviting (for the most part) tax policy and confidence in the rule of law. Old socialist Europe offers far fewer
opportunities to invest, and, while China has that huge market, its financial system is prehistoric and its legal
system precarious.
As long as this global policy "imbalance" prevails, so too will the unbalanced flow of capital and goods. In any
case, this division of economic labor has done very well by world growth in recent years, thanks especially to
the complementary roles played by China and the U.S. Mr. Greenspan has said he believes markets will
unwind these imbalances on their own, and gradually, absent a gross policy blunder. In our view, one such
blunder would be for the U.S. to fail to make the Bush tax cuts permanent, which would mean a huge tax
increase starting in 2008 and would reduce the U.S. as a destination for global investors.
---
A Fed Chairman deserves to be measured principally by how well he preserves the value of the national
currency. And on this score, Mr. Greenspan has done better than nearly all of his predecessors. The fact that
he did so by running monetary policy out of his hip pocket is all the more remarkable -- and all the more
relevant to Mr. Bernanke, who takes over this week.
For if there is a blot on the Greenspan record, we'd argue that it may yet be his performance during the last
couple of years. Take a look at the right edge of the first chart in Mr. Friedman's article nearby, and you can
see that the U.S. price level has begun to creep upward again. Though still a long way from the rising price

--- Page 61 ---
Page 61 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
levels of the 1980s, much less the 1970s, enough signs of inflationary expectations have been visible to
cause some of us to worry that the Fed has stayed too accommodative for too long.
Mr. Greenspan doesn't agree, and he has been able to point to the bond market for support. He argues that
our new world of global competition helps to keep prices down. On the other hand, such price signals as gold
and commodity prices have hit 20-year records and the dollar has been weak. Moreover, the government's
price indices are lagging indicators: Inflation doesn't show up in the consumer price index until as much as a
year after it has been baked into the monetary cake.
We won't know the truth about this part of the Greenspan legacy for some time, but it is clearly relevant to the
decisions that the Bernanke Fed will have to make in the coming months before it knows the answer. Mr.
Bernanke has in recent months called himself a "hopeless eclectic" on monetary policy, in a deliberate
invocation of the Greenspan method, or should we say, his mystique.
Previously, however, Mr. Bernanke has been known for endorsing "inflation targeting," which means keeping
inflation within a certain band (say, between 1% and 2% a year). Our concern with the latter policy is that in
the hands of a new and untested Fed Chairman it can sound like complacency -- as if a little inflation is no
big deal. It is also a backward-looking guide, as opposed to the forward-looking signals that commodities and
a price rule guideline can provide.
All of which is to underscore that running the Fed is such a big deal because so much is riding on the
performance of one man. The world has been fortunate in having two Chairmen over the past quarter century
who achieved maestro status; may the new Chairman conduct as well.

Nikkei's Win Streak Hits Five Stronger Dollar, Mittal Takeover Bid Propel Steel, Export Sectors

Nikkei's Win Streak Hits Five Stronger Dollar, Mittal Takeover Bid Propel Steel, Export Sectors
By Jamie Miyazaki Dow Jones Newswires

English

Tokyo -- JAPANESE STOCKS rallied for a fifth straight session to close at their latest five-year highs after
gains Friday on Wall Street, a stronger dollar, and Mittal Steel's takeover bid for Arcelor helped to push steel
and exporter stocks higher.
The Nikkei Stock Average gained 0.6%, or 90.55 points, to 16551.23, its highest close since September
2000, after a 569.66-point rise Friday.
"The Japanese market recovery is being underpinned by unusual strength in U.S. equities, and the dollar-yen
rate reacting to this . . . which all encourages market sentiment," said Akio Yoshino, market economist at
Societe Generale Asset Management.
The good news from American stock and currency markets gave exporters, like semiconductor maker Elpida
Memory, a shot in the arm. Elpida Memory, Japan's largest maker of memory chips by volume, closed up
3.9%.
Auto stocks, another export-dependent sector, also finished the day up, with Nissan Motor gaining 1.7%.
News last week that global steel giant Mittal was on the prowl for Arcelor caused Japanese steel stocks to
jump on hopes of a further round of consolidation in the sector. JFE Holdings ended the session up 6.7%,
while Kobe Steel gained 1.3%.
Sumitomo Mitsui Financial Group rose 1.5% in heavy trading after data released Friday showed a rise in
Japanese inflation for the second consecutive month in December, raising hopes of an end to deflation.
Real-estate firm Mitsubishi Estate rose 6.5%, its highest close this year.
Among the day's losers, Seiko Epson was the most conspicuous. The printer manufacturer slumped 9.7%
after issuing a profit warning precipitated by restructuring costs.
Glassmakers were among the handful of subsectors that finished the day lower. Nippon Sheet Glass
weakened 4.8% after cutting its earnings outlook for the financial year. Asahi Glass also finished lower, down
1.2%.
Amid an investigation by Tokyo prosecutors, Internet firm Livedoor reverted to its losing streak, sinking 16.5%
to 116 yen, after staging a rally Friday.
---
Other Market Activity
Markets in Hong Kong, Kuala Lumpur, Seoul, Shanghai, Singapore and Taipei were closed for the Lunar New
Year holidays. They remain closed today. China is closed all week.
Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 0.02%, or 0.04 point, to 245.32. Excluding the U.S, the index
decreased 0.09%, or 0.18 point, to 209.4.
In LONDON, the benchmark FTSE 100 ended 0.1% lower at 5779.80, as European markets traded narrowly
mixed. The British market was helped by oil giants BP and Royal Dutch Shell, which gained 2.4% and 1.7%,
respectively. Music and DVD retailer HMV rose nearly 17% after it said it received an offer approach from a
third party. The approach may or may not lead to an offer being made for the company, HMV said.
GlaxoSmithKline slipped 1.4% amid news reports the drug maker is in talks to buy biotech Serono for less
than the $15 billion originally sought by the Swiss firm. Serono's shares added 0.6%.

What's the New Guy Got? --- Stocks Are Flat as Edgy Investors Wait for Bernanke's First Pitch; Talk of a Parting Greenspan Gift
Monday's Markets
What's the New Guy Got? --- Stocks Are Flat as Edgy Investors Wait for Bernanke's First Pitch; Talk of
a Parting Greenspan Gift
By E.S. Browning
910 words
31 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS CHURNED UP and down but finished little changed as investors awaited one of the highest profile
Federal Reserve policy meetings in years.
Wall Street is looking on nervously as long-serving Chairman Alan Greenspan presides over his last
interest-rate meeting today, before turning over the Fed's reins to former Princeton professor Ben Bernanke
tomorrow. Talk, meanwhile, is circulating that the Fed could signal even more strongly today that it will stop
raising interest rates soon, which Wall Street would welcome with a sigh of relief. With the outlook so
uncertain, investors were reluctant to make big moves.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which had surged within sight of the 11000 mark last week, edged down
7.29 points, or 0.07%, to 10899.92, still up 1.7% this year. The broader Standard &Poor's 500-stock index
rose 0.12%, or 1.48 points, to 1285.20, up 3% in 2006.
Reflecting the nail-biting, there were rumors Mr. Greenspan might leave the market a little "going-away
present," by leaving interest rates unchanged for the first time since the Fed started raising them 19 months
ago, said Mike Viracola, head of trading at Boston brokerage firm Adams Harkness.
But "most people think there will be a little more tightening," or rate increasing, at today's meeting, Mr.
Viracola said. "The uncertainty is still there, so people are still waiting a little bit" before putting money to
work, he said.
Much as investors admire Mr. Bernanke's credentials as a Princeton economics professor and
Bush-administration adviser, he is untested as Fed leader. Investors greeted Mr. Greenspan's arrival 18
years ago with similar nervousness, and they recall today that the market crashed in 1987 within months of
Mr. Greenspan's swearing in. It probably will take some months before people adjust to a new hand on the
Fed's helm.
The Fed has raised its key short-term interest rate -- the target rate for overnight lending among banks -- at
13 consecutive policy meetings since June 2004, moving it to 4.25% from a 46-year low of 1%. Despite the
talk of a Greenspan parting gift, most investors expect the Fed to push its guideline rate up another quarter
point today, with banks adjusting consumer and business lending rates higher in response.
One question is whether Mr. Bernanke will feel the need to demonstrate that he is an inflation fighter by
pushing the target rate up by another quarter point when the Fed next meets, on March 28. Investors will
watch the Fed's postmeeting commentary today, released at about 2:15 p.m. EST, for hints about that. If
references to raising rates at a "measured pace" are removed from the statement, that will be seen as a sign
that Fed officials are considering a halt in rate increases. But since Mr. Bernanke won't yet be in charge,
investors still will feel uncertain about how he'll proceed.
Probably the biggest factor governing interest rates' future is the inflation rate. Recent readings have shown
measures of underlying inflation running at just below 2% a year, which is at the top end of what many Fed
officials are said to consider acceptable. The question is whether they will decide that it is safe now to stop
raising rates, or will instead try to push inflation lower with more rate increases.
With an unusually long period of almost two months before the next Fed meeting, investors will have time to
try to gauge Mr. Bernanke's intentions. Mr. Bernanke is scheduled to testify before Congress on Feb. 15, one
of the Fed chief's mandated twice-yearly appearances before lawmakers.
Although the market didn't reflect it, yesterday's economic and corporate news was relatively upbeat.
Government data showed consumer spending up more than expected in December, while inflation

--- Page 66 ---
Page 66 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
measures based on the consumer data, thought to be closely watched by the Fed, were almost flat for the
month.
Exxon Mobil Corp. offered a strong quarterly profit report. Saudi Arabia's oil minister said oil-exporting
countries have agreed that they won't cut oil output.
However, the news on the commodity front wasn't good. Crude-oil futures remained high, moving up 59 cents
to $68.35 a barrel in New York, which equaled a recent five-month high, ahead of today's Vienna meeting of
the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries.
Gold futures, to which investors turn in times of worry, jumped $7 to $565.80 an ounce in New York, yet
another 25-year high.
Reflecting continued bond-market concern about the direction of interest rates, the yield of the 10-year
Treasury note edged up to 4.533%, its highest New York finish in more than a month.
The Nasdaq Composite Index inched up 0.11%, or 2.55 points, to 2306.78, up 4.6% this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed; bonds fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 5/32, or $1.56 for each $1,000 invested. The
30-year bond was down 5/32 to yield 4.704%.
The dollar strengthened. It traded at 117.62 yen, up from 117.27 yen; the euro fell on the dollar to $1.2085
from $1.2102.

Prospecting for Mining's Winners --- If Gold Prices Maintain Heights, Even Less-Efficient Small Firms May Offer Benefits to Investors
Heard on the Street
Prospecting for Mining's Winners --- If Gold Prices Maintain Heights, Even Less-Efficient Small Firms
May Offer Benefits to Investors
By Gregory Zuckerman
1,039 words
27 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WITH GOLD PRICES near 25-year highs, more investors are searching for stocks that could benefit if the
surge continues, such as shares of small and midsize gold producers that haven't advanced as much as
those of larger gold companies.
Many of these smaller mining companies are known as risky bets, because of high production costs and, in
some cases, challenging pasts. That makes it a more difficult game for investors, though one with a potential
big upside if gold stays at these high levels.
As gold prices have climbed, to $559.70 an ounce yesterday, investors have flocked to shares of the largest
producers, such as Newmont Mining Corp., up 40% in the past year, and Freeport-McMoRan Copper &Gold
Inc., which has soared 75%. Focusing on these companies has made sense: They sport the lowest cost of
production, so every time gold jumps a dollar, these producers see added profits.
Some investors and analysts say gold is now expensive enough to enable even less-efficient producers to
benefit. Some of these investors have been focusing on Bema Gold Corp., a Vancouver miner with a $1.7
billon market value, whose stock price has risen 29% in the past 12 months. Those with even greater risk
tolerance are looking at Hecla Mining Co., a Coeur D'Alene, Idaho, gold and silver producer with a market
value of $600 million that has fallen more than 14% in the past year. Yesterday, Bema closed at $3.70, up
0.82%, on the American Stock Exchange. Hecla shares closed up 14% at $5.02 in composite trading on the
New York Stock Exchange.
On the heels of the recent acquisition of Placer Dome Inc., by Canadian miner Barrick Gold Corp., some
analysts have pointed to Bema and other midsize players such as Eldorado Gold Corp., and Yamana Gold
Inc., as possible takeover candidates.
"There's consolidation going on in the industry," says Bema Chairman and Chief Executive Clive Johnson.
"The big companies aren't making major discoveries, and if the bull market continues you'll see more of it."
Some hedge funds that are focusing on these smaller companies note that there often is a delay between the
rise of a commodity -- and an accompanying climb in the price of shares of the largest producers -- and the
catch-up rally that follows among the smaller companies with more challenges. That is why small oil and gas
exploration-and-production companies have rallied only in recent months, well after oil prices and the biggest
companies soared.
Others are bullish on smaller gold players in part because new money, including that from central banks,
investors from Persian Gulf states and individual investors, increasingly is looking for plays on gold.
Meanwhile, demand for jewelry from emerging markets is on the rise and some worry about whether global
gold production can keep up.
"There is significant positive momentum behind gold, with new investment funds reported to be investing
across the board," says John Meyer, an analyst at London securities firm Numis Securities Ltd., who is a fan
of a number of smaller gold producers.
The enthusiasm for gold could ebb if fears of inflation subside. And the smaller miners aren't exactly closing
in on windfall profits. Hecla is using profit from higher gold and silver prices to fund increased spending on
exploration. It had 12 years of losses end in 2002, a rough period for the business in which Hecla's former
CEO, Art Brown, said the company was "close to bankruptcy."
Perhaps most important, high production costs make it difficult for many of the smaller players to record big
profits. It costs Bema about $300 to extract one ounce of gold, according to Parvathy Krishnan, a stock
analyst at Morningtar Inc. That is somewhat above the industry's average. Bema has gold mines and

--- Page 73 ---
Page 73 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
development projects in Russia, Chile and South Africa. It reported a loss in the first nine months of 2005,
despite the rise in gold prices.
For the first nine months of 2005, Hecla's average cash cost to mine gold was $315 an ounce. By contrast, it
costs GoldCorp Inc., one of the giants in the business, less than $100 to extract an ounce of gold, according
to Ms. Krishnan. Bema says its production is expensive because it has big operations in South Africa, and the
strengthening of the South African rand has pushed up production costs in dollar terms.
Gold futures yesterday fell $2.50, to $559.70 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange.
Production costs also are rising because of climbing energy and steel prices. That makes it more difficult for
many producers to see windfall profits. And it is somewhat harder to get environmental approvals to mine for
gold.
"Gold prices will have to be very, very high for some of the companies to benefit," Ms. Krishnan says.
Bulls acknowledge the production challenges but say that gold is getting to the point that even some of the
less-efficient producers will be able to make more money.
Companies like Bema that are expected to step up their gold production in the next two years may be able to
spread out some of their production costs, potentially sending profits higher. Bema says it will produce almost
400,000 ounces of gold in 2006, but it expects production to increase to almost one million ounces in 2008,
thanks to more gold coming from its Kupol mine in Russia, a low-cost mine. If it can boost production, but
Bema's costs don't rise as quickly, earnings will jump, bulls say. The company says it locks in the price of
about 10% to 15% of its gold reserves and resources, as a hedge as it builds new mines, but the remainder
is enough to allow Bema to benefit if gold prices stay high.
"Production costs are rising in U.S.-dollar terms for many U.S. and non-U.S.-based companies," Numis's Mr.
Meyer says. "But gold prices have risen far faster and further than costs."

Home Sales, Bond Yields Stall Stocks As Gold Reaches a New 25-Year High
Wednesday's Markets
Home Sales, Bond Yields Stall Stocks As Gold Reaches a New 25-Year High
By E.S. Browning
520 words
26 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE WEEK'S mini-rally in the stock market stalled, as anxiety about falling home sales and rising bond yields
outweighed relief at another decline in oil prices.
With investors still nervous about the West's showdown with Iran over its nuclear program and about the
outlook for corporate profits, the Dow Jones Industrial Average edged down 0.02%, or 2.48 points, to
10709.74, still virtually unchanged for the year. Other major indexes also fell slightly in heavy trading.
Gold, which investors turn to in times of trouble, hit another 25-year high in New York, rising $4.50 to $562.20
an ounce.
Companies recently have released inconsistent quarterly profit reports, some beating expectations and others
missing. Worse, corporate forecasts of coming performance have been inconsistent.
"The market is a bit confused over how strong the economy will be over the next few quarters," said Jay
Suskind, director of trading at brokerage firm Ryan Beck in Florham Park, N.J. "It can't get going as a result."
Stocks slumped early in the day on a report that sales of previously owned homes fell more than expected in
December. That stoked worries about whether consumers, who have borrowed heavily against their homes,
will be able to keep up their spending habits.
Stocks temporarily recovered when crude-oil futures fell below $66 a barrel following a government report
showing surprisingly strong reserves of gasoline and heating fuel last week. Crude finished down $1.21 at
$65.85, continuing to reverse last week's strong gains.
But before stock investors could get too comfortable, they noticed that weak demand for a $22 billion auction
of two-year notes had driven Treasury-issue prices lower, pushing the yield of the 10-year Treasury note up
to 4.477%, its highest level in a month. Higher yields push up other market interest rates and put a brake on
the economy -- bad news since the Treasury Department plans a series of bond auctions in coming weeks.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.17%, or 2.18 points, to 1264.68, still up 1.3% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index declined 0.20%, or 4.60 points, to 2260.65, up 2.5% in 2006.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.58%, or 1.19 points, to 206.12.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.93 billion shares traded, 1,556 stocks rose and
1,832 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 22/32, or $6.88 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.477%. The 30-year bond was down 1 11/32 to yield 4.656%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 115.71 yen, up from 114.59, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2245 from $1.2282.

Stocks Muster Small Rebound After Friday Fall --- Strong Profit Report From Ford, Drop in Oil Prices Provide a Lift, But Investors Still...
Monday's Markets
Stocks Muster Small Rebound After Friday Fall --- Strong Profit Report From Ford, Drop in Oil Prices
Provide a Lift, But Investors Still Seem Cautious
By E.S. Browning
510 words
24 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS REBOUNDED, but only barely, after suffering their worst declines in nearly three years on Friday.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, down more than 213 points on Friday, recovered 21.38 points, or 0.2%, to
10688.77, still down 0.3% for 2006. Stocks benefited from Ford Motor Co.'s restructuring announcement and
profit report, which helped push up Dow component General Motors Corp.
Oil futures, which on Friday had surged to a four-month high, eased 38 cents to $68.10 a barrel.
But the miserliness of the stock uptick left traders still nervous.
"It was kind of encouraging that the bleeding didn't continue," said senior trader Bob Basel at Citigroup's New
York brokerage arm. "But I need to see it heal up over the course of a few days in order to be impressed."
Friday's declines were sparked by worries about the showdown between the West and Iran over Iran's
nuclear program, which some investors feared could lead to a reduction in Iran's oil exports and perhaps
even to a military confrontation. But there was little additional news on that subject over the weekend, and
investors were relieved to see oil prices ease amid talk that warm weather in the Northeast would restrain
demand for heating oil.
This week's flood of quarterly profit reports, including reports from GM and Microsoft Corp. due on Thursday,
could influence the market's direction. So could any developments on Iran or on oil. Investors also will be
looking at economic news, including reports on industrial production today, sales of previously owned homes
tomorrow, durable-good orders on Thursday and new-home sales and fourth-quarter economic growth, both
on Friday.
Yesterday, stocks benefited from comments from the presidents of two regional Federal Reserve banks that
were interpreted as hints that the Fed plans to stop raising short-term interest rates soon. Higher rates
attract money to dollar-denominated investments, and the dollar fell to a four-month low against the euro.
Other indexes also showed small rebounds. The S&P 500 index rose 0.18%, or 2.33 points, to 1263.82, up
1.2% this year. The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many tech stocks were battered last week, eked out a
gain of 0.03%, or 0.77 of a point, to 2248.47, up 2% for 2006. Gold, which investors often buy in times of
worry, rose $4.80 an ounce to $558.30, now 10 cents short of Thursday's 25-year New York high.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose; bonds fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 1/32, or 31 cents per $1,000 invested, to yield 4.359%.
The 30-year bond fell 7/32, yielding 4.538%.
The dollar weakened, to 114.46 yen, down from 115.26; the euro rose to $1.2306 from $1.2137.

Stocks Slide 2%, Driven by Worries On Oil, Earnings --- Worst Drop Since May 2003 Undoes Dow Gains for Year; Long-Term Fears over Iran
Stocks Slide 2%, Driven by Worries On Oil, Earnings --- Worst Drop Since May 2003 Undoes Dow
Gains for Year; Long-Term Fears over Iran
By E.S. Browning and Peter A. McKay
1,227 words
21 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The stock market dropped sharply Friday as worries about high energy prices, some disappointing corporate
news and fears of a showdown with Iran undid much of the market's strong start to the year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished down 213.32 points, or 1.96%, at 10667.39 -- its biggest drop in
percentage terms since May 2003. The industrials are now down 0.47% so far this year, after a decline of
0.6% last year. Other indicators were down sharply on the day, too, but clung to small gains for the year.
Almost 2.12 billion shares changed hands on the New York Stock Exchange, the heaviest trading in more
than three months.
After it was all over, investors were left to wonder whether the selloff was a one-day jolt or the beginning of
something more troublesome.
Among the immediate catalysts for Friday's decline were a $1.52, or 2.27%, surge in oil futures to $68.35 a
barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange, a disappointing quarterly profit report from banking giant
Citigroup, and quarterly sales figures from General Electric that missed Wall Street expectations. That news
came on top of soft forecasts earlier in the week from Apple Computer and eBay, disappointing profits at Intel
and Yahoo, and a three-day plunge in Tokyo's Nikkei Stock Average amid allegations of securities violations
involving once-highflying Internet company Livedoor.
GE fell 3.8% and Citigroup gave up 4.7%. Some of the heaviest declines came in technology stocks, which
had risen sharply amid the excitement of the stock rally at the start of the year. Google, whose stock price
more than doubled last year, fell 8.5%, leaving it down 14% for the week. Motorola, whose quarterly revenue
came in below Wall Street expectations, was down 7.6% Friday.
But many money managers say that even after accounting for all the immediate factors depressing the
market, there is a deeper problem. They fear that the West's confrontation with Iran over its nuclear program
could disrupt world oil supplies and, worse, lead to long-lasting tension and even military action.
"The price run-up in oil to $68 isn't going to bring this economy down. But if the Iran situation starts to unravel
more and there is some kind of embargo and an oil cutoff, that would be a problem," said John Waterman,
chief investment officer at Rittenhouse Asset Management in Radnor, Pa., an arm of financial-services group
Nuveen Investments. "And then you have the worry about the military option."
Mr. Waterman said he remains optimistic that companies will continue to report earnings gains this year and
that stocks will recover and move up as much as 10% for 2006. But their ability to do so, he added, will
depend in part on a resolution of the Iran issue that helps guarantee future oil supplies. "If Iran continues to
move forward on its nuclear program, then you have all these questions about what that would lead to," Mr.
Waterman said. "All of that makes us more cautious as investors."
That caution was evident on stock-trading floors across the country Friday as the sell orders flooded in and
the buy orders evaporated. "It was kind of hairy," said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at brokerage firm
SG Cowen in New York. "It was kind of an ugly day. People were selling stock and they were just knocking
down the prices because no one was buying."
To cap things off, he said, larger-scale selling driven by computer programs kicked in at day's end, as money
managers unloaded broad groups of stocks, worsening the declines.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, filled with technology companies, fell 2.35% Friday to 2247.70. It had been up
as much as 5.7% for the year as of Jan. 11, but now is up only 1.9%. The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock
index fell 1.83% Friday to 1261.49, still up 1.1% so far this year.

--- Page 81 ---
Page 81 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Optimists were hoping that the worsening confrontation with Iran would blow over, or at least lessen, in time.
That would have permitted oil prices to pull back, moderating some of the concerns about supply problems
that cropped up over the past week. Coming into the new year, many analysts had expected a modest
pullback or, at worst, highs of around $65 a barrel, with ample supplies and rising inventories keeping the oil
market in check.
But the Iran conflict hasn't blown over. Also contributing to rising oil prices are fears of rebel attacks against
oil facilities in Nigeria. Now, said Jon Najarian, chief market strategist at PTI Securities &Futures LP, the oil
market's Iran-related "risk premium" could grow, from around $7 a barrel now to $10 or $11 a barrel in the
next few weeks. "That would put the crude price around $72," a price he said would be "unheard of" at a time
when there are no hurricanes or refinery shutdowns. Oil most recently hit a 23-year high in August when it
closed in New York at $69.81 a barrel.
"That's what happens when people are worried about the world's No. 4 producer going offline at any point,"
Mr. Najarian said.
Among the broader worries that trouble some investors is the possibility of a resurgence of inflation. Some,
likewise, fear the opposite -- that the steady stream of Federal Reserve interest-rate increases has slowed
consumer borrowing and spending, the housing market and broader economic growth to the point where
corporate results now will prove disappointing.
The pessimists point to the performance of the bond market, in which yields on short-term notes and bills in
some cases are now higher than those for longer-term notes. When that happens, it can mean that the bond
market expects economic growth to be sharply slower in the future than now.
Gold prices fell 0.9%, or $5, to $554 a troy ounce Friday, off 0.4% on the week on Nymex's Comex division.
But for the year, gold has jumped more than 7% and remains near a 25-year high. Investors typically buy
gold in times of worries about inflation or international security.
"The data from the government haven't shown that inflation is much of a worry for the market, but if you look
at gold, I think it's telling us that maybe some inflation is upon us, especially due to energy prices," said
Michael Cuggino, president and chief executive of Permanent Portfolio Family of Funds.
Michael Farr, president of money-management firm Farr, Miller &Washington in Washington, took consolation
in the fact that the S&P 500 and Nasdaq Composite remain in the black for 2006.
He said stock prices should eventually "catch up" with quarterly profit gains of companies in the S&P 500.
Wall Street analysts expect those profits to grow at double-digit rates throughout the year, according to
research firm Thomson Financial.
"This is the sort of market where investors really earn their stripes," Mr. Farr said. "They need to hold on and
suffer."

U.S. STOCKS TUMBLED amid worries about Iran, high energy prices and disappointing corporate earnings.
The Dow industrials slid 213.32 points, or 1.96%, to 10667.39, their biggest percentage drop since May 2003,
wiping out the year's gains and leaving the average off 0.47% for 2006. The Nasdaq dropped 2.35%. The
S&P 500 fell 1.83%. Google declined 8.5%, its biggest ever percentage drop. Oil prices jumped $1.52 to

revenue fell short of forecasts.

rising, suggesting the bank is edging toward abandoning its ultra-easy monetary policy.

Still Morning in America
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Still Morning in America
889 words
20 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Twenty-five years ago today, Ronald Reagan was inaugurated as the 40th President of the United States
promising less intrusive government, lower tax rates and victory over communism. On that same day, the
American hostages in Iran were freed after 444 days of captivity. If the story of history is one long and
arduous march toward freedom, this was a momentous day well worth commemorating.
All the more so because over this 25-year period prosperity has been the rule, not the exception, for America
-- in stark contrast to the stagflationary 1970s. Perhaps the greatest tribute to the success of Reaganomics is
that, over the course of the past 276 months, the U.S. economy has been in recession for only 15. That is to
say, 94% of the time the U.S. economy has been creating jobs (43 million in all) and wealth ($30 trillion).
More wealth has been created in the U.S. in the last quarter-century than in the previous 200 years. The
policy lessons of this supply-side prosperity need to be constantly relearned, lest we return to the errors that
produced the 1970s.
The heart and soul of Reagan's economic agenda were sound money (making the dollar "as good as gold,"
as Reagan used to put it) and lower tax rates. On monetary policy, Reagan has won a resounding victory.
Today, nearly all economists agree with Reagan's then-controversial belief that the sole purpose of monetary
policy should be to keep prices stable. Double-digit inflation is a distant memory unlikely to recur anytime
soon.
On tax policy, Reaganomics has also carried the day, if somewhat less completely. Tax rates in the U.S. are
on average half as high now as they were in the 1970s, and almost every nation has followed the Reagan
model of lower tax rates. Even Bill Clinton only dared to raise the top marginal income tax rate back to 39.5%,
not 50% or 70%.
Nonetheless, tax cuts still stand in disrepute among most of the media, academics and Democrats in
Congress, albeit for shifting reasons. When Reagan proposed his 30% across-the-board tax-rate cut, his
critics howled that this would cause demand to rise and lead to hyper-inflation. In fact, supply rose faster
than demand, and inflation fell to 4% from 13% and has fallen even lower since. When the economy went
into a deep recession in 1981-82, Reagan's adversaries (and some of his own advisers) declared his tax cuts
a failure. Reagan said stay the course, and the moment the final leg of the tax cut took effect, in January of
1983, the economy roared to life with an expansion that lasted more than seven years.
When the budget deficit rose in the mid-1980s, the liberals warned that if Reagan would not raise taxes
interest rates would skyrocket. He didn't and rates didn't. After the 1987 stock market crash, liberal John
Kenneth Galbraith wrote that "this debacle marks the last chapter of Reaganomics . . . and the irresponsible
tax cuts." Again, Reagan refused to buckle and two months later the stock market recovered and the
expansion roared on -- an expansion that didn't end until George H.W. Bush reversed course and raised
taxes in 1990.
The Gipper's critics have written an economic history of the 1990s that they portray as a repudiation of
Reaganomics. In this telling -- known as Rubinomics -- the Clinton tax hikes of 1993 ended the budget deficit,
which caused interest rates to fall, which produced the boom of the mid- to late-1990s. In fact, the budget
deficit hardly fell at all in the immediate aftermath of the tax hike, and while long-term interest rates fell in
1993, they shot back up again in 1994 almost precisely through Election Day (rising by some 230 basis points
from October 1993 to November 1994).
On that day, voters repudiated the Clinton tax hikes and the specter of HillaryCare and gave Republicans
control of Capitol Hill to govern on the Reaganite agenda of lowering taxes and shrinking runaway
government. Both the stock and bond markets turned upward precisely on Election Day in 1994, beginning a
whirlwind six-year rally. By 1998, growth and fiscal restraint delivered a budget surplus for the first time in
nearly 30 years. In 1997 President Clinton signed a further reduction in the capital gains tax, which propelled
investment and the stock market to even greater heights.

--- Page 86 ---
Page 86 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The latest chapter of this story is the 2003 income and investment tax cuts enacted by the current President
Bush. As in 1981, opponents insisted those tax cuts would harm the economy by increasing the deficit and
driving up interest rates. But in the two and a half years since those tax cuts passed, the economy and tax
revenues have both surged.
Where Republicans have most strayed from the Reagan vision has been on controlling federal spending. But
most still adhere to his tax-cutting lessons, with a few prominent exceptions (notably Senator John McCain).
They should all recall the Gipper's words in his inauguration speech 25 years ago: "It is no coincidence that
our present troubles parallel and are proportionate to the intervention and intrusion in our lives that result from
unnecessary and excessive growth of government."

For Oil-Rich Brides, Caviar, Crystal, And 1,000 Guests --- Persian Gulf Sees a Boom In Extravagant Weddings; Almonds Coated in Gold
For Oil-Rich Brides, Caviar, Crystal, And 1,000 Guests --- Persian Gulf Sees a Boom In Extravagant
Weddings; Almonds Coated in Gold
By Yasmine El-Rashidi
1,153 words
20 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
DUBAI, United Arab Emirates -- When Noor Al-Roomi planned her son's recent wedding, she knew she was
up against tough competition from many other big weddings. "Everyone wants to hold a more impressive
wedding than the family before," says Ms. Al-Roomi.
She managed to hold her own. Arrangements included bringing in Beluga caviar from the Caspian,
tablecloths from Switzerland, a cake chef from France and flowers from Holland. Guests received as favors
gold-rimmed crystal bowls filled with Belgian chocolates. "You don't want to have anything that is easily
found on the local market. It has to be exclusive," she explains. The groom's family spent $250,000 on the
six-hour affair.
The Al-Roomi nuptials are part of a wedding industry that is resurgent in the Persian Gulf economic boom fed
by higher oil prices.
When oil prices fell in the 1980s and 1990s, fewer families in the region could spring for the extravagant
weddings their social set had come to expect. Unable to shoulder the cost of a properly ostentatious wedding,
couples put off marrying.
The average number of marriages in the United Arab Emirates dropped by one-quarter between the
mid-1980s and the end of the 1990s. In Kuwait, the number fell about 15% in that same period, as it did in
Bahrain, according to the Central Statistics Organization.
Given the rapidly growing populations and the increasing number of men and women reaching "marriageable
age," which generally means the early 20s, Persian Gulf rulers became concerned that lower marriage rates
could eventually translate into slower population growth. Some rulers, who are essentially the heads of large
extended families, worried that more of their young men would marry foreigners in order to forgo fancy
weddings and save money.
In the UAE, the late president Sheik Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan set up a marriage fund, offering grants to
male citizens to encourage them to marry local women. "In the '90s, a growing number of UAE women were
remaining unmarried," fretted an official UAE report on social development. Established in 1994, the fund
offered grants of about $20,000 to help young men meet wedding costs. The sheik himself then footed the bill
for group ceremonies in which dozens of young couples would marry. Because the government viewed
extravagant weddings as a deterrent to men marrying local women, it announced that families holding such
events would be subject to fines of up to $136,000 or even prison sentences. But it is unclear whether any
cases have ever been brought, and it appears today that the threat has not curbed lavish weddings.
Times have changed with the price of oil. UAE officials say marriage rates have climbed steadily since 2003.
The past two years have seen 20% annual increases in marriages. Meanwhile, mass weddings have tailed
off since their peak in 1999.
Wedding planners are now putting on the dog. "I have mothers coming to me requesting a particular crystal
glass that they saw at a royal ball in London. Or they want a design for a gift box that no one would have ever
seen before. Or they want edible-gold-coated almonds," says wedding planner Dina Al-Rashad, who is
based in Kuwait.
For Ms. Al-Rashad this has meant traveling the globe looking for the perfect glass, or fork, or ribbon or party
favor.
The wedding celebration for Hissa Khalaf, a 24-year-old bride from a wealthy family, played out over three
days recently in the Emirate of Abu Dhabi. On the first evening there was a henna party -- a sort of bridal
shower. More than 300 girls gathered in a ballroom to dance, dine and have their bodies decorated with

--- Page 88 ---
Page 88 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
temporary henna tattoos expertly placed by a team of Sudanese henna artists flown in from Egypt, according
to Hala Al-Abbar, a cousin of the bride. Guests took home finely engraved silver boxes, she says.
The following day, close to 800 guests gathered at a sprawling beach mansion for a party that lasted from
noon until well past midnight. The bride changed her outfit just about every hour, and the buffet, which
spanned 25 tables, was changed five times, says Ms. Al-Abbar. Guests received gold coins as souvenirs.
"They spent as much on that day as most couples spend on a wedding night. More," says Ms. Al-Abbar.
The main event on the following night had about 1,000 guests -- about 1,400 had been invited -- filling the
mansion and its grounds.
The groom was carried in on a litter by men in turbans. The bride followed on her own more delicate gold
throne, artificial smoke rising from below to give her an ethereal appearance. From above, glitter rained down
on her while guests tossed gold coins and turquoise stones, provided on tables as good-luck omens. That
night's party favor: a cut-crystal box filled with almonds and containing a large gold coin embossed with the
couple's names and a Sura, or verse, from the Quran.
For the wedding party, a European orchestra and a disc jockey from Spain were flown in. Other weddings
have featured Egyptian pop stars, at up to $40,000 for brief appearances. For a traditional touch, sprawling
Arabian desert affairs are held in tents that rent for as much as about $25,000.
Hoteliers say the ceremonies are their most reliable business, and now bring in at least half of annual
catering revenue for resorts such as the Ritz-Carlton, Dubai, according to officials at the hotel.
The London-based Diamond Trading Co. recently set up shop in Dubai, and a chunk of its business comes
from weddings. "Arab nationals are passionate about diamonds, and the boom makes this the fourth largest
market in the world," says Nicholas Graham-Smith, DTC's regional director. "The UAE is at the forefront of it
all."
While the Ministry of Labor and Social Affairs says the percentage of local men marrying foreigners has
dropped, rulers still have qualms about the lavish affairs. They've asked locals to curb spending. Parents, too,
are worrying. In the poorer and more conservative neighboring Emirate of Fujairah, the Parents Council of a
local school recently urged the Marriage Fund to continue to support mass weddings and to enforce penalties
against those who fritter away their money.
"This spending trend will make young men feel they can't get married to locals, which will mean they'll marry
foreigners, which we know leads to divorce. Or they'll marry an Emirati and end up in very large debt," says
Maryam Eissa, the mother of four young children. Fujairah's Sheik Abdullah Al-Sharqi has asked the Marriage
Fund to take measures to reverse the trend.

Nikkei Plunges Amid Livedoor's Legal Woes
World Stock Markets
Nikkei Plunges Amid Livedoor's Legal Woes
A Wall Street Journal News Roundup
1,326 words
18 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
JAPAN'S STOCK MARKET plunged for a second day Wednesday as investors were whipsawed by reports
that prosecutors are investigating allegations that Japanese Internet company Livedoor Co. may have
concealed massive losses.
The probe sparked a selloff of Japanese stocks, with the benchmark Nikkei 225 down 510.97 points, or 3.2%,
to 15294.98 at the beginning of Wednesday's afternoon session. That put the index down 5.4% since trading
began Tuesday, when concerns about Livedoor's legal problems helped push the market 2.8% lower and
investors locked in trading profits. Wednesday's early rout gained momentum after the Tokyo Stock
Exchange said it would suspend trading on the world's second-largest stock market if the number of trades
exceeded its system's capacity. The exchange said that the number of trades totalled 2.32 million during the
morning session, nearing the exchange's capacity to handle four million.
On Tuesday, traders erased nearly $1 billion of Livedoor's market capitalization as the stock fell 14% to 596
yen ($5.19). The Tokyo exchange said it had temporarily suspended trading of the company's shares.
Last year Livedoor founder Takafumi Horie became a symbol of the new, aggressive Japanese entrepreneur.
The 33-year-old college dropout rapidly built his Internet business by acquiring one company after another.
Livedoor staged a hostile takeover of a subsidiary of Japan's biggest television broadcaster, forcing it into a
business alliance with Livedoor.
Now, Mr. Horie's ascent has been checked, at least for the moment. On Monday night, investigators from the
Tokyo District Prosecutors Office raided Livedoor's Tokyo offices as part of their investigation into whether the
company had knowingly misled investors by spreading false information about its business practices and
financial state.
The investigation into Livedoor's operations has the potential to muffle -- at least temporarily -- the booming
Japanese stock market after a 40% rise last year fueled in part by an increasing embrace of Western-style
corporate-governance practices, analysts say. It might also cool Japan's increasing romance with mergers
and acquisitions, if companies become concerned they will attract unwanted attention.
Mr. Horie "is a symbol of the recent activity in M&A and is seen as a genius for raising company value and
improving shareholder value," said Ikuo Yasuda, chief executive of takeover consultancy Pinnacle Inc. "This
could really chill the stock market."
Tokyo prosecutors said they were looking for evidence that Livedoor had spread false information about its
business, a violation of the country's securities and exchange law. Neither the prosecutors' office nor
Livedoor would comment further on the nature of the probe.
Local media reported that prosecutors want to know if a Livedoor unit made misleading statements about an
acquisition in order to boost its share price. Reports also said prosecutors suspected the company may have
exaggerated its financial results in order to boost its share price.
At an early-morning news conference Tuesday, Mr. Horie apologized for the difficulties and pledged to
cooperate. "We are making an all-out effort to [deal with] the situation," he said.
Mr. Horie, whose net worth is estimated at $2 billion, rose to prominence by habitually defying convention. He
dropped out of the prestigious University of Tokyo to establish his own Web site design company, which
became Livedoor after a series of acquisitions and name changes.
Mr. Horie acquired numerous companies to give Livedoor far-flung business units from software to Internet
connections and online financial services. The expand-by-acquisition method is common in the U.S., but still
relatively new to Japan, where companies have usually expanded into new fields more cautiously. He

--- Page 90 ---
Page 90 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
exploited a loophole in Japanese market rules to buy a big stake in a radio company during off-hours trading.
He also launched a controversial 100-for-1 stock split that boosted Livedoor's share price.
The spiky-haired entrepreneur has ignored Japanese virtues of reservation and modesty. He has appeared
frequently on television programs, once showing off his blue-green Ferrari, and posed for fashion magazines,
including the Japanese version of GQ.
Still, Mr. Horie's willingness to break the mold has attracted the attention of Japan's most powerful. In
September, Prime Minister Junichiro Koizumi recruited him as a candidate for a snap election. Mr. Horie lost
the vote, but gained awareness of his company.
---
Other Market Activity
Stocks in Hong Kong and Seoul fell sharply amid a round of Asian declines spurred by Tokyo's selloff.
European markets closed lower, as oil prices topped $65 a barrel and investors eyed losses in Japan and
some mixed corporate news.
Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index fell 1%, or 2.46 points, to 241.76. Excluding the U.S., the index
decreased 1.6%, or 3.29 points, to 204.45.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 index shed 0.72%, or 41.20 points, to 5699.00. Tesco, Britain's biggest
supermarket chain, eased 1.8%, after an update on its holiday-period United Kingdom comparable sales.
Standard Chartered, a big gainer Monday, was down 2.2% as investors had second thoughts about potential
interest from U.S. banks in a combination.
In HONG KONG, the blue-chip Hang Seng Index dropped 1.3%, or 201.52 points, to 15576.20. The market
has begun to unwind a steep increase of more than 5% that began in late December. Not even Chinese gold
producer Lingbao Gold Ltd., which surged 33% on its first day of trading last week, managed to escape
profit-taking, despite rising gold prices.
In SEOUL, investors sold major banks and brokerage firms that had risen in recent sessions. The Korea
Composite Stock Price Index, or Kospi, declined 2.3%, or 32.21 points, to 1389.58.
In JAKARTA, Indonesian shares ended lower for a fourth consecutive session, led by further selling in bank
blue chips, on renewed fears of poor 2005 earnings because of high interest rates. The Jakarta Stock
Exchange Composite Index ended down 1.8%, or 22.39 points, at 1212.87.
In MEXICO CITY, stocks tumbled Tuesday as long-anticipated profit-taking brought the market down almost
as abruptly as investors had bid it up in recent weeks. Mexico's key IPC index closed down 2.5%, or 468.66
points, to 18489.63. Mexico's IPC has set five record high closes so far this year, and even topped 19000 for
the first time Wednesday. This year's advance comes after three years of solid gains. Market benchmark
America Movil's L shares pulled back 4.9% while the L shares for Telmex fell 2.5%.
In TORONTO, the key stock index ended lower, hurt by profit-taking in many sectors. However, the market
did get some support from gains in energy stocks on the back of higher crude prices. The SPX/TSX
Composite Index fell 31.36 points, or 0.27%, to 11689.61.
In BEIJING, China's shares ended higher on gains in financial companies. The benchmark Shanghai
Composite Index, which tracks both A and B shares, ended up 0.5% at 1208.44.

Tokyo Slips on Tech Selling --- Most Asian Markets Fall, While Brazil, Toronto Hit Record Highs
World Stock Markets
Tokyo Slips on Tech Selling --- Most Asian Markets Fall, While Brazil, Toronto Hit Record Highs
By Jamie Miyazaki and Sarah Turner
1,102 words
17 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TOKYO STOCKS slid yesterday while U.S. markets were closed, in only their second daily loss this year.
Most other Asian stock markets declined, too. Brazilian shares crossed a milestone, and Toronto stocks hit a
record.
European shares, meanwhile, traded near 3 1/2-year highs but were only modestly changed. Overall, the
Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding the U.S., rose 0.16%, or 0.33 point, to 207.74.
In TOKYO, prices were hurt by profit-taking on tech stocks such as Advantest and a slew of ratings cuts for
consumer-finance companies such as Aiful. The 225-share Nikkei Stock Average fell 1.1%, or 186.92 points,
to 16268.03.
"Technology stocks have been particularly hot over the past month, and after the strong gains are, along with
the market, due for a cooldown," said Hajime Kitano, equity strategist at J.P. Morgan Securities in Tokyo.
Semiconductor-equipment maker Advantest fell 2.3%, while consumer-electronics company Matsushita
Electric Industrial decreased 3.1%. Lingering worries about the weaker dollar continued to weigh on auto
makers. Honda Motor shed 2.6%, while Suzuki Motor fell 2.3%.
Consumer-finance companies took a hammering after a Japanese Supreme Court ruling Friday curtailed the
interest-rate levels they can charge customers. Analyst downgrades followed. Aiful, whose City's subsidiary
was involved in the court case, fell 9.4%. Fellow lender Acom's share price decreased 8.8%.
Headaches for consumer-finance issues translated into a boon for banking issues, which were unaffected by
the court's ruling. The banking sector rebounded after trailing behind much of the Nikkei's gains in the past
four weeks. Sumitomo Mitsui Financial Group, Japan's third-largest bank by assets, led the revival among
banks, rising 1.9%. Mitsubishi UFJ Financial Group, the world's largest bank by assets, increased nearly 1%.
Another big gainer: semiconductor manufacturer Elpida Memory, up 5.6%, after investors shrugged off a
profit downgrade it issued Friday.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 added 0.5%, or 29.30 points, to 5740.20. Standard Chartered jumped 5.7% after
weekend press reports that it could be in line for a bid from U.S. banks. Mining stocks moved higher, led by
Anglo American. It gained 1.3% after a report that it is trying to accelerate a program of asset sales that could
result in a $1 billion capital return to shareholders.
In SAO PAULO, Brazilian stocks hit another in a series of records, as the Bovespa index rose 1.8% to close
above 36000 for the first time, closing up 636.56 points at 36533.68. Investors showed enthusiasm over the
possibility that the country's central bank at a meeting this week will make a deeper-than-expected
interest-rate cut.
In SEOUL, South Korean shares rose to a record, led by gains in major technology companies Samsung and
LG.Philips LCD after they reported strong earnings last week. The Korea Composite Stock Price Index, or
Kospi, gained 0.4%, or 5.51 points, to 1421.79.
In HONG KONG, shares fell for the first time in 10 trading sessions as investors locked in profits after rising
more than 5% since the end of December. The benchmark Hang Seng Index declined 0.6%, or 10.25 points,
to 15777.72. Stocks hit hardest included China Unicom, which fell 2.2%, and port operator Cosco Pacific, was
down 1.7%. Tianjin Development decreased 0.6%. Its shares resumed trading yesterday after the company
disclosed it was purchasing a 91% stake in a Chinese steam-power supplier, and that it would issue 20
million new shares to fund the acquisition.

In KUALA LUMPUR, Malaysia's key stock index declined 0.6% on profit-taking and jitters caused by news of

increased 1, or 1.3%, to 79.25. Elsewhere, Dofasco surged 6.60, or 10%, to 72.40 as the bidding war for the
integrated steel producer continued unabated. ---

Gold Futures Jump After Dip to Hit A 25-Year High
Commodities Report
Gold Futures Jump After Dip to Hit A 25-Year High
By Allen Sykora Dow Jones Newswires
422 words
14 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold futures in New York surged to a 25-year high Friday, piercing through the previous trading range as
investors used a recent price dip as a buying opportunity.
The move was described as partially based on technical charts, but fundamental influences also played a
role, as worries about Iran's nuclear program and the potential for inflation underpinned trading, analysts
said.
The most-active February gold contract gained $7.70 an ounce to $557 on the Comex division of the New
York Mercantile Exchange. It peaked at $558.80, the highest price for Comex gold futures since January
1981. The January contract rose $7.80 to $556.10.
"We had a very impressive performance, led by a very strong physical market and investment-fund interest in
the market," said Jim Quinn, commodity floor analyst with A.G. Edwards.
Gold opened lower, attracting bargain hunting.
"It was almost a repeat of previous sessions in that when you have a dip, it seems to become a buying
opportunity," said George Gero, vice president with RBC Capital Markets Global Futures. "It was accelerated
by geopolitical events, such as Iran tensions."
Mr. Quinn reported that buy stops -- preplaced orders to buy when certain chart points are reached -- were hit
above $551 and $553.
"We finally broke out of the range we've been in all week long," said Paul McLeod, vice president for precious
metals at Commerzbank. "We saw some nice fund buying come in as we broke $550 on spot [gold]."
A couple of floor contacts said some dealer buying also occurred.
Some of the strength was short covering -- in which traders buy to offset previous positions in which they had
sold -- ahead of the long Martin Luther King Jr. weekend, Mr. Gero added. Markets closed early ahead of the
holiday and will be closed Monday. Normal trade resumes Tuesday.
Some of gold's strength may have been the result of economic data, said Mr. Gero. While he described it as
"benign," traders noticed higher energy costs.
The December producer price index rose 0.9%, led by a rise in energy and gasoline prices. Core PPI
excluding food and energy was up just 0.1%.
In other commodity markets:
COFFEE: Prices on the New York Board of Trade rose, supported by options-related activity and supply
tightness in top producer Brazil due to a smaller harvest. March coffee gained 2.4 cents to $1.1995 a pound.
