--- Page 100 ---
Page 100 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Supermarket
Supermarket
By Brian S. Wesbury
908 words
12 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average has traded above 11,000 for three days in a row, and stands just 680
points (or 6%) from its record high. There is nothing special about 11,000; but, there seems to be some
electricity in the air, a feeling that more good news for investors is on the way.
This is a little surprising. It was just a few weeks ago, on Dec. 27, when the yield curve inverted and
pessimism filled the air. Very often, however, the best time to buy stocks is when no one else wants them and
the pessimists are yelling loudly. That cannot be the only reason, however.
The risks for equity investors are lowest, and the returns greatest, when potential economic growth is high
and inflation is low. And the only way that occurs is if fiscal policy is focused on creating an environment
conducive to entrepreneurial activity, and monetary policy keeps the economy close to price stability.
Between 1965 and 1982, when fiscal and monetary policy fell off the track, stock prices went virtually
nowhere. Any positive returns were generated by dividends. Then President Reagan cut taxes and began
easing regulations, while Paul Volcker hiked interest rates (a lot). The result was an equity boom that began
in August 1982.
As everyone knows, it hasn't always been a smooth ride. But instances of prolonged turbulence in equity
prices are not random; they are the result of mistakes in policy. The market slid from mid-1983 to mid-1984,
when the Fed jacked up rates to fight a non-existent inflation. In 1987, a misguided effort to lower the value
of the dollar led to a crash, which still looks spectacular on charts.
Saddam Hussein's invasion of Kuwait in August 1990 helped push the S&P 500 down by 15% over a
three-month period. But the stage had already been set by an overly tight Fed policy and President Bush's
breaking of his "no new taxes" pledge.
The market sputtered in 1992 and 1993, and then stumbled in 1994, when monetary policy became volatile,
the government debated nationalizing healthcare, and taxes were hiked again. Finally, in 2000, the market
crumbled as the Fed dragged America into its first deflationary ditch since the 1930s.
In the moment of their occurrence, each of these rough patches in equities appeared more ominous than they
do now in the rearview mirror. Step back sometime and look at a long-term chart of the stock market. From
1982 until 2000, the market was in an almost constant boom, recovering very nicely after every stumble, and
moving to higher and higher ground. The reason: During this entire period, after drifting away, government
policies always reverted back to support free-market capitalism.
The big question for investors in 2006 is whether this pattern has changed. Some analysts think that the U.S.
equity market has returned to a period like the 1970s. Others see slow growth for stock prices, in the low- to
mid-single-digit range, for decades ahead.
But if the past is any gauge of the future, the actual performance of markets will be determined by the
direction of fiscal and monetary policy. During the past five years the Fed has taken the U.S. on a roller
coaster, tightening too much in 2000, easing too much over 2001 to 2003, and now tightening again. This is a
thrill ride we did not need to take. Even today, with gold over $540 an ounce, it is not clear that price stability
has returned.
Federal spending has grown much faster than GDP for the past five years, and the U.S. has added a new
drug entitlement. Sarbanes-Oxley has added regulations, while the willingness to move in a protectionist
direction (think steel tariffs) has increased. In addition, attempts to force China toward revaluation are
problematic.
These policies are not the best environment for a continued equity boom. With the extension of the 2003 tax
cuts uncertain, it is understandable that investors have been nervous about the direction of policy.

--- Page 101 ---
Page 101 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
This seems to be the only way to explain why markets have not responded to an incredible surge in
productivity and profitability. Since the third quarter of 2001, after-tax corporate profits have surged 80%,
interest rates have fallen, and yet the S&P 500 is up less than 8%. During this same time period the P-E
ratio, based on trailing earnings, has fallen from roughly 37 to 18. A capitalized profits model, which discounts
profits at current interest rates, shows the market more undervalued than at any time in modern history. The
fundamentals suggest that today is a good time to own stocks.
The tax cut, especially on capital gains and dividends, has been an undeniable success and appears to have
offset the damage from other policy errors. Despite all the negative policy moves, I continue to have faith in
the growing power of the "investor class." Since a majority of Americans own equities, policies that have
historically supported wealth creation should prevail. As a result, the past five years will prove to be just
another pothole in a continued bull market. While 11,000 gives us something to cheer about, a 12,500 Dow
this year is imminently (and eminently) doable, as is another decade of continued prosperity.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief investment strategist with Claymore Advisors LLC.

Dow Industrials End Above 11000 As Stocks Extend New Year's Rally
Dow Industrials End Above 11000 As Stocks Extend New Year's Rally
By E.S. Browning
1,343 words
10 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average pushed above 11000 for the first time since June 2001, continuing one of
the strongest New Year's rallies in two decades.
Spurred by a Goldman Sachs analyst's positive comment about beleaguered General Motors Corp., a
pullback in oil prices and -- above all -- continuing talk that the Federal Reserve soon will stop raising
short-term interest rates, the Dow Jones industrials rose 52.59 points, or 0.48%, to 11011.90. The spreading
sense that the economy remains healthy and interest rates are near their peak indicated that investors are
moving beyond their memories of the 2000 tech-stock collapse and the 2001 terrorist attacks.
All three major indexes -- the Dow industrials, the Nasdaq Composite Index and the Standard &Poor's
500-stock index -- have risen on each of the year's first five trading days, something that hasn't happened
since 1987. All three now stand at 4 1/2-year highs.
Analysts applauded the market's new strength after a downbeat end to 2005 that left the Dow with a decline
for the year of slightly less than 1%. Historically, January's performance has been a strong bellwether for the
rest of the year. And this year's gains reflect a growing belief that the Fed will pilot the nation's economy to a
soft landing: a slowdown that manages to keep inflation under control while also avoiding a recession.
Though the year's strong start sparked hopes for lasting gains, the echo of 1987 led some market watchers to
recall that stocks suffered a meltdown in October of that year, taking their sharpest short-term plunge since
1929. Skeptics also noted that the current bull market, which began in 2002, is more than three years old --
approaching elderly, as bulls go -- and lately has shown signs of fatigue. They warned that for all the day's
excitement, stocks still could be in line for a pullback as the year wears on.
Professional traders said their celebration was subdued and short-lived -- limited to a few whoops of joy,
rather than glasses of Champagne. They have seen the Dow break through 11000 and finish above it on 19
previous occasions, starting on May 3, 1999, only to slide back below that level. They are waiting to see
whether this move will prove more lasting.
But for the day, the optimists held the floor. The Dow now stands just 6% below its record 11722.98, hit on
Jan. 14, 2000. It is up 2.8% so far this year. Corporate profits remain strong. Minutes of the Fed's December
policy meeting, released Jan. 3, indicated that Fed officials are getting closer to an end to their cycle of
interest-rate increases, which began more than 18 months ago. If the Fed stops raising rates without
triggering a recession, many investors believe that will remove the main impediment to further stock-price
gains.
History shows that, in years when the Dow industrials finish higher in the first five days, they are likely to rise
over the rest of the year -- and have a better than 50% chance of turning in an above-average gain. The
widespread hope this year is that individual investors, still licking their wounds from the bear market of
2000-2002, may notice the big round number 11000 and seize on it as a reason to put more money into
stocks.
"If we can hold this beachhead, it could create a self-sustaining cycle of money coming back into the market,"
said Darren Kavesh, chief investment officer at money-management firm Bowling Portfolio Management in
Cincinnati. "We have this psychologically inculcated thing now where everybody has grown to distrust
stocks."
With gains from real-estate investments beginning to cool, some people may be looking for a new place to
put their money, he said. If the market can avoid falling back below 11000, those people could decide to jump
in. If the gains continue, hedge funds, big pools of investment capital that cater to pension funds, foundations
and the wealthy, could also jump on board.

--- Page 105 ---
Page 105 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
On the stock-trading desk at New York brokerage firm Bear Stearns, however, reaction was muted. "For most
of us here, it is not all that significant," said trader Michael Driscoll. "People are mildly positive on what they
have seen so far in 2006. Volumes are up. If we are closer to the end of the interest-rate hikes than we are to
the beginning, that is a positive," because an end to rate increases takes some pressure off of businesses
and consumers alike, he said.
"Maybe the market will grind its way a little higher as the year wears on," said David Briggs, head of stock
trading at mutual-fund group Federated Investors in Pittsburgh. But Mr. Briggs said he expects that, once the
January excitement quiets, "we are going to get much more of the same thing we have had for the past two
years. We will get movement in some sectors but I don't think the overall averages will move very much." As
long as oil supplies are assured and the threat of terrorism doesn't increase seriously, he said, he doesn't
expect a sharp decline in the indexes, but "it is hard for me to think of something that will make the market
gap up 20%."
Further evidence of the muted reaction came from the gold market. Gold, traditionally considered a haven in
times of economic worry, hit its highest New York close in nearly 25 years, rising $9.40 an ounce to $549.10.
It is up 6% so far this year.
One reason stock investors aren't euphoric about Dow 11000 is that other major indexes aren't nearing
records. The S&P 500 and the Nasdaq composite both were more exposed to technology stocks than was
the Dow, and thus fell harder during the bear market. Although they both have risen more than the Dow so far
this year, the S&P remains 15.5% below its record, hit in March 2000, and the Nasdaq, dominated by tech
stocks, is 54% below its record, which also dates from March 2000.
Two other much-watched indexes, on the other hand, did hit records yesterday: the Russell 2000 small-stock
index and the S&P index of midsize stocks.
Stocks commonly begin the year with gains, as new money flows into retirement funds and is invested in
stocks. The big question on investors' minds now is whether the gains can be sustained.
"Valuations of U.S. stocks are still attractive, and our stock markets may receive a boost when the Fed's
current tightening cycle is finished," wrote Benjamin Pace, chief investment officer at Deutsche Bank's
private-banking arm, in a letter to clients this month. "This is not to suggest that the U.S. markets will be
smooth sailing. There are a number of obstacles that may suddenly appear on the horizon." Mr. Pace said
those include the delayed effects on consumers and companies of the Fed's interest-rate increases, the risk
that the Fed will raise rates too high and stifle the economy, and the opposite risk -- that inflation will become
a problem.
While it isn't time to move away from stocks, "investors should be prepared to be nimble," Mr. Pace warned.
Mr. Kavesh of Bowling Portfolio Management said that skepticism about the Dow's latest milestone could be
a positive sign. It means, he said, that many people still are holding money on the sidelines. If the Dow
manages to stay above 11000, he said, optimism will spread, and "it could pull some money out of real
estate" and other alternative investments.

Gold Futures Hit Highest Level In 25 Years on Geopolitical Fears
Commodities Report
Gold Futures Hit Highest Level In 25 Years on Geopolitical Fears
By Allen Sykora Dow Jones Newswires
532 words
10 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Geopolitical concerns and worries about the potential for inflation vaulted New York gold futures to their
highest level in a quarter century yesterday, analysts said, with speculative commodity-fund buying noted.
The benchmark nearby-month January gold futures contract rose $9.40 an ounce to $549.10 on the Comex
division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. The most-active February gold futures gained $9.30 to
$550.50. They peaked at $553.10 in electronic trading about an hour after the open-outcry session, the
highest level for a Comex gold contract since $554 in March 1981.
February-delivery gold has gained $32.10, or 6.2%, from 2005's final price of $518.90.
February gold hit $546 overnight, surpassing the Dec. 12 peak of $544.50, pulled back on profit-taking, and
then raced ahead again. Traders suggested the temporary pullback was seen as a buying opportunity.
"It took out a prior high on the chart, and that is bullish," said Tom Boustead, metals analyst with Man
Financial. "It was the technicals after making a new high, and more fund money was coming in."
Otherwise, contacts said, the rise occurred despite moves in other markets that normally might be bearish for
gold -- weakness in crude and the euro. Analysts cited some background factors helping gold.
"It may be getting some lift from geopolitical concerns," Mr. Boustead said. He cited the health problems of
Israeli Prime Minister Ariel Sharon, which has caused worries about Israel's leadership.
There are concerns about Iran's nuclear program with some of the hawkish comments by that country's
president, added Dan Vaught, futures analyst with A.G. Edwards. "You have that in the background," he said.
"But I tend to think today's strength in the precious metals, gold in particular, is once again reflecting a strong
influx of capital into the commodities, particularly into index hedge funds and other methods people are using
to combat inflation."
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: A selloff in heating oil and gasoline spilled over into crude-oil futures amid expectations of a rise
in petroleum-production inventories spurred by mild weather in the Northeast. The February contract on the
New York Mercantile Exchange settled at $63.50 a barrel, down 71 cents.
SOYBEANS: Futures on the Chicago Board of Trade fell, pressured by unexpected rains in South America
over the weekend after hot and dry weather during the growing season. January slid 16.5 cents to $5.84 a
bushel.
ZINC: Prices on the London Metal Exchange hit a 16-year high as Grupo Mexico SA said it was too early to
say when it could reopen its main zinc refinery, which was shuttered Friday following an electrical overload.
Zinc prices hit their highest level since March 1989 on news of the refinery closure. Zinc for three-month
delivery rose $3 a metric ton, to $1,969. Grupo Mexico said it was trying to ascertain the cause of the
overload and evaluate how much damage was done at the plant in the central state of San Luis Potosi.

Market Numbers
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Market Numbers
373 words
10 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Market bulls gave a collective roar yesterday when the Dow Jones Industrial Average closed above 11,000
for the first time since June 2001. The 11,000 level doesn't mean all that much, except as a sign of our
economy's resilience and as one more symbol of investor confidence that has boosted stocks in a nice
January rally after a flat 2005.
Investors have reacted especially well since the release of last week's Federal Reserve minutes that many
bulls interpreted as a signal that the cycle of rising interest rates will soon end. And maybe they're right. If
they are, then stocks should have a very good year as market values catch up to the torrid increase in
corporate earnings. Other signs of steady economic growth have also continued, including job creation
symbolized by another reduction in the jobless rate to 4.9.% in December.
On the other hand, there's another market number from yesterday: $550. That was the price reached for a
time by gold, which has also surged since the release of the Fed minutes. Commodity prices in general have
also hit new highs, while the dollar's value relative to yen and euros has notably dipped. It isn't popular to say
this on Wall Street -- which hates rising interest rates -- but these price moves would appear to signal an
increase in inflationary expectations. If that's what they mean, then the Fed may not be as close to finished in
its interest-rate cycle as Wall Street hopes.
Our point isn't to join the economic boo birds. We've been writing for years that the power of the current
economic expansion has been underestimated, often willfully so for political reasons. But many of the same
folks who have misjudged the economy's strength have also been underestimating the degree to which Alan
Greenspan's Fed has flooded the world with dollars.
It's only human nature to want to believe that the Fed's maestro has called it exactly right, allowing his
successor to stop raising rates soon. But we'd sure feel better if price signals didn't soar every time the Fed
hinted at an end to monetary discipline.

Dow Industrials Keep Hot Start, Near 11000 Mark --- Clearing Focus on Fed's Stance, Positive Jobs Data Are Drivers; An Auspicious Run for S&...
Friday's Markets
Dow Industrials Keep Hot Start, Near 11000 Mark --- Clearing Focus on Fed's Stance, Positive Jobs
Data Are Drivers; An Auspicious Run for S&P 500
By Peter A. McKay
871 words
7 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ALMOST.
Helped by mostly beneficent jobs data and a clearer interest-rate outlook, stocks rallied for a fourth straight
day on Friday, putting the Dow Jones Industrial Average within spitting distance of 11000, a level the
blue-chip index hasn't closed above since the summer of 2001.
The gains put the index at a fresh 4 1/2-year high, joining other major indexes at that level.
The Dow's trading in the first week of 2006 looked and felt different than much of last year, when stocks
mostly traded sideways. The Dow ended at 10959.31, up 77.16 points, or 0.71%, and 2.3% on the week,
compared with a 0.6% decline for all of 2005.
The Nasdaq Composite Index and Standard &Poor's 500-stock index both gained 3% for the week,
compared with last year's overall gains of 1.4% and 3% respectively.
On Friday, the Nasdaq gained 1.3%, or 28.75 points, to close at 2305.62 The S&P 500 gained nearly 1%, or
11.97 points, to finish at 1285.45.
The week's rally marked the first time since 1988 that the S&P 500 rose in the first four days of the year
consecutively, according to data from Rabo Securities USA.
Meanwhile, the dollar broke two consecutive weeks of gains, and the 10-year Treasury note ended the week
with a yield of 4.377%, down almost 0.2 percentage point on the week.
In years past, a strong start to the New Year's stock trading bode well for the stock market -- with a notable
exception: On the six occasions over the last 50 years when the S&P 500 has posted an early four-day
winning streak, it has gone on to a 13.2% annual gain, on average. However, one of those years was 1987,
when the market crashed in October but still eked out a 2% annual rise.
The Labor Department Friday reported that 108,000 nonfarm jobs were created in December. That was only
about half the gain expected by economists, but Labor also sharply revised upward its November snapshot of
the nation's payrolls, saying 305,000 jobs were created that month instead of the original reading of 215,000
jobs. The unemployment rate fell to 4.9% in December, from 5%.
Economists and analysts characterized the jobs data as a mixed blessing. The numbers appeared to support
the idea that the economy isn't experiencing runaway growth -- or price pressures -- and thus may allow the
Federal Reserve to halt its pattern of interest-rate hikes following its meeting Jan. 31.
"This kind of jobs report is not the sign of an economy that is inflationary," said economist James Glassman
of J.P. Morgan Chase &Co.
Among the Dow's 30 component companies, International Business Machines and Exxon Mobil both rose
sharply. The computer maker's shares rose 3%, or $2.45, to $84.95 each, up 2.8% on the week, after IBM
announced it will freeze its U.S. pension plan in 2008. Exxon Mobil was helped by a rally in crude-oil prices.
The company's shares gained 2%, or $1.15, to finish at $59.43 each, up 5.8% on the week.
Among other market-moving factors, gold continued to surge, helped by expectations of a weaker dollar. The
grave illness of Israeli Prime Minister Ariel Sharon also pushed up gold, which is often used as an investor
haven during times of geopolitical uncertainty, said Richard W. Radez, vice president of precious metals at
the money-management firm David A. Noyes &Co.

--- Page 112 ---
Page 112 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Gold futures leapt for the second week, up $22.60 a troy ounce, or 4.4%, to $539.70 for the week on the
Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Gold prices have more than doubled since early 2001, when analysts say investors began shopping more
aggressively for alternatives to U.S. equities, including emerging-market stocks and commodities. "The focus
has changed regarding gold," said Mr. Radez. "People are realizing they have to diversify."
Outside the U.S., stocks rose in dollar terms, including another gain in Japanese stocks, putting the Nikkei
Stock Average at a fresh five-year high. The Dow Jones World Stock Index rose just under 1%, or 2.37
points, to 243.81. Excluding U.S. stocks, the index rose the same percentage amount, or 2.01 points, to
207.55.
In major U.S. market action Friday:
Stocks rose. On the Big Board, where 1.77 billion shares changed hands, 2,488 stocks advanced and 844
declined.
Bond prices fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 6/32, or $1.88 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield up
to 4.377%. The 30-year bond was down 10/32 to yield 4.563%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 114.42 yen, down from 115.89, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.2154 from $1.2104.

Stocks Edge Up, Aided by Rate Hopes
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Edge Up, Aided by Rate Hopes
By E.S. Browning
507 words
6 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE NEW YEAR'S RALLY survived for a third day, but just barely, as hopes for an end to Federal Reserve
interest-rate increases offset disappointing retail-sales news.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks are among the current rally's leaders, joined
the Standard &Poor's 500-stock Index at a 4 1/2-year high. The Dow Jones Industrial Average edged up
0.02%, or 2 points, to 10882.15. It is up 1.5% for 2006 but still short of its own 4 1/2-year high of 10940.55, hit
in March.
Minutes from the Fed's December meeting, released Tuesday, suggested that the central bank could stop
pushing up short-term interest rates early this year. The prospect that rate increases could end before rates
get too high fueled hopes for continued economic strength. The rally's biggest gains are coming in stocks that
are most sensitive to economic growth.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.59%, or 13.41 points, to 2276.87, up 3.2% in 2006. The S&P 500
advanced 0.02 points, virtually unchanged in percentage terms, to 1273.48, up 2% for 2006.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index hit another record, rising 0.37%, or 2.52 points, to 691.77. Foreign stocks
also rose, and the Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, hit its own record, up 0.14%, or 0.29
point, at 205.54.
Now investors are focused on the December employment figures due today. The Fed is said to look at those
data for indications of possible wage-driven inflation. Investors hope for a balanced report, strong enough to
suggest economic growth but not so strong as to suggest that the Fed will want to fight inflation with many
more rate increases.
"The clear focus is going to be on the employment report," said Todd Clark, director of stock trading at San
Francisco brokerage and money-management firm Nollenberger Capital Partners.
Retailers announced modest December sales gains. Wal-Mart Stores said its sales for the month rose less
than in any December since 2000 and that fourth-quarter profit would be at the low end of its forecast. But a
measure of December service-sector activity was strong.
Gold and oil, which had been rising sharply, both gave back some of their recent gains. Natural-gas prices
continued to fall.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.79 billion shares traded, 1,855 stocks rose and 1,506 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or 63 cents, for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.354%. The 30-year bond was down 3/32 to yield 4.544%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 115.89 yen, down from 115.97, while the euro fell
against the dollar to $1.2104 from $1.2127.

Business and Finance
What's News
Business and Finance
511 words
5 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal

A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BLACKSTONE AND H-P are exploring a possible buyout of Computer Sciences, as private-equity buyers
begin to sweep through the computer outsourcing business. A possible price remains unclear, but CSC has
hoped to attract an offer of $65 a share, or $12 billion. Meanwhile, a group including Blackstone could unveil
a deal for Affiliated Computer Services soon.
---
Computer Associates plans to announce that it has agreed to buy venture-capital-backed software maker
Wily for $375 million.
---
GM and Ford lost market share in December, and executives warned that 2006 sales may show little or no
growth from 2005.
Demand for U.S. factory goods rose 2.5% in November, boosted by orders for civilian airplanes.
---
The SEC publicly laid out a set of guidelines to help determine whether and how much to fine companies for
financial fraud.
---
Stocks rallied on optimism about Fed rate plans. The Dow industrials rose 32.74 to 10880.15; the S&P 500
hit a 4 1/2-year high.
---
Private-equity firms are paying themselves lavish dividends and fees from the companies they acquire,
financed by new debt.
---
Google plans an upgrade of its video-search service in partnership with major content providers including
CBS and the NBA.
---
ABN Amro's mortgage unit agreed to a $41.3 million settlement with U.S. regulators for falsifying
mortgage-loan documents.
---
A Shell unit and top oil trader were fined a combined $300,000 for executing a series of bogus oil-futures
trades on Nymex.
---
Morgan Stanley fired four staffers in its institutional-stock unit after they accompanied clients to an
adult-entertainment club.

Aetna said John Rowe will step down as CEO next month and will be succeeded by the health insurer's

Russia and Ukraine reached a compromise in a dispute over natural-gas prices that they said will secure

AT&T has started offering its Internet-based television service to customers in San Antonio.

Stock Rally Extends to a Second Day On Continued Reaction to Fed Remarks
Wednesday's Markets
Stock Rally Extends to a Second Day On Continued Reaction to Fed Remarks
By E.S. Browning
529 words
5 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE NEW YEAR'S RALLY extended into a second day as optimism about the Federal Reserve's plans for
interest rates helped push the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index to a 4 1/2-year high.
Investors continued to react to Tuesday's release of minutes from the Fed's December meeting, which
suggested that the Fed might stop raising U.S. rates before long.
After a gain of 129.91 points Tuesday, the Dow Jones Industrial Average yesterday added 32.74 points, or
0.30%, finishing at 10880.15. It is up 1.5% in the year's first two days, still below its 4 1/2-year high of
10940.55 hit in March.
The S&P 500 rose 0.37%, or 4.66 points, to 1273.46. It is up 2% this year, its highest level since the first half
of 2001. Foreign stocks also surged. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, hit a record
205.25, up 1.79% yesterday.
The Dow Jones Wilshire 5000, which includes almost all companies based in the U.S., finished at a five-year
high of 12773.49.
"It is encouraging on the heels of what happened Tuesday," said Bob Basel, senior trader at Citigroup's New
York brokerage arm. But he said investors remain nervous ahead of tomorrow's report on December
employment levels, which the Fed is thought to watch closely.
Stocks appeared to get a boost from the annual influx of new money into retirement accounts at the year's
opening. Trading was heavy. Investors also got a report showing November factory orders rising.
Bond prices rose, sending yields lower, reacting to the hopes for an end to Fed rate increases.
There were continuing signs of uncertainty, however. The prospect of an end to U.S. interest-rate increases
sent the dollar to its sharpest two-day percentage decline against the euro in two years, down 2.5% so far this
year.
Gold, an investment often sought in times of uncertainty, rose $3.20 to $533.90 an ounce, its highest New
York finish in almost 25 years.
Crude-oil futures rose 28 cents to $63.42 a barrel, the fifth consecutive gain and the highest finish in more
than two months. Crude is up 3.9% in the year's first two trading days. Natural gas, however, continued its
slide, down 9.2% in 2006.
The Fed minutes helped stocks shake off those worries. The Nasdaq Composite Index, filled with technology
stocks, advanced 0.88%, or 19.72 points, to 2263.46, up 2.6% in 2006.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks and bonds advanced. The 10-year Treasury note rose 7/32, or $2.19 for each $1,000 invested,
pushing the yield down to 4.348%. The 30-year bond was up 11/32 to yield 4.540%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 115.97 yen, down from 116.23, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.2127 from $1.2014.

Common Sense: Muted '05 Showing Could Offer Rewards In Market This Year
Personal Finance
Common Sense: Muted '05 Showing Could Offer Rewards In Market This Year
By James B. Stewart SmartMoney
867 words
4 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
D3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THERE WASN'T MUCH to celebrate in the markets this New Year's Eve. The major stock averages for 2005
were basically flat, and the yield curve inverted during the last week of the year, a harbinger of recession.
So let's start 2006 by sweeping away the end-of-year gloom. Anyone can make money in a big, broad-based
rally. It's times like just-finished 2005, and maybe the coming year, that reward intelligent, disciplined
risk-taking.
For Common Sense, 2005 was a banner year, with this column's key themes -- natural resources,
emerging-market equities, transports and Internet stocks -- performing better than I anticipated. I've often said
that it doesn't take a multitude of good ideas to outperform the averages. If you get the big ideas right,
stock-picking will fall into place.
The biggest idea is knowing when to commit capital to stocks and when to pull it out. As a year that ended
with the averages about where they began, 2005 offered few broad opportunities for market timing. In an
important calls of the year, I beseeched readers to buy stocks just before the S&P 500-stock index hit a low
for the year on April 20, as did the Nasdaq Composite a week later.
There were no broad selling opportunities, though I signaled numerous occasions when I found it prudent to
sell individual stocks. These included my premature advice to reduce energy holdings (though oil stocks did
decline from their peaks) as well as my decision to sell a small part of my Google holdings when the stock hit
$300. I reinstituted my Google "buy" recommendation after strong earnings pushed the stock to $335; now it
has surpassed $400.
I have been a consistent believer in the Internet Big Three -- Google, eBay and Yahoo -- and 2005 was no
exception. After extolling the category again on at the end of July, I embarked on an Internet-options
experiment, selling eBay calls and using the proceeds to buy Yahoo calls. Both were trading at about the
same price, just above $2. The final verdict won't be known for a few weeks, when the options expire, but so
far the Yahoo calls have far surpassed the eBay calls, with the January Yahoo calls at $4.70 and the eBay
calls at 90 cents when I checked recently. My hope is that the eBay calls expire worthless, meaning I'll keep
my shares, and Yahoo will hold its gains. (A call is an option to buy a security at a specific price, usually
above the current price.)
This column may have been early in urging investors to reduce their exposure to oil stocks, but it did
recommend investing the proceeds in other commodities and natural resources as an inflation hedge. I
bought BHP Billiton, Rio Tinto and Companhia Vale do Rio Doce. The CRB Index, a broad measure of the
performance of commodities compiled by the Commodity Research Bureau, subsequently hit an all-time high
of 337 in September and was only slightly lower, at 332, by year's end. Gold, the traditional inflation hedge,
topped $500 an ounce. BHP went from $26 in April to about $33 on Dec. 30; Rio Tinto from about $127 to
$183; and Rio Doce from about $28 to $41 in the same period.
More recently, in October, I urged investors to anticipate lower oil prices by buying transports, especially
FedEx and United Parcel Service. Since then the iShares Dow Jones Transportation Average Index, an
exchange-traded fund, has jumped from $66 to $75, a 14% gain in just three months. FDX shares went from
$86 to $103 and UPS from $68 to $75 in that same period.
One of my favorite calls of the year was the recommendation to buy Whole Foods Market in August. The
stock was added to the S&P 500 in December, giving it an immediate boost. The stock also split 2-for-1,
always a welcome sign of management confidence, even if you still own the same slice of the company as
before. Adjusted for the split, the shares went from $66 at the time of my recommendation to $77 at the end
of the year.

--- Page 123 ---
Page 123 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Common Sense isn't infallible. I sold my Wal-Mart Stores options at a loss in December. I find the case for
Wal-Mart compelling, and yet the results haven't been there. Just this week Wal-Mart indicated that holiday
sales were likely to be near the low end of its guidance.
Perhaps my most frustrating investment of the year has been Time Warner, which has barely budged despite
Google's purchase of a 5% stake in AOL for $1 billion. This values AOL at $20 billion, with the distinct
possibility of an IPO in the not-too-distant future. I'm still holding on.
---
James B. Stewart is a columnist for SmartMoney magazine and SmartMoney.com. Unlike Dow Jones staff
reporters, he may have positions in the stocks he writes about. For his past columns, see:
www.smartmoney.com/wsj_common .

Dow Industrials Kick Off 2006 With 1.2% Gain --- Investors Interpret Fed Minutes As Sign Bank Won't Raise Interest Rates Much Further
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Kick Off 2006 With 1.2% Gain --- Investors Interpret Fed Minutes As Sign Bank Won't
Raise Interest Rates Much Further
By Aaron Lucchetti
749 words
4 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average was up 3.1% in 2004. A chart that accompanied the Tuesday's Markets
column in yesterday's Money &Investing section incorrectly said that change was 3.2%.
(WSJ Jan. 5, 2006)
STOCKS JUMPED to solid gains in the first trading day of 2006, as investors interpreted the minutes of the
Federal Reserve's December meeting as a signal that interest-rate increases would cease soon.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 129.91 points, or 1.2%, to 10847.41. The broad Standard &Poor's
500-stock index rose 20.51, or 1.6%, to 1268.80 and the Nasdaq Composite Index charged ahead 38.42, or
1.7%, to 2243.74.
Investors took hope that the latest Fed commentary meant the central bank's open-market committee
perhaps will stop raising interest rates after one more increase this month. Currently, the Fed's short-term
interest-rate target is 4.25%, up from a recent low of 1% in 2004.
Bulls looked to a line in the Fed minutes saying that most committee members felt "the number of additional
firming steps required probably would not be large."
A stop, or even a pause, in raising short-term rates could make stocks more attractive relative to fixed-rate
investments such as bonds, while giving the economy a nudge by making borrowing and spending less costly
for consumers and corporations.
"There's a light at the end of the tunnel now," said Chip Dickson, chief U.S. strategist for Lehman Brothers.
Many investors watch the first trading days of the year as a harbinger of what is to come. Of course, market
analysts warn that such logic is a rough guide at best and that it is even rougher on the first day than after the
first week or month.
Still, since World War II, the Dow industrial average has proved slightly more likely to gain ground in years of
a successful first day. In the 32 years since 1945 that the Dow has risen on day one, it ended the year higher
78% of the time, compared with 69% of the time overall.
Until 2 p.m. EST, when the Fed's minutes were released, stocks traded in a tight range, giving back early
gains of about 35 points.
"The message really energized the market," said Theodore Weisberg, a floor broker at the New York Stock
Exchange for Seaport Securities. Before the announcement, investors "were still wiping the cobwebs out of
their eyes."
Stocks in the blue-chip average rose about 150 points after 2 p.m. The average gave back only about 15
points of its gains in the last half hour of trading.
The Dow's gains yesterday, while impressive, didn't come close to its performance on Jan. 2, 2003, when the
index rose 265.89 points, or 3.2%. Yesterday's opening day was the second best since 1945 in point terms
but only seventh best in percentage terms.

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Jack Ablin, chief investment officer at Harris Private Bank noted that the dollar fell yesterday because of the
growing view that rate increases will end soon. "The average [stock] investor was rather concerned the Fed
would go too far," he said.
Oil futures rose $2.10, or 3.4%, to $63.14 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange, while gold rallied
$13.60, or 2.6%, to $530.70 an ounce on the Comex division of the Nymex.
Outside the U.S., stocks rose in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index gained 4.11 points, or 1.8%,
to 238.47. Excluding U.S. stocks, the index advanced 3.7 points, or 1.9%, to 201.65.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.9 billion shares traded, 2,559 stocks rose and 841
fell.
Bonds were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note rose 5/32, or $1.56 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 4.375%, as prices move inversely to yields. The 30-year bond was down 11/32 to yield 4.56%
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency was down at 116.23 yen from 117.92. The euro rose
against the dollar to $1.2014 from $1.1826.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2005 --- Commodities Mark High Note --- Global Economic Growth Lifts Metal, Energy Prices; End of Rally...
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2005 --- Commodities Mark High Note --- Global Economic
Growth Lifts Metal, Energy Prices; End of Rally Could be Near
By Peter A. McKay
1,903 words
3 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
R9
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
COMMODITIES rallied strongly in 2005, led by energy and metals.
Agricultural products were a mixed bag. Still, widely followed commodity indexes registered a fourth-straight
year of increases, helped by strong demand in the U.S., Asia and other regions where expanding economies
required a variety of raw materials to churn out finished goods for consumers.
The Dow Jones-AIG Index of 20 commodities ended at 171.15, up 17.5%, or 25.54 points. Another
broad-based measure, the Reuters/Jefferies CRB Index, rose 16.9%, or 47.93 points, to finish at 331.83,
close to a record.
Still, the natural-resources sector also faced its share of woes. Along with the Federal Reserve's continued
interest-rate increases, last year's modest rallies in the stock market and the U.S. dollar were the sort of
trends that usually spell gloom for commodity prices.
A stronger U.S. currency, in particular, usually weighs down prices, because most commodities are traded
globally in dollar terms. When the dollar strengthens, it takes fewer dollars to buy the same amount of
commodities. The dollar strengthened 4.7% last year, according to a J.P. Morgan trade-weighted measure of
its value against a basket of other currencies.
Analysts say that might have held commodities' gains in check, but the sector's performance still was
impressive, with the Dow Jones-AIG and the Reuters/Jefferies CRB both rising at an even faster pace than in
2004.
In the new year, many analysts and traders say robust physical demand may keep the rally going, but
perhaps it would be as broad-based. For instance, many investing professionals expect lower, or perhaps flat,
crude-oil prices through the first half of the year. Gold, which is trading near an 18-year high, may be ready
for a pullback. But industrial metals, such as copper, still should be in heavy demand for electrical wiring in
new homes, computer parts and other uses.
In other words, a question for commodity markets throughout 2005 still lingers: When will the long-running
rally finally reverse? Many Wall Street veterans say that day still hasn't come. However, they are increasingly
cautious -- and trading accordingly -- placing financial bets that can be easily unwound to minimize losses
whenever the party does end.
"We're telling our investors that we're still pretty bullish on the commodity cycle, with the caveat that when
interest rates get to the point that they're greater than inflation, people will start to switch back to financial
assets and out of hard assets," says David P. Prokupek, chief executive of asset-management firm Geronimo
Partners, in Denver. He says he expects a rise of 6% to 8% in broad indexes like the CRB.
Along with the continued rise in prices, the commodities and futures industry itself continued to boom, marked
by record trading volume, increased participation by hedge funds and a march by member-run exchanges to
restructure as corporations. Notably, the CBOT Holdings Inc., parent of the Chicago Board of Trade, did an
initial public offering of its own shares and now has a market capitalization around $5.1 billion. Also, the New
York Mercantile Exchange is looking to sell a minority stake to private-sector investors, with an eye toward
doing an IPO later.
Meanwhile, one of the futures industry's biggest brokerage houses, Refco Inc., imploded in October following
the revelation that its then-Chairman and Chief Executive Phillip R. Bennett had moved about $430 million in
debts, including customer-trading losses, to a firm he controlled. The disclosure prompted a customer exodus
akin to a run on a bank. Mr. Bennett was arrested on federal securities-fraud charges, while Refco and its
main trading units filed for bankruptcy-court protection.

--- Page 129 ---
Page 129 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In regulatory news, the House of Representatives recently approved a revised charter for the Commodity
Futures Trading Commission, the industry's head regulator, including new rules covering the markets for
natural-gas and currency contracts. However, that legislation still must be reconciled with the Senate's
version.
Here is a rundown of the major commodity markets in 2005:
Energy
Crude oil continued to be the bellwether for the broader commodities market, helped throughout the year by
tight global output, compared with demand of around 83 million barrels a day.
Beginning in late August, Hurricane Katrina and other storms damaged refineries, drills and pipelines along
the Gulf Coast, pushing prices to records of more than $70 a barrel.
In the fourth quarter, rising inventories and relatively warm winter weather in much of the U.S. helped push
prices lower, erasing much of the earlier gains. Still, crude-oil futures finished the year at $61.04 a barrel, up
40.5%, or $17.59 on the Nymex.
In particular, many analysts believe robust storage and supply could keep prices in check -- albeit at high
levels -- in 2006.
"There's plenty of oil to meet the demand for now," says longtime energy investor T. Boone Pickens, who
expects highs around $65 a barrel this year -- a relatively slight rise compared with the roaring gains of the
past few years. For the early going of 2006, many, including Mr. Pickens, believe prices could first pull back
before recovering later in the year.
Other energy products followed crude oil's lead on Nymex. Heating-oil contracts soared 40.5%, or 49.83
cents, to $1.7280 a gallon. The prices of gasoline futures contracts -- a measure of wholesale prices, not
including taxes and other markups -- surged 57.1%, or 62.13 cents, to end at $1.71 a gallon.
Natural-gas futures rose even more markedly, jumping 82.6%, or $5.076, to $11.225 a million British thermal
units. In Katrina's aftermath, traders worried about winter shortages of the fuel, and their buying pushed the
price to records around $15 a million BTUs.
Those worries have since subsided, but analysts say natural gas remains subject to unique circumstances,
since it can be transported only via pipelines to and from fixed delivery points. By contrast, liquid fuels can be
transported via tankers or trucks wherever they are needed, from wherever they can be obtained at the
lowest cost.
Some market watchers, including outgoing Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan, have advocated
building more facilities to import liquefied natural gas, thus opening the market to freer competition from
overseas suppliers who might push prices lower. Such projects remain a matter of debate among opponents
concerned about potential environmental harm or terrorist attacks on such terminals.
Metals
Perhaps the commodity most notable for bucking its usual relationship with the dollar in 2005 was gold,
which surged to more than $500 a troy ounce for the first time since 1987. The yellow metal usually is
considered an alternative, widely accepted store of value when people lose faith in paper money, so it tends
to move in the opposite direction from the dollar.
Analysts say a few factors helped the metal rise despite last year's strengthening dollar. "We've had strong
physical demand" for jewelry and other uses of the metal, says fund manager Joe Foster of Van Eck Global.
"But we will eventually get back to the traditional relationship with the dollar."
Mr. Foster and other analysts say the late-year rally was helped by expectations that the Federal Reserve
soon will cease its pattern of interest-rate increases, which effectively take dollars out of circulation and drive
up their value. In trader parlance, the gold market already has "priced in" the idea that the dollar will cease to
enjoy such support sometime in early 2006.
Of course, that also means gold may have little reason to rise once the scenario comes true, since it is
already reflected in prices. Hence, some analysts say gold is likely to turn in a less impressive performance
in the new year.
For 2005, gold futures rose 18.2%, or $79.60, to finish at $517.10 a troy ounce on Nymex's Comex division.
Other precious metals followed suit. Silver futures climbed 29.6%, or $2.013, to $8.82 a troy ounce. Platinum
contracts advanced 12.7%, or $109.30, to end at $973 a troy ounce on the Comex.

--- Page 130 ---
Page 130 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Industrial-metals prices also were strong, led by the bellwether copper contract, which leapt 45.4%, or 67.45
cents, to $2.1615 a pound. Palladium, which is a component of the emissions-reducing car parts known as
catalytic converters, rose 41.2%, or $76.25, to end at $261.50 a troy ounce on Comex.
In London trading, metals such as zinc, tin and lead racked up big gains, helped by continuing demand from
China's emergence as a major consumer for electronics and other products.
Analysts say a strong U.S. housing market also buoyed prices -- a trend that seems poised to continue.
"There are pockets of strength left in the commodity markets, and the metals seem to be one of them," says
Anton Pil, a managing director at J.P. Morgan Private Bank.
Agriculture
Grain prices rebounded nicely, after a laggard performance in 2004. Speculators have bid prices higher
lately, expecting heavy buying in the new year from commodity funds that eventually will have to unwind
heavily bearish bets, known as short positions.
At the Chicago Board of Trade, soybean futures finished at $6.020 a bushel, up 54.25 cents, or 9.9%. Wheat
contracts rose 10.3%, or 31.75 cents, to $3.3925 a bushel. Corn futures rose 5.4%, or 11 cents, to 2.1575 a
bushel.
Livestock prices were mixed at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange. Strong beef demand pushed live-cattle
futures up 2.9%, or 2.6 cents, to 92.45 cents a pound. While a glut of animals weighed down pork prices.
Live-hog futures fell 14.6%, or 11.125 cents, to 65.275 cents a pound. Pork bellies -- the part of a slaughtered
hog from which bacon is made -- fell 12.8%, or 12.1 cents, to 82.15 cents a pound.
Also at the CME, lumber futures were essentially flat, ending at $359 a 1,000 square board feet, up $2.60, or
0.7%.
Tropical Commodities
For a second straight year, storm damage to Florida's orange crop pushed up prices of juice futures
contracts. In 2004, a succession of storms crisscrossed the Sunshine State, but last year, it took just one
well-placed storm -- Hurricane Wilma.
Contracts on frozen orange juice soared nearly 45.4%, or 39.1 cents, to $1.252 a pound at the New York
Board of Trade.
Other commodities known as "tropical softs" were boosted by strong demand and, in some cases, bad
growing weather. Sugar soared 62.4%, or 5.64 cents, to 14.68 cents a pound. Cotton climbed 21%, or 9.42
cents, to 54.19 cents a pound at NYBOT.
Cocoa futures were an exception to the trend, falling 2.8%, or $43, at $1,504 a metric ton, a unit equal to
2,204.6 pounds. Coffee contracts rose 3.2%, or 3.35 cents, to $1.071 a pound. Analysts are forecasting a
coffee-supply shortfall relative to demand in 2006 -- a scenario that could lead to higher prices.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2005 --- Iraq Elections, Hurricanes &The Supreme Court
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2005 --- Iraq Elections, Hurricanes &The Supreme Court
By Frank McGuire, Michael Lucas and Gregory J. Corcoran
15,198 words
3 January 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
R13
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
The New England Journal of Medicine said that data on three patients who suffered heart attacks should
have been included in a study of the drug Vioxx that it published in November 2000. A page-one article on
Dec. 9, 2005, incorrectly reported that the New England Journal said the heart-attack incidents were
"excised" from the study, which was sponsored by Merck &Co. The Wall Street Journal has repeated the
error at least six times since Dec. 9 in various editions.
(WSJ March 14, 2006)
(END)
It was a year that began in the shadow of a natural catastrophe overseas, the 2004 Asian tsunami, and was
defined in the U.S. by another, Hurricane Katrina. The devastation wreaked havoc on the lives of millions, but
the aftermath was marked by harnessing the power of the Internet for charitable giving.
Iraq's citizens three times braved the threat of violence to participate in that country's first free and open
elections since Saddam Hussein began his reign, though continuing battles with insurgents and terrorists
meant the year was marred by horrendous violence, bombings, kidnappings and a near-constant intoning of
the U.S. war dead, which surpassed 2,000.
On Wall Street, it was the year of the big deal, Procter &Gamble bought Gillette in January for $57 billion,
while ConocoPhillips agreed to acquire Burlington Resources in a $35 billion deal. In between, U.S. deals
were held hostage by the flexing muscles of China, whose companies twice threatened to unwind agreed-to
transactions.
Politically, the court hung in the balance, the Supreme Court that is, as nomination fights for two seats
brought the contest between Democrats and Republicans to a fever pitch.
Here is a summary of the news events as chronicled on the front page of The Wall Street Journal. In most
cases, the dates reflect when the items were published.
JANUARY
3 The U.S. economy is expected to expand a modest but healthy 3.6% amid subdued inflation and only
slight rises in interest rates this year, according to a survey of economists. The forecast assumes oil prices
will stabilize or decline and that the weaker dollar will make U.S. businesses more competitive and help tame
the trade deficit.
4 Halliburton Co. completes a $5.1 billion asbestos-litigation settlement, enabling the company to bring
several units out of bankruptcy-law protection and either sell or spin off its KBR arm.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average falls 53.58 points to 10729.43 on the first day of 2005 trading. Crude-oil
prices fall 3.1% to $42.12 a barrel.
5 Cendant's former vice chairman, E. Kirk Shelton, is convicted of fraud, but jurors can't reach a decision on
charges against former Chairman Walter Forbes. In August, Mr. Shelton is sentenced to 10 years in prison
and ordered to pay $3.27 billion for his role in an accounting scandal that cost investors and Cendant more
than $3 billion. Shelton says he will appeal.
10 Mahmoud Abbas declares victory in voting that may advance Mideast peace. Exit polls have him winning
65% of ballots to succeed Yassir Arafat as Palestinian Authority president.

--- Page 132 ---
Page 132 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
12 President Bush names longtime prosecutor Michael Chertoff to take over Homeland Security. Mr. Chertoff
helped craft the Patriot Act and was the Senate Republicans' chief Whitewater counsel against the Clintons.
He is confirmed in February.
Nortel Networks Corp. unveils details of additional accounting errors involving billions of dollars and says 12
executives will return $8.6 million in bonuses they were paid based on erroneous earnings. The
telecommunications-equipment maker also says five directors, including its chairman, will resign.
14 General Motors Corp. forecasts lower 2005 earnings amid projections of a $1 billion rise in health-care
costs, a substantial loss in Europe and lower earnings at its GMAC financing arm. Meanwhile, GMAC said it
is mulling a restructuring. Days later, GM reports a 37% earnings decline, despite big gains from tax benefits.
17 Died: Charlie Bell, 44, who quit as CEO of McDonald's Corp. in November to battle colorectal cancer, in
Australia.
19 Condoleezza Rice defends Iraq policy and promises to repair frayed alliances. At a Senate hearing on her
confirmation as secretary of state, the national-security adviser denies she let loyalty to President Bush
override facts that argued against war. She says an exit timetable depends on how fast an Iraqi army is in
place. She is confirmed a week later.
20 AMR and Northwest post hefty losses and Southwest Airlines' profit falls 15%. A day later, Delta Air Lines
reports a $2.2 billion fourth-quarter loss, while Continental Airlines swings to a $206 million loss, its steepest
in nearly two years, and neither offers much hope of relief soon from high fuel prices and brutal fare
competition.
21 President Bush begins his second term vowing to confront tyranny everywhere. In his inaugural address,
the president encourages foes of political and economic oppression to rise against despotic regimes in the
world's "darkest corners," and proclaims, "When you stand for your liberty, we will stand with you." A Wall
Street Journal/NBC survey shows Bush's approval rating is 50%.
24 Died: Johnny Carson, 79, the king of late-night TV as host of "The Tonight Show" for 30 years, retiring in
1992, of emphysema in Los Angeles. . . . Rose Mary Woods, 87, Nixon secretary who said she was to blame
for part of a Watergate tape's 18 1/2-minute gap, in Alliance, Ohio.
25 Republicans name Social Security overhaul as their top legislative priority. AARP, which opposes the
president's plan and plans its own proposal, releases a poll showing deep public skepticism about private
Social Security accounts.
26 The Securities and Exchange Commission orders Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Morgan Stanley to pay
$40 million each in a settlement that shows how securities firms pumped up prices of initial public offerings of
stock.
28 Procter &Gamble Co. agrees to purchase Gillette Co. for about $54 billion in stock, moving the
consumer-products giant into the men's grooming business and strengthening its clout with retailers.
31 Iraqis vote in large numbers despite the threat of violence. Election officials estimate more than the
expected 57% of about 14 million eligible voters cast ballots. Minority Sunni participation is low. At least 44
people die in attacks at polls.
SBC Communications Inc. agrees to acquire AT&T Corp. in a $16 billion deal. SBC later says the success of
the deal relies on cost cuts that will slash 13,000 jobs and save at least $2 billion a year by 2008.
FEBRUARY
1 Exxon Mobil's net rises 27% to $8.42 billion, its biggest quarterly profit ever and, effectively, a record for a
U.S. publicly traded company.
Marsh &McLennan Cos. agrees to pay $850 million to settle civil-fraud charges of bid-rigging and steering
business to insurers who paid fees.
3 President Bush begins to bring his Social Security proposal into sharper focus. In his State of the Union
address, the president raises the prospect of "bankruptcy" unless the program is changed. He would leave
untouched the benefits of those now 55 or older. The speech begins with some Republicans waving
ink-dipped index fingers to honor Iraqi voters, and Bush says he wants to build on that success of Iraq
elections by advancing Mideast peace efforts.
Kofi Annan aide Benon Sevan put himself in "an irreconcilable conflict of interest," United Nations oil-for-food
investigator Paul Volcker writes in an op-ed article that calls the program "tainted." Interim findings released a
day later say "ethically improper" Iraq oil-for-food deals had "seriously undermined" the world body's integrity.

--- Page 133 ---
Page 133 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Annan begins disciplinary proceedings against Sevan and a second U.N. official, and says he will cooperate
in any criminal inquiry and waive immunity of the accused. Sevan's lawyer says his client "never took a
penny."
The Federal Reserve raises short-term interest rates a quarter point to 2.5%.
7 Merck &Co. records show an external committee charged with monitoring Vioxx's safety in a clinical trial
had early data suggesting users could be at increased risk of heart problems after taking the painkiller for as
little as four months. The records shed light on central issues for Merck in defending itself against a wave of
lawsuits accusing it of negligence.
Tobacco companies score a victory as a court tosses out the possibility of a $280 billion penalty sought by
the Justice Department for allegedly misleading the public about the danders of cigarettes. The Supreme
Court in October rejects a government appeal to hear the penalty case.
8 President Bush proposes a budget seeking domestic austerity not contemplated in years. The president
says the more than 150 programs he intends to eliminate or slash, from Amtrak to community police grants,
aren't "achieving results." Democrats say worthy goals would be sacrificed for tax cuts the president wants to
make permanent and promised deficit reduction is illusory because costs of war operations and the
president's Social Security plan aren't factored in.
Defrocked priest Paul Shanley is convicted of raping a boy at his Roman Catholic church in the 1980s, the
highest-profile case in the Boston clergy scandal. He is later sentenced to 12 to 15 years in prison.
9 A cease-fire is declared at a Middle East summit. Israeli and Palestinian leaders pledge to return to
diplomacy. Their statements pave the way for Israel's withdrawal from the Gaza Strip and parts of the West
Bank in the summer.
14 Shiites cement a dominant political role in Iraq's final vote tally. With their clergy-backed slate taking 48%
and the most seats, the various elements of the majority group Saddam Hussein long kept prostrate are
promising a disciplined unity to achieve their ends, which some fear may mean Islamic law. Kurdish parties
take 26%, and their bid for more autonomy and control of oil-rich Kirkuk scares Turkey.
MCI Inc., the No. 2 U.S. telecommunications company, agrees to be acquired by Verizon Communications
Inc. for $6.8 billion in cash, shares and dividends.
GM agrees to pay Fiat SpA of Italy $1.99 billion to end their alliance. Under the deal, GM can't be required to
buy the 90% of Fiat's auto unit it doesn't already own. A day later, Moody's Investor Service places a negative
outlook on GM's credit rating, due in part to the Fiat payment.
15 A Beirut bomb strikes ex-Premier Rafik al-Hariri's motorcade, killing him and nine others and ending a
period of relative Lebanon calm. Hariri quit last year to protest political meddling and occupation by Syria,
which is suspected in the attack. Damascus proclaims its innocence.
American International Group says it received subpoenas from Eliot Spitzer and the Securities and Exchange
Commission seeking information about the possible use of certain insurance products to manipulate
earnings. Deals with Berkshire Hathaway Inc.'s General Re unit in late 2000 or early 2001 are later said to be
drawing scrutiny to see if they were aimed at inflating AIG's reserves.
16 ChoicePoint Inc. alerts thousands of Californians to a potential for identity theft because files were hacked.
Some fear problems may be bigger. In March, the SEC and FTC launch inquiries into the matter. In
November, ChoicePoint says in a regulatory filing that it has sent out 17,000 additional notices.
17 Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan backs private Social Security accounts, with caution. The Fed chief's
eagerly anticipated views on Bush's keynote second-term project gives the president a boost. Greenspan tells
Congress the current system "is not working" but urged changes made in a "cautious, gradual way."
The National Hockey League calls off whatever is left of the lockout-frozen 2004-05 hockey season, a first for
any professional sport.
18 Congress passes a bill making it easier for defendants to move many class-action suits to federal courts.
The vote is a victory for the business lobby, which has campaigned to prevent plaintiffs' lawyers from
shopping among jurisdictions for big awards. Consumer and labor groups opposed the change.
President Bush chooses John Negroponte for the new job of national intelligence chief. The Iraq
ambassador's long experience in Washington bureaucracy may have commended him. The Sept. 11
commission had called for authority unified in one person to better fight U.S. enemies. He is confirmed in
April.

--- Page 134 ---
Page 134 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
22 Novartis AG agrees to pay $7.37 billion to buy 100% of Germany's Hexal AG and 68% of Eon Labs Inc. of
the U.S., both generic-drug makers, and will launch a nearly $1 billion tender offer to buy the remaining
31.9% of Eon that is publicly traded. The deal will transform Novartis into the top seller of generic drugs.
23 The Dow industrials slide 174.02 points, or 1.6%, on news South Korea's central bank plans to diversify
its foreign-exchange reserves, which traders take to mean a slowdown in purchases of dollar-denominated
securities. A day later, South Korea says there are no such plans.
New York says it has exhausted all efforts but is unable to identify the remains of 1,161 of the 2,749 people
killed in the Sept. 11, 2001, attacks there.
25 Qwest Communications International Inc. revises its offer for MCI, directly challenging Verizon's $6.75
billion takeover agreement. Qwest's latest offer of nearly $8 billion is identical in value to a bid already
rejected, but the timing for paying out the cash portion has been accelerated.
28 Federated Department Stores Inc. agrees to acquire rival May Department Stores Co. for about $11
billion, a deal that will combine the top two department-store companies and create a national chain of nearly
1,000 stores.
Wichita, Kan., police arrest the man they think is the BTK strangler after 31 years and 10 murders. The
suspect is a churchgoing city official. In June, Dennis Rader will plead guilty to the murders and gave details
of Kansas' "BTK" killings. In August, he receives 10 life sentences.
MARCH
1 Biogen Idec Inc. of Cambridge, Mass., and Elan Corp. of Ireland pull their promising new multiple-sclerosis
treatment Tysabri from the market following a patient's death. The suspension raises questions about the
Food and Drug Administration's accelerated drug-approval process.
2 Senate Majority Leader Bill Frist and House Majority Leader Tom DeLay concede congressional votes on
President Bush's Social Security plan may not be possible this year after surveying colleagues' reluctance.
4 Bank of America Corp. agrees to pay $460.5 million to settle a suit brought by WorldCom Inc. investors who
lost billions of dollars.
8 Boeing Co. ousts CEO Harry Stonecipher, citing his relationship with a female executive. The indiscretion
undercut the self-appointed ethics enforcer's credibility in restoring the aerospace titan's reputation amid a
sea of scandals. Chairman Lewis Platt will take on more responsibilities and help guide the search for a new
chief.
President Bush names a sharp U.N. critic as the U.S. envoy to the world body. The selection of arms-control
negotiator John Bolton shows the White House expects deep reforms after the oil-for-food scandal and won't
countenance any yielding of sovereignty to bodies such as the International Criminal Court. Critics worry it
signals an uncompromising U.S. stance on issues like Iran and North Korea.
9 Fannie Mae's regulator tells the mortgage company to fix "deficiencies" in accounting-ledger and
corporate-records controls. The move underscores the tightening grip of the Office of Federal Housing
Enterprise Overnight in the wake of an accounting scandal that emerged last year.
10 Reed Elsevier PLC says 32,000 personal files were accessed fraudulently from its LexisNexis
online-information service. In April, the company raises that number to 310,000, raising fresh concerns about
the data-collection industry's ability to guard against hackers amid a surge in identity-theft crimes.
14 Walt Disney Co.'s board names President Robert Iger to succeed Michael Eisner as CEO. Mr. Iger, Mr.
Eisner's hand-picked choice, will assume the post in October.
15 Maurice R. "Hank" Greenberg agrees to step down as American International Group's chief executive
amid mounting regulatory pressure over a transaction the insurer did with General Re. Martin Sullivan, who
has been vice chairman and co-chief operating officer, is named the new CEO. Mr. Greenberg is to stay on
as nonexecutive chairman, though he cedes that title two weeks later. AIG also names a new chief financial
officer and says it will delay filing its 2004 10K statement with the SEC.
16 Bernard Ebbers is convicted of participating in the largest accounting fraud in U.S. history. The jury finds
the former WorldCom CEO guilty of all nine counts related to fraud at the telecommunications company that
topped $11 billion. In July, Ebbers agrees to transfer nearly all his assets to a trust for WorldCom
shareholders to settle a class-action lawsuit. In mid-July, he is sentenced to 25 years in prison, the harshest
sentence yet handed down to an executive convicted in the recent wave of corporate scandals. He appeals
his conviction in September.

--- Page 135 ---
Page 135 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The SEC files a civil lawsuit against Qwest ex-CEO Joseph Nacchio, alleging he and top aides engaged in
financial fraud. His lawyer later says, "Mr. Nacchio has consistently stated, he did nothing wrong and did not
instruct anyone else to do anything wrong during his tenure at Qwest." In December, he is indicted on 42
federal insider-trading charges.
17 GM slashes its earnings estimates and said its CEO's profit strategy needs an overhaul. The warning
reflects the auto maker's falling market share, unsold inventory, increased foreign competition and heavy
health-care and pension costs. GM shares tumble 14% and its bonds are battered as Standard &Poor's and
Moody's move closer to cutting their ratings to junk status.
J.P. Morgan settles a suit by WorldCom investors for $2 billion, bringing the total amount recovered in the
case against Wall Street firms to just over $6 billion.
18 President Bush names Rep. Rob Portman, an Ohio Republican with close ties to the White House, to be
U.S. trade representative.
21 Internet conglomerate IAC/InterActiveCorp buys Web-search service Ask Jeeves Inc. for around $2 billion
in stock. The move by Barry Diller's electronic-commerce company promises to roil the Internet's most
competitive arena amid a search-services battle among Google, Yahoo and Microsoft.
Congress takes extraordinary action in a Florida right-to-life fight over Terri Schiavo. Congressional panels
vote to subpoena the brain-damaged woman to try to forestall the lethal step. A judge rebuffs them and
Congress passes a bill to force a federal-court review of her case. Bush flies in from Texas to sign it. She dies
in early April.
Died: John DeLorean, 80, maverick whose futuristic car venture crashed spectacularly amid a failed drug
prosecution, in Summit, N.J.
22 AIG fires two top executives after they signal they would invoke their Fifth Amendment rights against
possible self-incrimination as regulators investigate whether the insurance titan manipulated its books to
mislead investors. AIG and a Berkshire unit last year unwound at least half of a transaction that is being
probed, raising questions whether the deal had any economic purpose.
A 16-year-old student opens fire at the Red Lake high school on a Minnesota Indian reservation, killing seven
and wounding 14 before shooting himself in the worst such U.S. rampage since the one at Columbine in
1999. Earlier, he killed his policeman grandfather and a girlfriend at their home.
23 The Fed nudges interest rates higher, raising its target for the federal-funds rate to 2.75%. In April,
minutes to the meeting show the Fed expects to raise rates higher than it previously believed would be
necessary and possibly at a faster pace, with officials citing evidence of increased inflation pressure and
more durable economic expansion.
24 An explosion wrecks part of a BP refinery in Texas, killing 15 and injuring more than 100. The Occupation
Safety and Health Administration had fined the facility twice recently, last year for a mishap that left two dead.
In October, BP, which earlier said it accepted responsibility for the blast, agrees to pay OSHA a $21.4 million
fine over safety violations.
25 The IRS says it has collected $3.2 billion from 1,165 participants in a tax shelter called "Son of BOSS," its
biggest such case ever.
29 Morgan Stanley CEO Philip Purcell shakes up the securities firm's top management, naming Stephen
Crawford and Zoe Cruz as co-presidents, succeeding Stephan Newhouse. The move in part triggers an
intense assault from eight former Morgan Stanley investment bankers who aim to oust Purcell because they
believe he has damaged a culture they helped to create.
SunGard Data Systems Inc. is to be acquired for $10.8 billion and the assumption of debt by a consortium led
by Silver Lake Partners, in what would be the biggest-ever technology-sector buyout by private-equity firms.
30 Hewlett-Packard picks NCR chief Mark Hurd to succeed Carly Fiorina as CEO.
APRIL
1 The Dow industrials ended the first quarter down 2.6%, marking the third straight year blue chips started
with a losing quarter.
Pope John Paul II takes a turn for the worse, as the Vatican reports he has a high fever and is being given
antibiotics to fight urinary infection. He dies a day later, on Saturday, at age 84, ending a 26-year pontificate
that both invigorated and was buffetted by the social currents of his time.

--- Page 136 ---
Page 136 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
4 Died: Frank Perdue, 84, poultry tycoon who served as his own TV pitchman, in Salisbury, Md.
5 ChevronTexaco Corp. agrees to buy Unocal Corp. in a cash-and-stock deal valued at $16.8 billion, the
largest oil-sector deal since 2001.
Bush awards the first Medal of Honor of the Iraq campaign, citing the heroism of Army Sgt. Paul Ray Smith,
who died defending his men near Baghdad airport April 4, 2003.
6 Pfizer Inc. scales back earnings estimates, announces a sweeping restructuring and says it will seek cost
cuts of $4 billion. The drug maker says profit in 2005 will be about $8.6 billion, down from $11.4 billion in
2004. Pfizer, which has been hurt by pricing pressures amid a wave of patent expirations, now faces a further
challenge from the new Medicare drug benefit.
8 Time Warner, Comcast and Adelphia creditors reach an agreement in principle for the two cable giants to
acquire Adelphia, which collapsed in the wake of an accounting and corporate-looting scandal, in a cash-
and-stock deal valued at slightly more than $17.6 billion.
13 Former AIG chief Greenberg invokes the Fifth Amendment at his deposition. Separately, he gives his wife
$2.2 billion of AIG shares three days before stepping down as CEO, though he later will unwind that gift.
14 Disappointing March retail sales, slowing job growth and a drop in consumer confidence show that the
U.S. economy appears to have lost steam toward the end of the first quarter. The Dow industrials fall 104
points.
Morgan Stanley names two acting co-heads of investment banking after its two top bankers, Joseph Perella
and Terry Meguid, resign.
15 The House overwhelmingly approves a new bankruptcy bill, removing the last hurdle to enacting a major
overhaul that will make it more difficult for consumers to walk away from their debts.
The FDA ephedra ban is ruled illegal by a federal judge in Salt Lake City, throwing into question whether the
agency has the legal tools for taking such herbal diet pills or any other supplement off the market.
18 Adobe Inc. plans to buy Macromedia Inc. for $3.4 billion in stock, in a deal that would create a software
company with broad ability to distribute media to PCs, computers, cellphones and hand-held devices. The
deal is the biggest software transaction since 2004's Symantec-Veritas merger.
19 The National Football League says "Monday Night Football" will move from ABC to ESPN, which will pay a
record $1.1 billion a year for the rights.
20 Joseph Ratzinger is named the 265th pope, Benedict XVI, on the fourth round of ballots on a second day
of deliberations by the College of Cardinals. The 78-year-old is the first German pope in centuries and an
adherent to what he sees as the absolute truths upon which the Catholic Church was built.
GM reports a $1.1 billion loss, its worst in more than a dozen years, and declines to reaffirm its earnings
forecast for the rest of 2005.
The Supreme Court rules investors who allege securities losses due to company misrepresentations must
show the lies were to blame.
21 The New York Stock Exchange plans to acquire electronic-trading concern Archipelago Holdings Inc. in a
deal that will turn the Big Board into a publicly traded company and help it expand electronic trading to better
compete with rivals. The deal values the newly formed company at $3.5 billion and will essentially look like a
reverse-merger, with the NYSE being subsumed into Archipelago.
Pernod Ricard SA and Fortune Brands Inc. agree to a $14.2 billion takeover of Allied Domecq PLC in the
latest liquor-industry consolidation.
22 The Dow industrials rise 206.24 points to 10218.60, the biggest one-day gain since 2003. A weekly report
shows initial jobless claims plunging, which, when combined with a report showing a pickup in manufacturing
activity in the mid-Atlantic region, suggests the economy may have regained momentum.
A U.S. district judge throws out part of the first big fraud case brought under the Sarbanes-Oxley law, against
HealthSouth founder Richard Scrushy.
25 Valero Energy Corp. agrees to buy refiner Premcor Inc. for $6.9 billion in cash and stock plus the
assumption of about $1.8 billion of debt, a deal that creates the largest crude-oil refiner in North America and
marks a big step in the U.S. industry's rapid consolidation.
