Page 137 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
26 Deloitte &Touche LLP will pay $50 million to settle allegations over fraud at Adelphia Communications
Corp., and Arthur Andersen LLP agrees to a $65 million resolution of a suit by WorldCom Inc. investors,
capping a string of accounting settlements.
29 U.S. GDP rose at a 3.1% annual rate in the first quarter, its worst performance in two years, amid signs of
a further slowdown.
MAY
2 American International Group Inc. says it will restate more than four years of results to correct accounting
errors and admits that former executives were able to circumvent internal controls.
3 Verizon wins a three-month bidding war for MCI after increasing its offer to $8.44 billion, or $26 a share,
prompting Qwest to drop out. It is the last in a series of four giant deals that reorder the U.S.
telecommunications industry.
Neiman Marcus Group Inc. agrees to be acquired by private-equity firms Warburg Pincus and Texas Pacific
Group for about $5 billion, or $100 a share.
4 The Fed raises rates a quarter point to 3% and says it plans continued increases at a "measured" pace,
signaling inflation remains its priority.
5 Kirk Kerkorian says his Tracinda Corp. investment company will offer about $868 million, or $31 a share, for
28 million shares of General Motors, nearly 5% of the total stock outstanding. The deal would make Tracinda,
which says it already owns 22 million GM shares, the auto maker's third-largest shareholder. GM's stock
surges 18% to $32.80. The offer is launched more than two weeks later.
6 Standard &Poor's cuts its credit rating on GM and Ford, downgrading the auto makers' debt to junk status,
citing doubts about their strategy, in particular the reliance on big sport-utility vehicles, sales of which are
falling. GM and Ford debt plunges in heavy trading. The downgrades threaten to transform the
corporate-bond market, especially in the junk arena.
Merck &Co. names Richard Clark as CEO to succeed Raymond Gilmartin, who is leaving earlier than
scheduled amid Vioxx litigation and a weak drug pipeline.
Lazard Ltd. launches an $854 million IPO, ending private control at the 157-year-old banking firm. The shares
fall 4%.
Britain's Tony Blair wins a third term as prime minister. Still, final results show the Labour Party parliamentary
majority slipped to 66 seats from more than 160. Later, Blair will face, and reject, calls from his own party to
hand power quickly to finance chief Gordon Brown.
10 Duke Energy Corp. agrees to buy Cinergy Corp. for $9.1 billion in stock, creating a utility stretching from
the Midwest to the Southeast and accelerating the power industry's consolidation.
11 UAL's bid to transfer four underfunded pension plans to the federal government is approved by a
bankruptcy judge, paving the way for the largest pension default in U.S. corporate history. The decision could
have wide repercussions in the airline industry. A day later, UAL posts a $1.1 billion loss, and says it will seek
to void its labor contracts.
12 Blockbuster Inc. holders elect Carl Icahn and two other dissidents to the board, defeating Chairman and
CEO John Antioco. Icahn's victory demonstrates the clout of hedge funds, which backed the financier.
Antioco is reappointed days later and says he has a year or less to show results from his rebuilding strategy.
17 Newsweek retracts a report of Quran desecration at Guantanamo Bay amid White House criticism of the
article, which sparked Muslim riots. The magazine later says it will tighten guidelines on unnamed sources.
Morgan Stanley suffers a legal setback when a Florida jury awards billionaire financier Ronald Perelman
$604.3 million on his claims that the Wall Street firm defrauded him when he sold camping-gear maker
Coleman to Sunbeam in 1998. Morgan Stanley later appeals. The award rises to $1.45 billion after the jury
sets punitive damages at $850 million.
The Supreme Court strikes down state laws restricting sales to consumers by out-of-state wineries, a boost
for small vintners seeking to sell over the Internet.
18 Hewlett-Packard's CEO says he plans significant cost cuts at the printer and computer maker, and urges
Wall Street to lower profit expectations for the company in coming months. Mark Hurd's comments come as
H-P reports that earnings rose 9.3% in the latest quarter, despite a slowdown at its printer unit. Revenue
increases 7.2%.

--- Page 138 ---
Page 138 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
20 US Airways Group Inc. announces a merger with America West Holdings Corp. The carriers value the
equity of the combined airline at $850 million. The deal will enable the far larger US Airways to emerge from
bankruptcy-court protection. The companies said the merged entity could be profitable at current oil prices
because of $600 million in annual cost savings and synergies.
Maytag Corp. is being bought by an investment group led by private-equity firm Ripplewood Holdings LLC for
$1.13 billion.
Researchers in South Korea report extracting stem cells from embryos cloned using DNA from 11 sick or
injured people, believed to be a startling advance in "therapeutic cloning" of tissue genetically matched to
intended recipients.
23 China says it is raising duties on some of its garment exports by 400%, or five times the current rate, in
response to pressure from the U.S. and Europe to curb textile exports.
24 Moderates reach a deal to avert a Senate showdown on judges. Under the bipartisan compromise,
Democrats agree to allow yes-or-no votes on some disputed nominees and not to block future ones, including
to the Supreme Court, except in "extraordinary circumstances." The Republicans will back off a bid to end
filibusters in such cases. The agreement breaks a logjam of Bush's judicial appointees.
27 New York officials charge in a civil suit that AIG, former CEO Greenberg and a former finance chief
manipulated results and duped investors.
31 The U.S. says it will take its case against Airbus subsidies to the World Trade Organization, in what could
be the most-expensive dispute in the trade body's history. The European Union had offered to cut the aid
European countries give Airbus to develop new aircraft, but Washington wants an end to the support before it
would consider negotiating away money Boeing receives.
Europe faces a tougher road to integration and expansion after French voters decisively reject a proposed EU
constitution and their Dutch counterparts follow suit a day later in a nonbinding but sharp defeat. The results
have prompted questions about whether the high-water mark of the postwar project to integrate the
historically fractious Continent has been reached. Britain later shelves plans for its referendum on the EU
constitution.
JUNE
1 The Supreme Court overturns the 2002 criminal conviction that doomed Arthur Andersen, finding that the
trial judge erred by granting a government request to loosen the standard jury instructions regarding the
shredding of Enron-related documents. The ruling won't revive the accounting firm, but could hamper the
government crackdown on white-collar crime.
American International Group releases its annual report, slashing its net worth by 2.7%. The insurer also
warns of longer-term costs from accounting errors.
W. Mark Felt and the Washington Post confirm the onetime No. 2 at the Federal Bureau of Investigation was
the mystery Watergate source known as "Deep Throat."
2 Securities and Exchange Commission Chairman William Donaldson, who angered big business and some
Republicans during his tenure by pushing such measures as hefty fines for corporate wrongdoers, says he
will resign at the end of the month. A day later, President Bush names California Rep. Christopher Cox to fill
the post. Rep Cox backed legislation making it harder for investors to file fraud suits and opposed stricter
rules on expensing stock options and merger accounting.
8 General Motors says it plans to cut about 25,000 North American jobs, or 14% of the region's work force, by
2008. CEO Rick Wagoner says the auto maker also will close more plants and is studying "strategic options"
for its financing arm.
9 Maurice R. "Hank" Greenberg resigns from the board of AIG. His attorneys complain that AIG hasn't
provided him with information or consulted him about an internal company probe since he resigned as
chairman and CEO in March.
Kirk Kerkorian's Tracinda says it nearly doubled its General Motors stake to 7.2% but fell short of its 8.8%
goal, in part because some investors are betting the stock will rise.
10 Theodore Sihpol is found not guilty of improperly trading mutual funds, dealing a major setback to Eliot
Spitzer in his campaign against financial fraud. The former Bank of America Corp. broker had been accused
of helping hedge fund Canary Capital trade after hours while still receiving same-day pricing. In October,
Spitzer says he won't retry the case.

Page 139 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A former executive at a unit of Berkshire Hathaway Inc.'s General Re pleads guilty to fraud over a deal with
AIG and implicates four current or former colleagues.
13 Italy's UniCredito Italiano SpA agrees to buy Germany's HVB Group AG for $18.81 billion in stock, in
Europe's biggest cross-border banking deal.
China and the European Union resolve a dispute over textile exports, in a deal that contrasts with the U.S.
approach of unilateral quotas.
Citigroup Inc. agrees to pay $2 billion to settle a class-action suit by Enron Corp. investors who lost billions of
dollars when the energy firm collapsed.
14 Morgan Stanley Chief Executive Officer Philip Purcell agrees to step down as soon as a successor is
named. Mr. Purcell receives exit pay of $44 million. Days later, Morgan Stanley is reassessing its plan to spin
off its Discover credit-card unit. A big concern is that Discover may need a bigger capital infusion than
previously thought to become a stand-alone company. It later decides against that move.
The Supreme Court rules drug makers should have broad exemption from patent infringement during
early-stage research, in a case brought by Merck KGaA of Germany.
15 J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. agrees to pay $2.2 billion to settle a class-action lawsuit filed by Enron investors.
The move represents a strategic shift by the securities firm, which had been criticized for being too slow to
settle a WorldCom Inc. suit.
16 Two former Bristol-Myers Squibb Co. executives are indicted and the drug maker agrees to pay
shareholders $300 million over a scheme to inflate results.
17 Bank of America agrees to buy a 9% stake in China Construction Bank for $2.5 billion in its first big move
into China.
20 Former Tyco International Ltd. top executives L. Dennis Kozlowski and Mark H. Swartz are found guilty of
charges including grand larceny, conspiracy and securities fraud in the looting of more than $150 million from
the company. Jurors in the retrial say they convicted the two after concluding they were lying on the witness
stand.
Guidant Corp. discloses rare defects in several of its surgically implanted heart defibrillators, including one
possibly linked to a death. A week later, it pulls five types of defibrillators off the market, leaving it sidelined
from the fastest-growing category of the devices.
Only 7 1/2 months after the presidential election, Sen. Joseph Biden, a frequent critic of the Iraq war's
handling, says he plans to run for the 2008 Democratic nomination.
21 John Rigas is sentenced to 15 years in prison for fraud and conspiracy, essentially a life sentence for the
80-year-old founder of Adelphia Communications Corp. His son Timothy Rigas, the former finance chief,
receives a 20-year sentence. The two were convicted of looting the cable company of more than $100 million
and lying about its condition.
22 Ford Motor slashes its 2005 earnings outlook for the second time this year and says it will make deeper
job and cost cuts than originally announced. The auto maker blames a worse-than-expected slump in sales of
SUVs amid rising gasoline prices.
A Mississippi ex-Klansman is convicted of manslaughter, not murder, in the 1964 race killings of Andrew
Goodman, Michael Schwerner and James Chaney. Days later, Edgar Ray Killen, 80, draws three 20-year
terms.
23 Chinese oil company Cnooc Ltd. makes an unsolicited $18.5 billion bid for Unocal Corp. in an effort to
break up the U.S. oil company's pending $16.5 billion acquisition by Chevron Corp. The effort is by far the
largest foreign acquisition ever attempted by a Chinese company and fuels debate about China's growing
global economic weight.
24 Justices expand eminent-domain power for private development. The Supreme Court, 5-4, says local
governments can seize private property to encourage tax-base enhancement by advancing private ventures
such as hotels and offices, the same as for parks or roads. Justice Stevens, in the majority opinion, says
localities can and should enact strict safeguards against abuse.
The Dow industrials erase almost a month of gains, plunging 166.49 points to 10421.44, as oil touches $60 a
barrel. Crude settles at a New York Mercantile Exchange record of $59.42. Days later, the contract settles at
$60.54, the first close over $60 a barrel.

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Existing-home sales eased in May. Median home prices rose to a record $207,000, with no evidence the
market will cool soon.
27 Iran's new president takes a much tougher nuclear stance. U.S. officials see Tehran becoming a bigger
headache under Mahmoud Ahmadinejad. Days later, five American ex-hostages say they believe
Ahmadinejad was among student captors in 1979. A sixth doesn't. An Ahmadinejad aide denies it.
Venezuela's Hugo Chavez warns oil companies that he will end contracts unless they give priority to domestic
needs over exports. Reviews are under way.
28 The Supreme Court rules file-sharing firms may be liable for copyright infringement if their products
encourage illegal swapping of songs and movies. It also rules cable-television firms aren't required to share
high-speed Internet links with rivals, in a win for the cable industry. Separately, the court turns away appeals
by a New York Times and a Time reporter facing possible jail time for refusing to divulge sources in the
Valerie Plame Central Intelligence Agency-leak probe.
HealthSouth restates financial statements to slash more than $1 billion in sales and profit from its
2000-to-2002 results.
29 Richard Scrushy is acquitted of participating in a $2.7 billion accounting fraud at HealthSouth, the chain of
clinics he founded. The verdict is a big defeat for the government's campaign against corporate corruption
and in its first attempt to convict a CEO for violating the Sarbanes-Oxley Act. Prosecutors later say they won't
retry Scrushy on three perjury charges.
30 The U.S. economy expanded at a faster-than-expected 3.8% pace in the first quarter, propelled by the
sizzling housing sector. Investors are buying homes at a record pace, adding more fuel to the housing
market.
JULY
1 Time Magazine gives in to a court demand that it name sources. In a decision labeled disappointing by both
the New York Times, which has a reporter facing jail in the Central Intelligence Agency-leak probe, and his
own reporter targeted in the case, Time editor Norman Pearlstine agrees to hand over notes, saying the
"responsible thing" was to "obey the law."
Bank of America Corp. is buying MBNA Corp. for about $35 billion, making it the largest issuer of
general-purpose credit cards in the country. The deal makes the company No. 2 in the U.S. in market value
and assets, trailing only Citigroup Inc. Bank of America plans to cut 6,000 jobs and take a $1.25 billion
charge.
The Fed raises rates a quarter point to 3.25%. Economists surveyed expect rates to rise to 4% in the next 12
months. The Dow industrials fall 99.51 points to 10274.97 to end the quarter down 2.2%.
5 Sandra Day O'Connor announces her retirement. Conservative groups press hard to influence President
Bush's choice of a nominee, fearing they will miss a long-awaited opportunity to tip the court decisively to the
right.
General Motors scored its best U.S. sales month in 19 years in June and a day later extends its employee
discounts for consumers through Aug. 1, prompting Ford Motor and DaimlerChrysler's Chrysler Group to
adopt GM's pricing tactic.
7 The New York Times's Judith Miller is ordered jailed until she tells a grand jury with whom she had spoken
while researching a never-written article on an undercover CIA agent, whose identity was disclosed by Robert
Novak in what some say was retribution for her husband's undermining of a Bush claim on Iraq. Matthew
Cooper of Time avoids Miller's fate by agreeing to cooperate, saying his source freed him from a
confidentiality pledge just before court.
8 Terror falls on London. In a 56-minute burst amid the morning rush, bombs tear apart three subway trains
and a bus, killing at least 37 and wounding 700, a level of carnage never achieved by the IRA and unseen in
Britain's capital since the German blitz. A shaken Blair calls the attacks "barbaric" acts done by terrorists
falsely appropriating Islam's banner. A group called al Qaeda in Europe claims it did the bombings to punish
the U.S. ally for supporting Iraq and Afghan wars. Police later call the suicide attacks a worrying sign of a
deepening fanaticism. Still, financial markets rebound after initially shuddering. London's FTSE 100, a day
away from hitting a three-year high but down nearly 4% at one point, finishes at 5158.30, 1.4% lower. The
Dow industrials rise 31.61 points to 10302.29.
13 Cardiac stents made by Boston Scientific Corp. continue to malfunction in operations performed after
2004's temporary recall, doctors report to the FDA. The agency received at least 45 reports of deflation

--- Page 141 ---
Page 141 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
difficulties with balloons used to deploy the stents from Oct. 1 to April 1. Three people died and dozens more
were injured.
14 The NHL and players reach a six-year deal after a lockout that killed the 2004-05 season. Owners wring
big concessions.
18 Whirlpool Corp. makes a preliminary offer of $1.33 billion, or $17 a share, to buy rival appliance maker
Maytag Corp. That compares with a $14-a-share deal from a Ripplewood Holdings-led private-equity group
and a $16-a-share proposal from a triumvirate of China's Qingdao Haier Ltd. and private-equity funds
Blackstone Group and Bain Capital, though Haier will drop out days later.
19 Bush hosts a state visit by Indian Premier Manmohan Singh, agreeing to push for nuclear cooperation, a
move that may shake the nonproliferation regime and tilt the U.S. away from rival Pakistan. Addressing a joint
session of Congress days later, Singh says his country is a terror-war ally and "responsible nuclear power."
Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan rejects the use of Fed tools to control housing prices and says
the economy is "coping pretty well" with higher oil costs.
20 President Bush names appeals court Judge John Roberts to be O'Connor's successor. He was picked by
Bush for the District of Columbia circuit in 2003, having worked for Ronald Reagan and as a deputy solicitor
general under Kenneth Starr in the administration of the president's father. Bush praises Roberts, 50 years
old, as one of the country's "finest legal minds," one who won't "legislate from the bench." Conservative
Republican lawmakers are pleased. Democrats hold their fire but vow deep scrutiny.
The newly renamed Chevron Corp. raises its bid for Unocal Corp. by $2 a share in cash to about $63 a share,
or more than $17 billion, narrowing the gap between Chevron and China's Cnooc Ltd.
Hewlett-Packard is eliminating 14,500 jobs, or about 10% of its workers globally, cutting pension benefits and
simplifying its sales force.
21 Sunnis suspend participation in drafting an Iraq constitution, demanding better security after the murders
of two drafters and threats to others; other participants vow the draft will be done by Aug. 15. Iraqis hold a
nationwide moment of silence for bombing victims. Ten more are killed at a recruiting center.
A jury declines to convict five former Enron Corp. officials on any of over 160 criminal counts in a case
involving the firm's telecommunications unit.
22 London escapes fresh tragedy as bombs again hit public transport. As in the July 7 attacks, three subway
trains and a bus were targeted; this time the only injury is a person with an asthma spell, as the main charges
fail to go off. One person arrested later says the plot wasn't tied to July 7 attacks, wasn't meant to kill, and
was intended as a protest of the Iraq war.
China revalues its currency, making it 2.1% stronger against the dollar, and says it no longer will peg the
yuan's value only to the dollar but instead will let it trade within a narrow band against a basket of currencies.
The Dow industrials fall 61.38 to 10627.77. Treasury prices plunge and the dollar weakens.
26 Teva Pharmaceutical Industries Ltd. says it will acquire Ivax Corp. for $7.4 billion, creating the largest
generic-drug company. The deal gives the Israeli company certain product lines and an international
presence it lacked.
Sales of existing homes rose 2.7% in June from May to a record annual rate of 7.35 million units, boosted by
demand for condos.
Labor faces an uncertain future, with implications for national politics, as the service workers and Teamsters
bolt from the AFL-CIO, two days before John Sweeney is re-elected AFL-CIO chief. Later, two more unions
disaffiliate.
27 Discovery blasts off, returning a shuttle to space 2 1/2 years after Columbia's tragedy. Debris falls off a
tank, but didn't appear to strike the craft. The shuttle lands two weeks later without incident at Edwards Air
Force Base in California.
28 The Central American Free Trade Agreement clears Congress. The president manages a 217-215 House
win after he and Vice President Dick Cheney make personal appeals and leaders cajole members uneasy at
constituents' discontent with globalization's effect on U.S. jobs and economic security. Meanwhile,
House-Senate negotiators agree on a $286.4 billion highway bill. Though that is $2.4 billion over the ceiling
Bush set, he withdraws a veto threat.
29 DaimlerChrysler CEO Jurgen Schrempp says he plans to retire at the end of 2005, three years before his
contract was set to expire. He will be succeeded by Dieter Zetsche, who engineered a turnaround at Chrysler.

--- Page 142 ---
Page 142 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The announcement comes as the auto maker posts mixed results and as Deutsche Bank begins to sell 35
million DaimlerChrysler shares.
The Nasdaq Composite Index hits a four-year high of 2198.44 on optimism about the economy, profits and
rates.
The Irish Republican Army says it ordered members to put down weapons, which may resuscitate peace
efforts and end violence in Northern Ireland and Britain.
AUGUST
1 Lachlan Murdoch quits as News Corp.'s deputy chief operating officer, in part due to a rift with his father,
Rupert, over future control of the media giant. Rupert Murdoch's four children from his first two marriages
were to inherit the vast majority of the family's stake, but the News Corp. chairman has tried to give a share to
two young children born to his new wife.
2 Bush makes John Bolton the U.N. envoy, brushing off Senate resistance. The recess appointment on the
first working day since Congress went on vacation shows the president's impatience at Capitol Hill jousting
that blocked a vote.
King Fahd, believed to be 84, dies, arousing anxiety on long-term Saudi Arabian stability, though for now it
changes little since Abdullah, his successor, has been de facto ruler for the decade following Fahd's disabling
stroke.
3 Cnooc Ltd. abandons its efforts to acquire Unocal, setting the stage for Chevron Corp. to win control. The
Chinese company blames political opposition in the U.S. for its withdrawal, but the failure of its $18.5 billion
bid also reflects Cnooc's own missteps.
4 Time Warner Inc. sets aside $3 billion to settle fraud claims by shareholders who lost money after the 2001
AOL merger, closing the book on one of the Internet boom's biggest disasters. Of that, $2.4 billion will go to
settle a class-action suit. Creation of the reserves causes Time Warner to post a $321 million loss.
8 The Nasdaq plans to withdraw from a system that links U.S. stock markets, in an effort to better compete
against the NYSE.
Shares in China's Baidu.com Inc surge more than fourfold from their offering price, in the best U.S. stock
debut in more than five years.
Pakistani police say an Islamic militant has told them he was working with two al Qaeda operatives when he
arranged a meeting that led to the 2002 kidnapping and murder of Journal reporter Daniel Pearl.
9 Delphi posts a $338 million loss and says it might file for bankruptcy-law protection if it can't reach a pact
with workers and GM by Oct. 17.
An independent panel accuses two U.N. officials of oil-for-food corruption, including Benon Sevan, the
program's ex-director, who resigned from the U.N. a day earlier. The U.N. revokes the diplomatic status of
former procurement officer Alexander Yakovlev, who federal attorneys later say admitted soliciting a bribe
under the Iraq humanitarian program.
Japan's Koizumi loses a postal-privatization vote, a defeat that highlighted differences over the economy. The
prime minister dissolves the Lower House of Parliament and calls for a Sept. 11 election in a bid to confirm
public support.
10 The Federal Reserve lifts short-term rates a quarter point for a 10th straight meeting to 3.5%.
11 Iran fully restores a plant to uranium-conversion operations. Iranian officials remove seals voluntarily
placed on equipment at the U.N.'s behest eight months ago, and technicians resume processing uranium into
gas for enrichment. A day later, the IAEA adopts a resolution calling for Iran to stop nuclear work. Diplomats
say Iran could face a referral to the Security Council -- a move an Iranian diplomat says would be a step
toward confrontation -- and possible sanctions after a September deadline for an IAEA report.
12 Former WorldCom Inc. finance chief Scott Sullivan, who cooperated with the government, receives a
reduced five-year prison term.
A U.S. grand jury indicts Republican fund-raiser and DeLay political ally Jack Abramoff for defrauding two
lenders in his purchase five years ago of a casino cruise line. His lawyer says he is not guilty of the charges.

--- Page 143 ---
Page 143 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
15 Maytag's board backs Whirlpool's takeover offer of $1.7 billion, or $21 a share, over a Ripplewood
Holdings-led group's bid. A deal with Whirlpool will create the world's No. 1 home-appliance maker with an
estimated 48% of the U.S.'s major-appliance market. Ripplewood later decides not to sweeten its offer.
The widening U.S. trade gap, driven by rising oil prices, is expected to hack second-quarter growth estimates.
The deficit grew 6.1% in June to $58.82 billion.
Israel seals the Gaza Strip to its civilians, marking the opening move in the bitterly contentious withdrawal of
all settlers, 8,500 of whom are facing forcible eviction if they don't leave by Wednesday.
16 Iraqis stumble at the finish line as snags hold up a constitution. Parliament grants a one-week extension to
resolve differences over the role of Islam and regional autonomy, with Shiites, Kurds and Sunnis still far
apart.
17 Israel commences its wrenching showdown with settlers in Gaza. About 2,000 troops enter the biggest of
the enclaves but don't immediately resort to force to evict those refusing to leave as the Sharon government's
withdrawal orders took effect. Confrontations are highly emotional on both sides, but violence is minor.
Hewlett-Packard reports better-than-expected results and boosted forecasts for the current quarter. Still, net
falls 88% on a tax adjustment related to a decision to repatriate $14.5 billion of foreign earnings.
18 The former Federal Bureau of Investigation agent who warned about Moussaoui prior to the Sept. 11,
2001, attacks is joining antiwar protests led by Cindy Sheehan, the mother of a slain soldier, near Bush's
ranch. An Ohio mother whose Marine son died with 13 others Aug. 3 rejects retreat talk at his funeral.
19 Google Inc. said it plans to sell 14.2 million additional shares, an offering valued at more than $4 billion.
The surprise move prompts speculation about how the Web-search company might use the proceeds, which
would more than double its cash balance. Google says the cash will be used for general corporate purposes.
The shares fall $5.11 at $279.99, still more than three times the IPO price.
22 Merck loses the first Vioxx trial, with jurors producing a $253 million verdict that presents a worrisome
harbinger as the drug maker faces thousands of lawsuits over the drug and that could stoke a
pharmaceutical-industry backlash that already has affected drug development and marketing.
23 Citigroup Inc.'s Marge Magner is leaving. One of the most powerful female executives in the financial
world, she clashed with CEO Charles Prince over his plans to restructure consumer-banking operations.
Iraqis present a draft constitution, but Sunnis aren't on board. Parliament puts off for three days a vote on the
charter, but Sunni leaders threaten to seek its defeat at referendum if their concerns, chiefly federalism
language, aren't addressed.
Israel completes evacuation of settlers from Gaza, clearing the last enclave. A day later, Israel empties four
West Bank settlements, implementing Sharon's "disengagement" plan ahead of schedule and thwarting
ultranationalist efforts to rally enough support to stop it.
24 Existing-home sales fell 2.6% in July from June and inventories of unsold homes rose, a sign residential
real estate may be losing steam.
26 In the wake of its Vioxx loss, Merck says it may consider settling a small number of Vioxx cases if patients
who claim they were harmed fit a very narrow profile. The company's general counsel stresses the
fight-every-case strategy is still in force, but a spokeswoman says Merck would "carefully look" at instances
where patients took the painkiller for more than 18 months and had no other risk factors for heart attacks or
strokes.
HealthSouth's first finance chief, Aaron Beam, is sentenced to three months in federal prison for his part in a
scheme to inflate profit at the health-care company.
29 New Orleans empties under mandatory evacuation as Hurricane Katrina, much-strengthened over water,
bears down. Those unable to get out pack the Superdome. More than one million flee the Gulf Coast and
much oil production shuts down.
31 The U.N. names four pro-Syria generals and an ex-legislator as suspects in the Hariri assassination.
SEPTEMBER
1 MasterCard Inc. plans to sell 49% of the company in an IPO. It also delays plans to charge fees for all card
transactions abroad.

--- Page 144 ---
Page 144 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A stampede kills about 800 Shiites during a pilgrimage in Iraq. Participants in a massive Baghdad procession
marking a revered saint's death panic when rumors of a suicide bomber sweep the crowd, already on edge
over a mortar barrage near a mosque that was their destination.
6 Spain's Gas Natural SDG SA launches an unsolicited bid for Spanish power utility Endesa SA in a $28.8
billion deal that would create one of Europe's top energy firms, though Endesa later rejects the offer, setting
the stage for a tough takeover battle.
President Bush says John Roberts now will succeed Chief Justice William Rehnquist, who died of thyroid
cancer. The 80-year-old jurist served 33 years on the court and was the nucleus around which the
conservative majority assembled.
7 The U.N. and its contractors are criticized scathingly by the Volcker panel, which found oil-for-food graft
served Hussein's purposes. Paul Volcker finds U.N. corruption let Saddam Hussein rake off $10.2 billion in
oil-for-food funds. Questions on Annan and Boutros-Ghali remain.
9 A grand jury indicts a political action committee formed by House Majority Leader Tom DeLay, alleging it
illegally accepted a $100,000 contribution. A Texas business group also is indicted. Mr. DeLay isn't charged.
12 In Japan, Koizumi's LDP wins a strong mandate, picking up 47 Diet seats in snap elections called to test
Japanese support for pension and health-care strategies.
13 EBay Inc. agrees to buy Internet-calling start-up Skype Technologies SA for about $2.6 billion in cash and
stock, a new challenge to phone firms.
15 Delta and Northwest file for Chapter 11, underscoring how old-line carriers increasingly see
bankruptcy-court protection as the best route to revival amid an industry crisis. The filings mean four of the
seven biggest U.S. carriers now are operating under bankruptcy protection.
19 Germany's election ends in uncertainty with no clear victor. Gerhard Schroeder and challenger Angela
Merkel both claim the right to form a new coalition government and be the next chancellor. Merkel's Christian
Democrats win slightly more votes, well short of a majority. Germany faces the prospect of prolonged
coalition talks and delays in measures to lift the economy.
20 North Korea agrees in principle to give up nuclear arms and abandon efforts to build more, but the
breakthrough is incomplete as no timetable or inspections were set. Talks made progress only when the U.S.
agreed to discuss giving a civilian reactor; Pyongyang casts doubt on whether its position really changed,
saying there is no deal until it gets it.
Tyco's two former top officials, Dennis Kozlowski and Mark Swartz, are sentenced to 8 1/2 to 25 years for
looting the company. In October, a judge denies bids to get out of jail while their appeals are being heard.
21 The Fed raises rates a quarter point to 3.75%, concluding Katrina's impact on inflation is more worrisome
than its effect on growth. The Dow industrials fall 76.11 points to 10481.52.
Died: Simon Wiesenthal, 96, Holocaust survivor who spent his life unmasking Nazi perpetrators trying to
blend into postwar society, in Vienna.
22 Texas cities empty as Hurricane Rita grows more fearsome. Galveston and low parts of Corpus Christi
and Houston are ordered evacuated as the storm becomes a Category 5, 165-m.p.h. monster. Including parts
of Louisiana, at least 1.3 million take to the roads ahead of landfall. Oil refiners brace for the storm amid fears
operations will be disrupted and send gasoline prices spiraling upward again.
23 Sony's new CEO Stringer vows to slash costs, products and 10,000 jobs while boosting sales, and
forecasts a $90 million fiscal-year loss.
The SEC is investigating sales of HCA stock made by Senate Majority Leader Frist shortly before the
company issued an earnings warning.
WHO says that the bird-flu outbreak may not be widening despite fears in Indonesia and that there is no sign
of easy person-to-person transmission. Still, companies are preparing for a pandemic.
24 Hurricane Rita inflicts pain even before it strikes the coast. In Texas, a bus evacuating nursing-home
patients catches fire in traffic, killing at least 21. Two patched levees give way in New Orleans amid the heavy
outer rains preceding landfall, flooding low-lying areas that had just been pumped out following Katrina. Rita
weakens to a Category 3, 125-mph storm as it nears Texas.

--- Page 145 ---
Page 145 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
26 Hurricane Rita shuts down a huge swath of the U.S.'s energy infrastructure, but the storm's long-term
economic impact is more muted than that of Katrina. Still, it shows again how vulnerable the U.S. energy
system has become.
27 Alan Greenspan says consumers have become enormously dependent on borrowing against their homes
to fuel their spending, and that a rise in mortgage rates could trigger a pullback. Meanwhile, existing-home
sales climbed 2% in August and the median sales price soared 15.8% from the year ago to $220,000.
A military jury convicts Army Pfc. Lynndie England, seen in many photos of Abu Ghraib prisoner abuse. She
later draws a three-year sentence.
The IRA has fully disarmed, according to a Northern Ireland decommissioning panel started in 1997.
Hard-line Protestant leaders are skeptical.
28 Michael Brown, former head of the Federal Emergency Management Agency, blames local Democrats for
mishandling Katrina. In a grilling by the House, Mr. Brown also finds some fault with the White House for a
botched initial storm response and rejects allegations he was unqualified for the job.
29 Rep. Tom DeLay is indicted. The charge of a conspiracy to violate Texas campaign laws forces the
hard-punching party pugilist to relinquish his House leadership job while he fights it. A caucus gives
Missouri's Blunt the majority leader title, saying the two will share duties. DeLay casts himself as victim of a
"partisan fanatic" bent on avenging Democrats' defeats in a tough Texas redistricting battle. The Democratic
district attorney says he is simply prosecuting a crime, the funneling of corporate money to state races.
AIG files suit for control of $20 billion of the insurer's shares, raising the stakes in the battle with former CEO
Greenberg.
30 Bayou Management's two founders, Samuel Israel III and Daniel Marino, plead guilty to criminal-fraud
charges related to the collapse of the Connecticut hedge fund, which closed its doors in July. Authorities say
the firm never turned a profit despite years of sunny reports to clients, and it mostly stopped trading securities
in mid-2004, as its founders tried desperately to recoup losses with fanciful bets totaling $150 million -- all that
was left of the $450 million it had collected from investors.
Judith Miller leaves jail after agreeing to testify to a grand jury investigating the Plame leak, saying her
source, Cheney aide I. Lewis "Scooter" Libby, released her from a confidentiality vow.
OCTOBER
1 Personal income fell $5.3 billion in August from July, due in part to the impact of Hurricane Katrina.
Consumer spending fell by $47.2 billion, the largest drop since November 2001, hurt by cooling auto sales
and surging energy prices. Inflation jumped 0.5%, the biggest increase in 15 years.
The Dow industrials rose 15.92 points to 10568.70, up 1.4% for the week and 2.9% for the quarter. Oil futures
ease to $66.24 a barrel.
Documents confirm Majority Leader Sen. Bill Frist in April set in motion the process that led to July HCA stock
sales scrutinized for timing.
4 Bush nominates Harriet Miers for Sandra Day O'Connor's Supreme Court seat. The 60-year-old White
House counsel and Bush loyalist from his Texas days has no track record as a judge or publicly known views
on hot-button political issues such as abortion, leading to disgruntlement on the part of the president's
conservative Christian base but satisfaction by business backers because of her extensive experience as a
corporate lawyer.
House Majority Leader Tom DeLay is re-indicted by a Texas grand jury for money laundering in the same
fund-raising transaction that led to last week's conspiracy charge, which his attorneys said relied on a law not
yet in effect. He accuses the district attorney behind both cases of "prosecutorial abuse."
The search for bodies ends with Louisiana's Katrina toll at 964.
5 China Construction Bank begins what could be China's largest initial public offering, as the lender sells $9.2
billion in Hong Kong.
6 Health and Human Services Secretary Mike Leavitt pronounces the U.S. unprepared for any bird-flu
pandemic and laid out steps costing as much as $10 billion, such as reviving the U.S. vaccine industry.
7 UAL says it received commitments for a $3 billion all-debt financing package from J.P. Morgan Chase and
Citigroup. The six-year loan, which carries an interest rate of about 8.5%, will enable the United Airlines
parent to repay an interim bankruptcy loan and emerge from Chapter 11 in February.

--- Page 146 ---
Page 146 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
8 The job market showed surprising strength in September despite Hurricane Katrina's impact.
Unemployment rose to 5.1% but payrolls fell far less than expected. The data suggest the U.S. economy is
likely to recover well from Katrina, keeping the Fed focused on containing inflation by lifting rates.
10 Rescuers rush to find quake survivors in Pakistan and India. The final death toll tops 87,000 people. In
harder-hit Pakistan, remote towns and villages are destroyed, and hundreds of schoolchildren are trapped
beneath rubble. The 7.6-magnitude quake, the strongest in Pakistan's memory, is centered in the mountains
of Pakistani Kashmir but also jolts Afghanistan and India. The U.N. says more than 2.5 million people need
shelter. Muslim militant groups report heavy casualties among their fighters in the disaster, in a region where
many believe Osama bin Laden himself hides. Pakistan later accepts an offer of aid from longtime foe India.
Delphi Corp. will shutter or sell a substantial part of its U.S. operations, but the auto-parts supplier, which filed
for Chapter 11 last week, isn't yet sure if it will ask the federal government to take over its pension obligations,
its CEO says.
11 Refco Inc. CEO Phillip R. Bennett takes a leave after the brokerage firm finds he transferred $430 million
of company assets to a firm he controlled. Prosecutors later charge Bennett with securities fraud.
The Nobel prize in economics is awarded to Thomas Schelling and Robert Aumann for their seminal work on
game theory.
14 Samsung Electronics Ltd. becomes the third big memory-chip maker to plead guilty to a criminal
price-fixing charge for its role in a global cartel, and is fined $300 million.
15 The Refco crisis intensifies, with banks and securities firms scrambling to see if an outright failure can be
averted. Refco begins unwinding customer positions at a securities unit and S&P says the commodities
broker is "highly likely" to default on its rated debt. The CFTC's chairman says the part of Refco his agency
regulates is financially sound. Refco files for bankruptcy-court protection four days later.
18 GM says it will slash health-care coverage for unionized retirees and try to sell a big stake in its GMAC
financing business. The moves come as the auto maker posts a $1.63 billion loss amid deteriorating sales of
trucks and SUVs. GM says the agreement with the UAW would cut health-care liabilities for unionized retirees
by about $15 billion.
19 Roche Holding AG says it may let other companies make its antiviral drug Tamiflu, widely seen as the best
treatment for avian flu.
Johnson &Johnson signals to Guidant that their pending merger is in jeopardy, a prelude to a renegotiation of
terms in which J&J wins a 15% cut in its Guidant purchase price to $21.5 billion.
20 Saddam Hussein pleads not guilty to charges including mass murder and torture, while saying he rejects
the Iraq court's authority on the first day of his trial.
21 Ford Motor reports a $284 million loss, its first since 2003, and CEO William Ford vows to present a
restructuring plan in January.
A U.N. report implicates high-level Syrian and Lebanese security officials in former Lebanese Prime Minister
Rafik Hariri's assassination. The report also accuses Syrian officials, including the foreign minister, of
attempting to mislead the investigation. Syrian and Lebanese officials criticize the report, while Bush and
Secretary of State Rice call for accountability.
24 Cendant Corp. is to split in 2006 into four companies, concentrating on real estate, travel, hotels and car
rentals. Henry R. Silverman, the CEO who built the sprawling concern through acquisitions, will run only the
travel-distribution firm.
25 Florida avoids Wilma's worst as the hurricane travels rapidly. Still, the storm floods much of Key West and
leaves rain pouring into homes and businesses. Insured damage totals $6 billion to $10 billion and make
Wilma the third-costliest hurricane in U.S. history. The storm kills at least 10 in Florida. Oficials later estimate
agriculture damage at $1 billion.
Died: Rosa Parks, 92, civil-rights icon. Under a House resolution, she becomes the first woman to lie in honor
in the Capitol Rotunda.
26 Iraq's charter passes, against a backdrop of increasing U.S. deaths. An official count finds 78.6% of voters
support the constitution. Sunnis narrowly fail to gather enough backing to defeat the measure, which
envisions a decentralized Iraq but also has highlighted the country's ethnic and sectarian divisions. The U.S.
military, meanwhile, discloses the deaths of a Marine and a sailor in a battle last week with insurgents and

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
says another wounded soldier had died, bringing American military fatalities in Iraq to 2,000. Bush warns to
prepare for more.
BP PLC's earnings rise 34%, as soaring oil prices and refining profits offset the impact of hurricanes Katrina
and Rita and higher oil-field costs. Revenue climbs 46%, despite an overall decline in output.
27 WorldCom Inc. investors reach a $651 million settlement over losses incurred following the company's
collapse. The settlement covers about 60 institutions, each of which had opted out of a separate class-action
settlement. Nearly all of the money will be paid by WorldCom's former investment banks, led by Citigroup and
J.P. Morgan Chase.
28 Harriet Miers's candidacy buckles in the face of a conservative onslaught, as the Supreme Court nominee
withdraws.
Exxon Mobil and Royal Dutch Shell report record third-quarter earnings amid high prices for oil, gasoline and
natural gas. Exxon's net surges 75% to $9.92 billion, among the biggest quarterly profits of any company in
history, while Shell's earnings jump 68% to $9.03 billion.
29 I. Lewis "Scooter" Libby resigns after coming under indictment in the CIA-leak probe. A U.S. grand jury
charges Vice President Dick Cheney's chief of staff with five criminal counts following a two-year investigation
into the disclosure of a covert agent's identity. The indictment says Libby made false statements, perjured
himself and obstructed justice but doesn't charge him with leaking the name of Valerie Plame. He later pleads
not guilty to the charges.
GDP rose a robust 3.8% in the third quarter, marking the 16th straight period of growth and confirming that
the U.S. economy weathered the Gulf Coast hurricanes. The Dow industrials climb 172.82 points to
10402.77.
NOVEMBER
1 Bush nominates Samuel Alito for the high court. Many conservatives who had attacked Miers rush to praise
the new nominee, who, in his 15 years on the third circuit court of appeals in Philadelphia, applied
conservative interpretations to a range of issues, including the display of religious symbols on government
property, police misconduct and criminal justice.
Barrick Gold Corp. makes an unsolicited $9.2 billion cash-and-stock offer to buy rival Placer Dome Inc. and
create the world's biggest gold producer. Placer later says it is considering asset sales, purchases or other
moves to defend itself.
Novartis AG agrees to acquire full control of Chiron Corp. for $5.1 billion, reflecting the Swiss firm's drive to
enter the flu-vaccine market.
Syria is told it must cooperate with an international inquiry. A U.N. resolution -- sponsored by the U.S., France
and Britain -- demands that Damascus assist fully with a probe into the February killing of former Lebanese
Prime Minister Rafik Hariri. The Assad government avoids the direct threat of sanctions, though Syria's
foreign minister blasts a U.N. commission's interim report as politically motivated and based on a presumption
of guilt.
2 President Bush proposes a $7.1 billion plan to prepare for a flu pandemic. Most funds would go toward
boosting domestic vaccine manufacturing and reserves. The three-pronged initiative also includes improved
international monitoring of the avian-flu virus and enhanced state and local preparations.
The Federal Reserve raises short-term rates a quarter point to 4%.
4 Merck &Co. isn't liable for the heart attack of an Idaho man who took the painkiller, a jury in New Jersey
found. The judge later says future cases should involve plaintiffs who took the Merck drug for at least 18
months.
French rioting branches out from Paris to other towns as demonstrators protesting housing-project conditions
shoot at police and firefighters. The rioting ultimately will extend to more than two weeks and spread
throughout France and to other European countries.
5 Venezuela's leader trades barbs with Bush at a summit. President Chavez assails a U.S.-backed proposal
for a Free Trade Area of the Americas at the two-day hemispheric meeting in Argentina. Instead, he touts an
economic partnership of Latin American nations he is proposing as an alternative, in which Venezuela would
take a prominent role. Bush later in Brazil urges Latin Americans to reject efforts to curb democracy.
7 The U.S. and China agree to a textile pact giving Chinese producers room to raise exports to the U.S.

--- Page 148 ---
Page 148 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
8 Viacom Inc.'s CBS and General Electric Co.'s NBC Universal strike deals with cable and satellite providers
that will let viewers watch some popular TV shows whenever they want, for a fee of 99 cents an episode. The
deals with cable company Comcast Corp. and News Corp.'s DirecTV, a satellite-TV provider, mark a major
shift for the TV industry, which has long resisted efforts to offer on-demand viewing.
Grokster Ltd. shuts down as part of a settlement with the music industry. The file-sharing service had allowed
users to illegally download copyrighted songs.
9 Democrats retain New Jersey and Virginia governorships, deepening Republican angst over the toll of
President Bush's problems. Californians vote down measures backed by Arnold Schwarzenegger. Texans
approve a gay-marriage ban.
Jacques Chirac declares a 12-day state of emergency as France attempts to contain civil unrest. Authorities
report rioting in 226 towns, compared with about 300 a day earlier.
10 Bombers kill at least 57, not including the bombers, at three Western-owned Jordan hotels.
Near-simultaneous explosions tear through the lobbies of the Radisson, Park Hyatt and Days Inn hotels in
Amman, wounding at least 100 people. Al Qaeda in Iraq later says four Iraqi suicide bombers, including a
husband and wife team, carried out the bombings.
General Motors says it must restate financial results for 2001 and possibly subsequent years. The auto maker
says it overstated 2001 earnings by as much as $300 million to $400 million by "erroneously" booking credits
from suppliers. The admission, which came after the close of trading, ends a day in which GM shares fell
$1.23 to a 13-year low of $24.63.
11 Jordanians swarm the capital spewing anti-Zarqawi invective and issuing calls for the terrorist leader's
death. King Abdullah II promises to track down the perpetrators "wherever they are." Bush pledges
assistance. Jordan's foreign minister vows to remain a U.S. ally.
The U.S. trade deficit ballooned 11.4% to a record $66.10 billion in September as energy prices surged in the
wake of the hurricanes. Exports fell as civilian-aircraft sales softened, while the high oil prices and demand for
foreign-made goods boosted imports. The trade gap is on pace to exceed last year's record of $617.58 billion
and damp GDP growth.
Man Financial Inc., a unit of Man Group PLC, wins an auction to buy the assets of bankrupt futures broker
Refco Inc. in a deal Man valued at $323 million. Refco ex-CEO Phillip Bennett is indicted on charges of hiding
up to $720 million in bad debts. His lawyer says, "Mr. Bennett welcomes the opportunity to face these
charges in the court of law and clear his good name."
12 President Bush uses the Veterans Day pulpit to excoriate critics of the Iraq war. In a speech at a
Pennsylvania Army depot, the president accuses lawmakers of revisionist history regarding the war's start.
John Kerry responds that the administration selectively used intelligence to stretch the truth, and Harry Reid
pledges Democratic pursuit of information about the war's buildup.
UAW members ratify a plan that will enable GM to significantly reduce the amount of money it spends on
health care for its employees and retirees.
Died: Peter F. Drucker, 95, management-theory pioneer, in California.
14 Koch Industries Inc. is buying Georgia-Pacific Corp. for $13.2 billion. The deal will make Koch the U.S.'s
largest private firm by revenue, with about $80 billion in annual sales, and interests ranging from oil refining to
cattle ranching to Georgia-Pacific's Brawny paper towels and Dixie cups.
16 Ben Bernanke affirms his support for an explicit numerical inflation target. The nominee for Fed chairman
promises the Senate Banking Committee he would move forward on the issue only if a "consensus" can be
achieved. The comments spark a rally in the 10-year Treasury note, pushing its yield down to 4.565%.
The Senate votes to require President Bush to report on Iraq-war progress. A Democratic bid to force the
president to estimate withdrawal dates is defeated, but the milder resolution that passed still signals
impatience with the war's direction. Defense Secretary Rumsfeld warns against showing insurgents any
weakening of resolve.
Israel and the Palestinians reach an accord to open up Gaza's border with Egypt, a vital step in advancing
the territory's economic viability. Condoleezza Rice brokers the deal, refusing to end talks without an
agreement.
The U.N. reverses the only firing in the oil-for-food scandal as an appeals body finds dismissal of
procurement official Stephanides too harsh.

Bird flu fears prompt China to attempt to vaccinate its entire 14 billion-strong flock, Vietnam to push to rid its
two largest cities of all poultry.
17 Torture charges inflame sectarian bitterness further in Iraq. Sunni officials, outraged after 173
malnourished and apparently abused detainees are found at an Interior Ministry jail, demand an international
investigation and accuse the Shiite and Kurd-led government of trying to intimidate Sunni voters ahead of
Dec. 15 elections. The government denies that, saying not all the detainees were Sunnis.
18 Iraq-war fissures grow in Congress as senior House Democrat John Murtha, who voted for the war, urges
a pullout over six months. Republicans pounce, saying he wants to "wave the white flag." Iraq says it will
investigate alleged torture of Sunni detainees as the U.S. warns it won't tolerate abuse and says Shiite
militias shouldn't have official security roles.
Google marches past $400 a share, giving it a market capitalization of more than $112 billion, surpassing
Cisco, Coca-Cola and Time Warner.
19 Swiss Reinsurance Co. agrees to pay $6.8 billion for most of General Electric Co.'s insurance business,
creating the world's No. 1 reinsurer by premiums and accelerating industry consolidation.
22 General Motors raises its job-cut target by about 5,000 to 30,000 and says it will close operations at nine
North American plants. CEO Rick Wagoner says the moves should help the auto maker save as much as $7
billion by the end of 2006. Some analysts question if the cost-cutting plan is robust enough.
A judge clears a plea deal in the burgeoning Abramoff inquiry as partner and former DeLay aide Michael
Scanlon pleads guilty to bilking his Indian casino clients and agrees to cooperate in return for leniency.
23 The U.S. files charges against Jose Padilla 3 1/2 years after his detention; a jury confirms a plot to kill
President Bush. The indictment against Padilla and four others makes no mention of oft-repeated accusations
he plotted with al Qaeda to set off a "dirty bomb" in the U.S. Instead, it accuses the U.S. citizen, held in a
Navy brig since 2002 as an "enemy combatant," of conspiracy to murder Americans abroad as part of a "jihad
support cell."
Oracle Corp. CEO Larry Ellison will be required to donate $100 million to charity and pay legal fees under a
stock-trading settlement.
25 Spitzer won't pursue possible criminal charges against former AIG Chairman and CEO Greenberg in
connection with the insurer's accounting scandal. The New York attorney general's office instead is focusing
on civil-fraud allegations it lodged against Greenberg and AIG itself, which it could amend as soon as next
week to reflect new information.
A Korean cloning pioneer admits two junior scientists on his team donated eggs for stem-cell study, and other
donors had been paid. A Seoul National University panel later concludes that Hwang Woo Suk falsified data
on all 11 lines of embryonic stem-cell lines he claimed to have cloned.
29 Merck says it will cut its work force 11%, or 7,000 people, close or sell five plants, and improve the
efficiency of its drug-making operations. Investors, disappointed the cost-cutting plan didn't include fresh
ideas for generating growth, push the stock down 4.6% to $29.56.
Republican Randy "Duke" Cunningham quits his House seat after pleading guilty to conspiracy and tax
evasion in accepting $2.4 million in exchange for steering Pentagon work to defense contractor MZM Inc. He
could face 10 years in prison.
DECEMBER
1 Bush details Iraq progress he sees, but gives no hint of a departure date. Beginning a series of speeches to
shore up war support, the president draws cheers at the Naval Academy with his vow that "America will not
run in the face of car bombers," but a less-warm response on Capitol Hill, where the House Democratic
leader embraces Murtha's call to withdraw promptly.
Research In Motion Ltd.'s deal with NTP Inc. was voided by a court, paving the way for a possible halt to
BlackBerry sales and service in the U.S. More than a week later, NTP offers to settle its legal battle with RIM
in return for 5.7% of BlackBerry's expected revenue in the U.S. until 2012.
2 A New York Stock Exchange seat sells for a record $4 million.
Tokyo's Nikkei Stock Average hit a five-year high of 15130.50, boosted by confidence in an economic
upswing and changes in Japan's markets.

8 An air marshal shoots dead a passenger on a Miami airport jetway who claimed he had a bomb as he ran
off an American Air flight that was boarding for Orlando. None is found. It is the first shooting in the post-Sept.

Gold rises $4.10 to $514.30 an ounce, its highest close since April 1981.
9 Data were excised from a Vioxx study sponsored by Merck concerning three patients who had suffered

mistrial, citing the medical journal report. The Vioxx case days after that is ruled a mistrial, as one juror

13 Bush acknowledges Iraqi sacrifices, estimating 30,000 war deaths. In a speech offering encouragement to

false starts," and for the first time put a number to the tally of Iraqis killed since the U.S.-led invasion, both in
military operations and at insurgent hands. Aides label it an unofficial figure.
ConocoPhillips strikes a $35.6 billion deal to buy oil and natural-gas producer Burlington. The $92-a-share
deal, a 21% premium, highlights the surge in natural-gas prices and could jump-start a wave of consolidations
as big energy companies snap up smaller natural-gas producers.
S&P slashes GM's credit rating two notches to a B and warned that CEO Wagoner's restructuring plan may
not be enough to turn the auto maker around.
14 The Fed raises rates a quarter point to a 4 1/2-year high of 4.25% and suggests it probably will raise rates
a little more to keep the economy from overheating, but it left open the possibility that more increases won't
be necessary.
16 Iraqis display their resolve again, turning out to vote in droves. Taking a step toward political autonomy
and an end to U.S. military presence, Iraqis for the third time in a year defy insurgents and choose a
permanent government. Turnout is so heavy that some polls are kept open an extra hour.
19 Bush addresses the nation two times, to promote Iraq policy and control damage from spying revelations.
In a televised Oval Office address, Mr. Bush hails the Iraqi election as progress and said "we are winning the
war." The speech came amid bipartisan calls for congressional investigations into Bush's post-9/11
authorization of eavesdropping in the U.S. without court approval. In his radio address, following a leak, Mr.
Bush concedes the program existed and says it will continue.
Trade ministers save global talks in Hong Kong with an interim accord to end farm export subsidies by 2013
and give new support to the poorest nations.
20 Bush fights back against critics of his decisions on eavesdropping. The president, at a surprise news
conference, said he had legal power to authorize the National Security Agency to tap domestic calls as a
necessary response to the Sept. 11, 2001, attacks, that he intended to continue and that civil liberties have
been protected. He called the leaking of the spying project to the media a "shameful act."
BP and Exxon Mobil are charged by an Alaska state authority with conspiring to withhold natural gas from
U.S. markets and reinforce control over North Slope supplies. The antitrust suit alleges collusion by the two
companies has choked the flow of Alaskan natural gas in an effort to drive up prices.
21 New York wakes to its first transit strike in 25 years, making the city a sea of plodding humanity in what
will be a three-day strike.
GM shares fall to a 23-year low of $19.85, as Toyota says it will boost output, potentially surpassing GM as
the world's biggest auto maker by volume. Separately, Kirk Kerkorian's Tracinda said it has sold 12 million
GM shares, leaving it with 7.8% of the company.
22 Bush is sharply rebuked by the Fourth Circuit appeals court, which refused to transfer Padilla to civilian
custody for trial until the administration explains why its arguments to justify his indefinite detention without
charge, which the court had upheld, now no longer apply.

Former Qwest Communications International Inc. executive Marc Weisberg agrees to plead guilty to fraud

EXISTING-HOME PRICES climbed last month even though sales declined. But economists say the trend
can't last if inventories of unsold homes, especially condominiums, continue to grow. The November sales
drop was the second consecutive monthly decline. Despite the cooling, existing-home sales were on track to

Below Market's Still Waters in 2005, Performance Flowed to the Midcaps
Fund Track
Below Market's Still Waters in 2005, Performance Flowed to the Midcaps
By Diya Gullapalli
1,065 words
30 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
DON'T LET THE PAST YEAR'S sleepy stock market fool you.
Mutual-fund categories did some major flip-flopping in 2005, amid flat overall market returns in the U.S. In
one of the most significant switches, midcapitalization stocks delivered better returns than did small-cap
stocks, the reigning kings for more than five years.
All this has forced fund managers to shuffle some of their largest holdings. For individual investors, it
contributed to what can only be called a "muddle-through" year, says Bob Doll, chief investment officer for
Merrill Lynch Investment Managers -- meaning many investors spent more time chasing returns than actually
capturing them.
An individual who plunked down $10,000 on U.S. diversified stocks at the end of last year, for example, was
only $413 richer on average toward the end of this year, compared with $721 richer on average in the same
period last year, according to fund researcher Lipper Inc.
U.S. investors flocked to performance, both abroad, in regions such as Latin America and Japan, and at
home. Four of the five top-performing U.S. stock-fund categories represent narrow sectors -- for example,
utilities, real estate and energy -- rather than broadly diversified holdings, according to Morningstar Inc.,
which calculated data through Wednesday's market close.
Among U.S. stock funds, Gartmore Global Natural Resources Fund and Guinness Atkinson Global Energy
Fund have posted the highest returns -- more than 60%.
The natural-resources sector was the top-performing category this year, delivering nearly 40% in average
returns. However, "value" portfolios (which typically focus on stocks judged bargains according to measures
like the price/earnings ratio) are starting to lose a bit of their luster as "growth" stocks (companies whose
earnings are rapidly rising, for instance) have picked up steam.
"We said it would be a stock picker's year, and it was," says Don Cassidy, a senior research analyst at Lipper.
"Investors were particularly fickle and short-term driven," he adds, experimenting even with gold funds, which
had an impressive year.
A closer look at some popular categories illuminates the dangers of such behavior. While Japanese stocks
are up 34.18% this year, their average return over the past five years is 4.25%. The story is similar for
utilities, which delivered 14.3% in 2005 but less than 2% on average since 2000, according to Morningstar.
Better bets often were found abroad. World stock funds returned 17.68%, or more than twice the 7.42%
return of U.S. diversified stock funds. Both domestic and foreign funds also outperformed U.S. stock markets,
with the Dow Jones Industrial Average up only 2.47% and the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index up 5.73%
for 2005, including dividends reinvested through Wednesday, according to Lipper.
Investors seized on such trends by pouring more money into international holdings than ever before.
International investments drew more than half the net $240 billion that flowed into mutual funds this year
through November, the latest data available, up 63% from the same period in 2004, according to Financial
Research Corp., a Boston firm.
Mutual funds focused on large-cap stocks continued to idle in 2005, even as profits and cash piled up at big
blue-chip companies. Definitions for what constitutes a small-, large- and midcap stock can vary. Morningstar
classifies small-cap companies as those with a market value of less than $1 billion, and it defines a big one
as topping $5 billion. Some analysts say large-cap stocks look poised for a turnaround in coming months.

--- Page 155 ---
Page 155 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Despite flat overall mutual-fund inflows this year compared with 2004, one popular category was so-called
life-cycle or life-stage funds, which shift into more conservative holdings as clients age. The funds are picking
up steam as favored destinations for 401(k) retirement-account money, and their inflows are beginning to
close in on those of other once-favored options like exchange-traded funds.
Fund investors also continued funneling money into stock mutual funds rather than bond funds, marking an
important shift from the pattern of a few years ago. Stock funds took in a net $125 billion in the first 11 months
of 2005, the latest data available, while bond funds took in a net $33.2 billion, according to the Investment
Company Institute, a mutual-fund trade group.
Such patterns make sense given mediocre bond-fund performances in 2005. Setting aside the outsize
11.88% return of emerging-market bond funds, most long- and short-duration bond funds returned 2% to 3%,
according to Morningstar. As U.S. interest rates rose steadily in 2005, with the federal-funds target rate
reaching 4.25% in December, such returns began looking paltry even compared with those of less risky
money-market mutual funds. Investors also all but gave up on the junk-bond investments that were fairly
popular as recently as 2004.
Fund managers cautiously shuffled among asset classes and categories in 2005. Bala Iyer, manager of the
JPMorgan Investor Growth &Income Fund, says his team scaled back on its six-year overweight position in
midcap stocks after deciding the category was fairly valued in recent months. It also neutralized its 2%
overweight junk-bond position. The roughly $1.8 billion fund is up 6.07% so far this year, according to
Morningstar.
Companies tarnished by the mutual-fund-trading scandal of 2003, such as Janus Capital Group Inc. and
Putnam Investments, continued to see sizable cash outflows in 2005, while American Funds took in a net
$72.2 billion, nearly doubling the inflows at Vanguard Group Inc. and comprising 33% of the fund-industry
total through November, according to Financial Research Corp.
Fund flows were "remarkably concentrated," this year, says Kunal Kapoor, director of fund analysis at
Morningstar. "The rich kept getting richer."

Rodney Dangerfield Revisited
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Rodney Dangerfield Revisited
934 words
30 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Less than a year ago, we labeled the current U.S. expansion the "Rodney Dangerfield economy" -- because it
"gets no respect." Today our now $12.5 trillion economy has only maintained its strength even as it still gets
disparaged in the media, which continues to fret about the fragility of what has undeniably been a resilient
expansion. There are a few policy lessons here, assuming Washington is awake.
The 3.5% to 4% rate of growth in 2005 has been especially remarkable given eight Federal Reserve Board
interest rate hikes, oil prices as high as $70 a barrel, and one of the most devastating natural disasters in
American history. Yes, fourth quarter GDP may come in softer thanks to limping auto sales, but the
entrepreneurial U.S. economy will still have grown at about twice the pace of Old Europe in 2005. As
economist Michael Darda of MKM Partners, puts it: "This is the most derided and ridiculed growth cycle in
post-World War II history, even though by many measures, including productivity and corporate profits, it's
one of the most impressive."
Remember the 2004 debate over the "jobless recovery" and "outsourcing"? Here's the reality: The great
American jobs machine has averaged a net increase of nearly 200,000 new jobs a month this year. Some 4.5
million more Americans are working today than in May of 2003, before the Bush investment tax cuts. The
employment expansion in financial services, software design, medical technology and many other growth
industries dwarfs the smaller job losses in the domestic auto industry.
Even many of the widely publicized lost jobs at Delphi and General Motors are silently offset by the tens of
thousands of Americans employed at new domestic plants built by multi-national companies like Honda and
Toyota of North America. This job growth has been accompanied from 2001 to 2005 with the best rate of
labor productivity over any four-year period since the Labor Department started tracking this statistic. This
productivity revolution augurs well for higher wages in 2006.
Critics of the U.S economic model charge that income gains for workers still have not caught up with the
losses from the 2000-2001 high-tech collapse. Now they have. The Treasury Department reported last week
that "real hourly wages are up 1.1% versus the previous business cycle peak in early 2001." Workers are now
earning more per hour in real terms than they did at the height of the 1990s expansion.
The real gains for families have been in the value of their assets. In 2005 Americans owned an all-time high
level of wealth (mostly housing and stocks), valued at $50 trillion, according to the Federal Reserve. Median
household net worth is now estimated at more than $100,000. Rather than being overloaded with credit card
bills, the truth is that Americans' assets are rising in value faster than they are taking on debt.
It's not just the idle rich who are getting richer. It's the tens of millions in the middle class who could afford to
visit malls this brisk Christmas shopping season and purchase cell phones, DVD players, $2,500 flat-panel
HDTVs, $400 Xboxes, digital cameras, pink iPods and laptop computers. Hello? This isn't the buying
behavior of people who are feeling poorer.
Looking ahead to 2006, the main risks to another good year are likely to come from policy mistakes in
Washington. Higher taxes, energy price controls, anti-Chinese protectionism and incipient inflation are
always possible when politicians are in session.
Here's what's on our New Year's wish list. First, soon-to-be Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke will have to
establish his hard money credentials by snuffing out the signs of inflation caused by the Fed's
accommodating monetary policy of the last few years. The markets are already testing Mr. Bernanke's
credibility by bidding the price of gold well above $500 an ounce since his nomination. The most important
question in the next year is whether the economy's current momentum will be enough to offset Mr.
Bernanke's necessary attempt to rein in the inflationary expectations that the Fed's easy-money policy since
2001 has created.

--- Page 157 ---
Page 157 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Next, since the investment tax cuts of 2003 were one of the triggers for the surge in asset values, business
investment, and job growth, extending the 15% capital gains and dividend tax rates should be Congress's first
order of business. Opponents of those lower rates will moan about "the deficit," but the truth is that those tax
rates corresponded with a record $284 billion increase in tax revenues in Fiscal Year 2005 and a $100 billion
decline in the budget deficit. We'd also like to see Republicans begin a campaign for a simplified and
pro-growth tax reform, with New Europe's popular flat tax as a potential model.
Finally, it would be nice to see Republicans in Congress start to act as if they truly believe in limited
government by putting Uncle Sam on a low-pork budget diet. The most conspicuous blemish on the 2005
economic scorecard was frenetic federal spending, estimated to be up another $180 billion, or 8%. If
Congress were to cut that spending growth rate in half, and if the economy continues to spin off tax revenue
dividends as in 2005, the budget deficit would fall in half by this time next year. And, then, who knows, the
pessimists may run out of things to complain about and this expansion might finally get the respect it
deserves.
