Bank Ready for India's Harvest --- ICICI Is Positioned to Capitalize On Growing Middle-Class Hunger For Retail Credit Products, Services
Bank Ready for India's Harvest --- ICICI Is Positioned to Capitalize On Growing Middle-Class Hunger
For Retail Credit Products, Services
By Eric Bellman
1,182 words
29 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AS MILLIONS of Indians discover credit cards and car loans, the country's largest bank is cashing in on its
shift from staid government-directed lending to aggressive retail banking, riding a wave of consumer spending
that is transforming the subcontinent.
For most of its 50 years, ICICI Bank Ltd. acted like an arm of the socialist-minded central governments that
ran India for decades. Essentially, it was in charge of parceling out the meager amounts of foreign money
allowed into India, which was to be used for state-approved projects, at interest rates set by the authorities.
But in the past five years the bank has been reborn. With India rapidly overhauling its economy -- but still
restricting foreign banks from expanding quickly -- ICICI Bank has emulated big international names like
Citigroup Inc. and HSBC Holdings PLC to create a retail-banking empire from scratch.
The strategy has paid off. After a new stock issue in Mumbai and New York this month, ICICI Bank overtook
State Bank of India Ltd. to become India's largest bank in terms of market value and the strongest player in
the sweet spot of the Indian economy: the growing middle-class market. With the help of foreign investors,
who own 70% of the company, ICICI Bank raised $1.7 billion in a stock offering this month, including $500
million on the New York Stock Exchange.
"A few foreign banks were using a [retail] model in India, but none of them could do it on a big scale," says
Kalpana Morparia, deputy managing director of Mumbai-based ICICI. "We learned this from them, and then
just scaled it up." In 4 p.m. composite trading yesterday on the New York Stock Exchange, Icici Bank shares
gained 46 cents, or 1.6%, at $29.07. It traded at 575.95 rupees ($12.75), down 0.35%, or 2 rupees, on the
Bombay Stock Exchange.
ICICI's next target is India's rural population of 600 million people, who are only now beginning to turn to
banks. It is a risky move, given the difficulties of assessing credit-worthiness in a nation that still doesn't have
the individual credit-rating companies that are commonplace in more developed countries.
In addition, there are cultural obstacles because, traditionally, millions of India's poorest eschew banking
almost entirely, instead putting their savings into gold bangles and other jewelry. It's one reason that India
ranks as the world's biggest consumer of gold, despite an average annual salary of around $600.
In an attempt to serve these small savers, ICICI Bank is creating technologies that it hopes will enable it to
reach them without adding expensive new branches. The bank has developed a rural ATM, for example, that
will run on solar energy and accept the tattered bank notes that are commonly found in rural India.
The bank also is working with a growing national network of rural Internet kiosks so that farmers can use the
Web and teleconferencing to apply for microinsurance for their crops or $100 loans to buy water buffalo.
Foreign banks, including Citigroup, HSBC and Standard Chartered PLC, have been in India for decades, but
each has fewer than 50 branches in the nation. ICICI Bank started building a branch network in the past
decade and already has more than 600. India's banking industry was controlled by state-owned banks until
deregulation allowed local private players in the 1990s.
Today the industry is divided among innovative institutions like ICICI; small, struggling private banks; and
large and slow-moving state-run institutions like State Bank of India. SBI still ranks as the largest bank in
terms of branches, with 9,000 nationwide, but that number is in some respects a measure of the hurdles it
faces in refashioning itself. It has so many branches partly because, as a state-owned bank, it is expected to
maintain unprofitable offices in even small towns to serve the rural saver. Its powerful union also makes it
tough to close branches down.

--- Page 161 ---
Page 161 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
India's retail-banking business is expected to grow 16% a year over the next four years to $34 billion a year,
according to McKinsey &Co. While that is still less than half the $82 billion in revenue generated by Chinese
banks last year, or Citigroup's $86 billion in revenue in 2004, it is one of the world's highest growth rates for
the banking industry.
ICICI's transformation from government-guided lender to entrepreneurial outfit mirrors a coming of age for
India's economy. It demonstrates how aggressive companies there can use their newfound commercial
freedom and technology to survive and thrive.
"Because the government has a game plan to when it wants to open the sector to [foreign] competition, it
creates an interesting opportunity" for ICICI Bank, says Gopal Jain, managing director of Gaja Capital
Partners, a private-equity firm in Mumbai. The company has "turned an industrial-credit behemoth with
questionable asset quality into a retail bank."
ICICI Bank's profit has climbed on average more than 50% a year for the past five years. In the fiscal year
ended March 31, earnings jumped 22% to $461 million.
The company was born in 1955 as Industrial Credit and Investment Corp. of India. It was created with the
help of the World Bank because India needed a financial institution that could borrow money abroad. The
socialist government at the time allowed only a handful of institutions to raise funds abroad.
The government also restricted industrial production and consumption, and regulated imports and exports.
Companies would come to ICICI to borrow the dollars they needed to import equipment and raw materials
required to expand their textile mills and steel plants.
But triggered by the Indian economy's near-bankruptcy in 1991, the government began reforms that opened
the country to foreign investment and allowed Indian companies to raise money abroad. In the late 1990s,
Icici started pushing its retail-banking business. Today, ICICI Bank has distinguished itself with branches that
aren't only clean, well-lit and air-conditioned, but also computerized, which enables staff to make decisions
and dispense cash, loans and account statements more quickly than rivals.
Some industry analysts and bankers warn that Icici's game plan to add customers from lower income
brackets could backfire. If the bank tries to broaden its retail market share too widely, they contend, its
bad-loan balance could surge. ICICI also has to battle a growing number of local players like HSBC and even
State Bank of India, which are also starting to target the middle class with modern retail-banking service.
ICICI executives maintain that there is plenty more room for growth. To minimize risk, the bank has
developed innovative ways of checking on the credit-worthiness of first-time borrowers. The bank deploys an
army of surveyors to visit potential clients' homes and workplaces to confirm their addresses, assets and
salaries.

A technical breakout occurred in the most-active February futures when they moved above the area from the

as we broke through there, we saw continued fund buying," he said.

"The specs were targeting that (gap)," Mr. Boustead said. "We got some bank selling when we got into that
area."
Funds appeared to be positioning themselves for the New Year, Mr. Boustead said.
"I think they're still favorably disposed (to gold)," he said. "A little bit of dollar weakness and some strength in
crude probably helped things."

Bond Fears Push Dow Industrials Back Into the Red
Tuesday's Markets
Bond Fears Push Dow Industrials Back Into the Red
By E.S. Browning
585 words
28 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE MUCH-ANTICIPATED Santa Claus rally didn't materialize. Instead, stock investors gazed nervously at
the bond market, and didn't like what they saw.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 105.50 points, or 0.97%, to 10777.77, the sharpest point or percentage
drop in two months. With three trading days remaining in 2005, that nudged the blue chips back just barely
into the red for the year, down 0.05%.
The problem: The bond market is behaving as if the economy faces trouble. Amid expectations that economic
growth will slow and that the Federal Reserve may stop raising short-term interest rates, bond prices rose,
pushing yields downward.
Ordinarily, that would be good news, because lower yields make borrowing cheaper. But, for the first time in
four years, the yield of the 10-year Treasury note fell below the yield of the two-year note, an unusual
occurrence that in the past has sometimes signaled economic trouble ahead.
Analysts disputed whether the bond activity really was significant, but markets were rattled. The reason is that
when investors take lower interest rates for longer-term bonds than for short-term ones, it means they
expect rates to fall, a symptom of an economic slowdown.
The technical term for this is an "inverted yield curve." The 10-year note finished with a yield of 4.343%,
compared to 4.347% for the two-year.
"From a fundamental and historical standpoint, when you hear the phrase `inversion of the yield curve,' that
means the risk of recession down the road," said Jay Suskind, director of trading at brokerage firm Ryan Beck
in Florham Park, N.J. "With a lot of people off this week and with the concerns over the yield curve, the stock
market's path of least resistance seems to be to down." Although stocks could bounce back after their big
drop, he said, "I think we had our year-end rally in November."
Oil and natural-gas prices fell amid expectations for warmer weather in the Northeast, which ordinarily would
be good news but which couldn't overcome the negative mood in the market.
Reflecting the concerns, the price of gold, which benefits from times of uncertainty, rose $5.00 to $508 an
ounce. The dollar also strengthened.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.96%, or 12.12 points, to 1256.54, still up 3.7% this year.
The Nasdaq Composite Index declined 1.0%, or 22.53 points, to 2226.89, up 2.4% this year. Trading was
light.
Reports on retail sales were mixed. Reflecting the broader mood of uncertainty, retail stocks fell along with
the rest of the market.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.22%, or 0.43 point, to 198.05.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.15 billion shares traded, 1,030 stocks rose and 2,364 fell.
Bond prices gained.The 10-year Treasury note rose 8/32, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 4.343%. The 30-year bond was up 21/32 to yield 4.508%.

the dollar to $1.1834 from $1.1874.

Corporate Scandal:
HealthSouth Issues
A Progress Report
----
By Rick Brooks
NOW THAT IT'S OUT of intensive care, HealthSouth Corp. next week will take another big step in its
recovery from a staggering accounting fraud by holding its first shareholder meeting in more than three years.
Thursday's meeting at the health-care provider's headquarters in Birmingham, Ala., follows the Dec. 2 filing of
an overdue annual report for 2004 that keeps HealthSouth on track to resume filing regular quarterly reports
early next year. Shareholders last met in May 2002, shortly before a profit shortfall that spiraled into an
insider-trading probe and revelations that a slew of HealthSouth executives were cooking the books.
The meeting is expected to be brief. Item No. 2 on the agenda is a shareholder proposal recommending that
HealthSouth amend its bylaws to require that the board chairman be an independent director. Jon F. Hanson,
HealthSouth's current chairman, already meets that definition, and the company isn't opposing the proposal.
Just as significant will be what doesn't happen: HealthSouth founder Richard M. Scrushy, acquitted in June of
masterminding the fraud, says he isn't planning to attend. He is still the company's second biggest
shareholder, with 3.7 million shares in hand as of Sept. 30, or about 0.9% of the total, according to a
HealthSouth proxy statement. But he has a family vacation planned and said in an email that the family
"would be very upset if Dad told them we were not going."
That would eliminate the chance of a confrontation with President and Chief Executive Jay Grinney, whose
first 19 months on the job have focused on a forensic reconstruction of HealthSouth's books, stabilizing
operations, a management house-cleaning and carefully worded denunciations of what he calls Mr. Scrushy's
"CEO-centric" reign. The company puts the extent of the fraud at nearly $4 billion.
Mr. Scrushy was excluded from a list of 10 nominees up for election to the board, leading him to resign as a
director earlier this month. Previously, he had refused to quit, even after HealthSouth handed all boardroom
decision-making power to a special committee that included every director but him.
Still, there will be signs of the long shadow he casts over the company. Some shareholders are sure to
notice, for instance, that the meeting is being held in the former Richard M. Scrushy Conference Center,
which workers stripped of that name right after the scandal erupted.
Mr. Grinney is likely to update HealthSouth investors on efforts to restore the company to respectability, such
as relisting its stock on a major exchange. HealthSouth also plans to file its 2005 annual report so that it can
hold another shareholder meeting in the spring.
The company may be hoping its next annual meeting coincides with another Scrushy family vacation. Mr.
Scrushy earlier this month sued HealthSouth for breach of contract, claiming his acquittal means the
company had no grounds to fire him "for cause." He also tried to put a proposal on the proxy to increase the
size of the board. HealthSouth said it arrived past the deadline.
"Richard Scrushy is a figure of our past, not a figure of our present or our future," a HealthSouth spokesman
says.

A Smoother Ride
For Ski Operators
----
By George Anders
SKI-RESORT OPERATORS, who spend months planning for the post-Christmas rush to the slopes, have
been jinxed by melting snow and bare mountains the past few years. But this season might be different.
Many resorts on the East Coast and in the Rockies have been deluged with snow in recent weeks. Ski
conditions lately have been excellent, and resort operators say next week could see some of the most brisk
ski traffic ever, barring any nasty weather surprises.
The ski-resort industry needs the help. Visitor traffic in the U.S. has grown just 13% since the 1981-82
season, according to the National Ski Areas Association.
Last year was especially frustrating for some East Coast resorts. Warm weather delayed the start of the ski
season. Then fierce storms right after Christmas closed many runs.
"It was disastrous," says Mike Colbourn, vice president for sales and marketing at Stowe Mountain Resort in
Vermont. By contrast, he says, visitor traffic so far this season is up 30%.
In Tannersville, Pa., Camelback Ski Area opened all 33 of its trails this month, compared with just 14 during
the start of last year's warm, rainy season. "We haven't had them all open this early in a long, long time," says
Craig Low, a Camelback spokesman.
Officials at Jackson Hole Mountain Resort in Wyoming say their outlook has brightened, too. Winter room
reservations for their town of Teton Village -- which are made almost entirely by skiers -- are running 29%
ahead of last year's pace. January airplane bookings are up 15% as well, they add.
Only California resort operators are still biting their nails. Snowfall in the Sierras was sparse until
mid-December, forcing resorts to rely more on man-made snow. Recent storms brought a mix of snow and
rain. As a result, some Sierra resorts haven't been able to open their most challenging runs at the tops of
mountains.
Snow conditions play the biggest role in influencing ski traffic, but the overall economy and public attitudes
toward travel matter, too. Since the 1970s, some of the biggest slumps in ski traffic have come during
recession years. Business suffered in the 2001-02 season as well, when public anxieties about air safety
were most intense.
This year the economy is strong and terrorism-related fears have abated somewhat. What's more, says Jeff
Jones, chief financial officer of Vail Resorts Inc. in Colorado, recent advances in ski design make it easier for
aging baby boomers to stay on the slopes without too much pain. That should help resorts attract a wider age
range of visitors, he says.
While better snow may be the norm this year, steeper prices will be part of the experience, too. Resorts have
raised the price of a single-day lift ticket anywhere from 4% to 15%, in part because of higher energy costs
associated with snow grooming and lift operations.
Japan:
Hoping the Price
Is Finally Right
----
By Sebastian Moffett
AFTER THREE YEARS of mild expansion, rising profits and stock market gains, Japan may be ready for a
formal end to years of economic frustration.
On Tuesday, the government announces consumer price data for November. Economists' consensus is that
the core consumer price index -- store prices without the effects of volatile fresh food -- will have risen over
the previous year.

--- Page 169 ---
Page 169 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
There have been several one-off increases in the past few years. But this time, with the economy in Japan
growing, there's a decent chance that core CPI will continue rising -- something that hasn't happened in
seven years. Normally of interest only to economists, the data could thus mark a historic turning point for
Japan.
Falling prices might sound great in a country like Japan, which has long known for the astronomical cost of
everything from apples to houses. But falling prices depress an economy. Consumers feel poorer because
their homes and stock portfolios are worth less. They also put off spending in anticipation of future cheaper
goods. Producers, meanwhile, see thinner revenue in prospect, and so don't invest so much in production
equipment.
The overall effect is a mild version of the Great Depression, and it has drained the vigor from Japan's
economy for years.
But the economy has been growing again recently. Price increases on products from tires to fish have come
as companies and consumers get more comfortable about spending again. The Bank of Japan said recently it
believes an end to deflation is close.
That likelihood is sparking renewed optimism. The government forecasts Japan's economy will grow 2.7% in
the fiscal year ending March 31, and 1.9% the next year -- not spectacular, but a vast improvement on the
recovery-recession cycles of the 1990s.
Japanese growth helps other countries because Japan is more open to foreigners than in the past.
Deregulation has made it easier to send exports there, while Japanese companies have sought lower-cost
suppliers overseas. Japanese growth could boost economies elsewhere in Asia by between 0.4 and 1.7
percentage points from 2004 to 2006, according to Goldman Sachs.
Still, the end of falling prices presents a challenge for Japan's central bank too. The Bank of Japan is widely
criticized for having tightened monetary policy too early in the past. In 1999, it cut the target for the lending
rate that banks charge each other virtually to zero, in order to encourage borrowing. But the bank quit this
policy in 2000, thinking the economy had recovered, and after that, Japan suffered more deflation and
recession.
This time, the escape from deflation is proceeding at a snail's pace. Economists forecast 0.1% year-on-year
growth in core CPI for November and December -- or at least higher prices in 2006. But rises are expected to
remain well below 1% for the next year or so.
Politicians worry the central bank will prematurely end its super-easy monetary policy, halting the gathering
momentum of the economy. The ruling Liberal Democratic Party earlier this month set up a monetary policy
subcommittee, which has demanded a target for "reflating" the economy -- either an inflation target like that
of the European Central Bank, or else a target for economic growth including price rises. Some lawmakers
advocate changing the law to force the bank to adopt such a goal.
"Unless they show us some clear objective target, they will not have persuasive power," says subcommittee
chairman Kozo Yamamoto. "I think we should increase transparency and show clear responsibility."
Natural Gas:
A Cold Case
In Futures Markets
----
By Russell Gold
THE END OF THE YEAR is a nerve-wracking time for natural-gas traders, and this year is no exception.
After hitting a record earlier this month, gas prices have been tumbling in recent days. That's good news for
homeowners with gas-fired furnaces, but they're not out of the woods yet. If the winter shapes up to be cold,
gas prices are likely to begin climbing again in the coming days and weeks.
A quick primer on the natural-gas market: Each year, utilities prepare for peak winter demand by socking
away gas during the summer and fall, storing it in underground caverns and then pulling it out during the cold
snaps.
By the end of December, traders have a decent handle on whether there's adequate gas in storage to get the
U.S. through the winter without price spikes. Traders can begin to ask: is there too much gas in storage,
which will drive down prices, or too little, which could produce a spike?

--- Page 170 ---
Page 170 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
It's no coincidence that one of the great recent natural-gas price spikes occurred in the last week of
December in 2000. Cold weather and larger-than-expected withdrawals from storage got traders nervous.
Prices climbed quickly, soaring 57% in a month to peak at $9.98 per million British thermal units on Dec. 27th.
This year, it's going the other way. Natural-gas prices on the futures market hit a record, inflation-adjusted
high earlier this month of $15.38 per million British thermal units. Since then, prices have plummeted. On
Thursday, a weekly federal report that less gas than expected had been pulled out of storage sent prices
tumbling, down $1.39 to settle at $12.92. On Friday, it fell an additional 64 cents to $12.28.
Traders will keep a close eye on the final storage data of the year, due this Thursday morning in an attempt to
discern a trend.
Many people see a coming run-up in the gas markets. The reason: It's been cold. The first three weeks of
December were the coldest in New York and Boston since 1989, says Michael Schlacter, chief meteorologist
at Weather 2000 Inc. He expects January to be colder than most traders expect -- and he's been telling that
to a cadre of traders who pay for his long-range climatologic forecast. "I think we're in for it," he says. Mr.
Schlacter's prediction runs counter to the National Weather Service, which predicted a warming trend in
January.
The recent drop in gas prices suggests traders also expect a warmish January. If the jet stream doesn't
cooperate and cold weather blankets New England and the Midwest, prices will surge again. "If we have a
January like the December we've had, people will be very worried," says Gerry Saccente, a natural-gas trader
for ABN Amro. "Then maybe $18 or $19 [per million BTUs] won't even be crazy," he says.

A Taste of Paris
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
A Taste of Paris
666 words
21 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Yesterday the 33,000 New York City transit workers presented their Christmas gift to their seven million
customers: an illegal strike that has paralyzed the city they "serve."
One predictable result has been gridlock. During one of the most frigid mornings of the year, the Brooklyn
Bridge was clogged, not just with cars, but with hordes of New Yorkers on foot, in many cases walking miles
to work. The strike is walloping the city's economy with an estimated $400 million of lost commerce each day
the strike persists. Retailers are missing out on the busiest shopping days of the year.
Make no mistake: This strike is a carefully timed union strategy to inflict maximum economic damage in
exchange for maximum extortion at the bargaining table. The Transport Workers Union is playing hardball,
and we only wish New York Governor George Pataki and other officials representing the state's commuters
and taxpayers would be as tough. But since public-employee unions are among the largest donors to the
coffers of the very politicians with whom they are negotiating, it's not surprising that the union bosses have full
confidence that the state will eventually pay their ransom.
No doubt adding to union bravado is the knowledge that city and state officials have for decades caved to
virtually every union demand. Mr. Pataki famously buckled to Dennis Rivera and the health-care workers
union -- offering a $1.8 billion payoff before the 2002 election -- even as the state was awash in red ink. New
York Mayor Mike Bloomberg failed to use the fiscal crisis after September 11 to drive any changes in
public-sector work rules. This is how the unions are repaying them.
The average salary for a New York bus driver is $63,000 a year -- about 25% more than for similar
private-sector workers. Over the next three years the union wants a package that would raise salaries about
three times the rate of inflation. The real union plum is a "25-55" rule that allows retirement at half salary at
age 55 after just 25 years of service. The state wants to raise the retirement age to 62; the union said
fuhgeddaboutit.
There's also the gold-plated health-care retirement package, which will saddle the city and state with billions
of dollars of future unfunded outlays. (No one knows for sure the real amount because there has never been
an audit of the agency's health-care pensions finances.) Current employees and most retirees pay nothing for
health-care benefits, and the state is merely asking all new workers to contribute 1% of salary to offset the
costs. Again the union scoffed.
On the opposite page, Steven Malanga argues for firing the workers and hiring replacements. That's fine by
us. But if that's too much for Messrs. Pataki and Bloomberg, they should at least announce that any fines
imposed on the unions by the courts (such as the $1 million assessed yesterday) will under no circumstances
be negotiated away in any settlement.
Better yet, they could use this strike as an opportunity to end the public transit monopoly by legalizing all
forms of private competition -- including jitneys. Los Angeles, Denver and San Diego have all saved money
by contracting out transit services to private bidders, and New York could do the same.
Disabling strikes of this sort are what happens to cities that become dominated by public-employee unions.
Political leaders operate as if employers (taxpayers) serve the employees (unions), not vice versa. Labor
leaders begin to think they can get away with anything. Meanwhile, the tax burden rises (New York's is nearly
twice the national average) while the tax base shrinks. Just as in France, the unions punish a city and pay no
price for it. Will New York's political leaders let them get away with it again?
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Spitzer, Where Art Thou?" -- WSJ Dec. 22, 2005)

The Wall Street Journal

Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SUBWAYS AND BUSES STALLED across New York City, and stocks did the same.
U.S. Investors fretted about a strong housing report that could portend further interest-rate increases. The
Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 30.98 points, or 0.29%, to 10805.55. The broader Standard &Poor's
500-stock Index retreated 0.02% to 1259.62, while the technology-heavy Nasdaq Composite Index slipped
0.01% to 2222.42.
General Motors, one of the 30 Dow index stocks, dropped 5.7% to $19.85, a multiyear low. Toyota Motor set
a production forecast for 2006 that some analysts predict will put the Japanese auto titan ahead of GM as the
world's No. 1 producer of cars.
The New York transit strike inconvenienced millions of commuters and hampered some local businesses, but
economic and financial-market ripple effects are expected to be contained unless the strike is prolonged.
But a strong economic piece of data knocked stocks off their game yesterday. In the familiar Wall Street
paradox, good news became bad when the Commerce Department said the housing market surged in
November. Construction of new homes jumped a unexpectedly sharp 5.3%.
That is "a little bit of a warning sign," said Peter Hooper, chief U.S. economist for Deutsche Bank. With
greater strength in housing, the Federal Reserve's policy of raising short-term rates "may not be turning
around as soon as expected and that's a negative for stocks."
Tempering that concern was a tame inflation report. Falling energy prices pushed the producer-price index
for finished goods down 0.7%. Excluding volatile food and energy prices, the index rose just 0.1% in
November following a 0.3% decline in October.
Yesterday, oil futures broke a four-day slide, rising 64 cents to $57.98 a barrel, and gold fell $9 an ounce, to
$494.60, the first close below $500 this month.
"We saw a pretty muted response" to the inflation data, said Mike Rutigliano, an independent floor broker on
the New York Stock Exchange. "Aside from special situations" in individual stocks, "it's going to be a pretty
slow week."
Holiday vacations in the days leading up to the weekend are the main cause for that slowness, though the
transit walkout added to that.
While there were no reports of major trading problems, stocks couldn't muster much strength. The rally that
some expected to push the Dow over 11000 this year has only seven trading days left to materialize, and
skeptics on stocks say most of the year-end rally probably took place in November.
Outside the U.S., stocks fell in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks fell
0.15%, or 0.29 points, to 196.49. In major U.S. market action:
Stocks eased. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.47 billion shares traded, 1,662 issues rose and
1,650 declined.
Bond prices fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 7/32, or $2.19 cents for every $1,000 invested, to yield
4.466%. The 30-year Treasury bond fell 9/32 to yield 4.4657%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, the currency was at 117.12 yen, up from 116.04 yen on Monday,
while the euro fell against the dollar to $1.1864 from $1.2009.

Bidding War Chills U.S. Plan To Import Gas
Bidding War Chills U.S. Plan To Import Gas
By Russell Gold
1,305 words
19 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
EVEN WITH natural-gas prices surging to new heights and heating bills soaring across the U.S., much of the
nation's import capacity remains idle.
The nation has four onshore terminals for receiving and processing imported gas, and they are importing only
about half the volume they can handle. The reason: U.S. buyers are being aggressively outbid by Europeans
and Asians for the limited number of cargoes available.
The supply crunch means imports won't cool the U.S. market and natural-gas prices could stay high -- and
sensitive to weather changes -- for years to come, even as the U.S. builds more terminals to handle overseas
gas.
"There will be continued competition for supply, certainly through the end of the decade," says Martin
Houston, president of North American operations for BG Group PLC, the largest importer of liquefied natural
gas into the U.S.
In the U.S., natural-gas prices are up fivefold since the beginning of the decade, and near records in both
nominal and inflation-adjusted terms. At one point early last week, prices hit an inflation-adjusted record of
$15.38 per million British thermal units.
They dropped quickly Thursday after models predicted warmer-than-average weather after Christmas,
despite news that a surprisingly large volume of gas had been drawn out of underground storage due to the
recent U.S. cold snap. Friday, natural gas closed down 1.1% at $13.633 per million BTUs.
About 57% of the nation's 110 million households use natural gas for heat, according to the Census Bureau.
The Energy Department forecasts that the average U.S. household that heats primarily with natural gas will
spend 38% more this year, compared with a projected 21% increase for users of home heating oil.
Natural gas also is used for such purposes as generating electricity and producing plastics and fertilizer.
Demand has grown amid a strengthening economy and interest in cleaner-burning fuel.
High prices are one reason big producers are looking to boost North American gas production. Last week,
ConocoPhillips said it would pay $35.6 billion to acquire Burlington Resources Inc. in the largest energy
acquisition in years. Eighty percent of Burlington's assets are North American gas.
But imports also are key. While the majority of natural gas consumed in the U.S. comes from North American
wells, many fields are aging and the industry has found it difficult to boost production. With domestic
production leveled off, the energy industry expected to compensate with imports from the Middle East and
Africa, where excess supplies of the fuel are sometimes flared off and never brought to market.
Still, shipping natural gas is a tricky process. The gas is shipped in cold liquefied form, which dramatically
boosts volume and must be reheated and returned to a gaseous form at terminals when received.
The current import conundrum dates to the beginning of this decade, when prices spiked -- at a time when
production was flat -- and government and industry officials began pushing to increase natural-gas supply.
In 2001, the industry began the process of reopening mothballed liquefied natural-gas terminals and
proposed building dozens of new ones. The Bush administration backed the effort, and the federal
government streamlined the regulatory process. Companies campaigned to persuade communities to allow
them to build terminals, often in the face of local opposition. After facing federal reviews, the lengthy process
of building new terminals has begun.
The theory was that if the U.S., the world's largest gas consumer, opened for imports, there would be tankers
lining up to discharge their cargo. There was "a self-indulgent, myopic belief that if the U.S. builds a terminal,

--- Page 183 ---
Page 183 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
everyone wants to supply us. And that is what has been wrong," says James Jensen, an industry consultant
in Weston, Mass.
Instead, a pressing global shortage has developed, in part because of overseas competition. As the price of
liquefying natural gas fell, a global building boom began. While supply increased and the number of cargoes
available for purchase on the spot market grew, so too did the number of new import terminals in other
countries.
Global production capacity for natural gas, in liquefied form, is about 20 billion cubic feet a day, but there are
now enough terminals around the globe to eat up twice that volume, according to the Federal Energy
Regulatory Commission.
As a result, a global shortage has developed in recent months, amid supply glitches, cold weather in the
United Kingdom and a drought in Spain, which has been turning to liquefied natural gas to make up for a
shortfall in hydroelectric power.
In an extreme example of the situation, a tanker carrying liquefied natural gas last month arrived from Nigeria
and idled in the Gulf of Mexico for a week -- during which prices soared in Europe -- before sailing to Spain to
unload its cargo. Recently, the Spanish have been willing to pay $2 to $3 per million BTUs above Gulf Coast
spot prices, according to PIRA Energy Group, a New York consultant. South Koreans, meanwhile, are paying
a premium of about $2 and the British a premium of $2 to $6.
The yawning gap underscores the differences between the oil and gas markets. Oil can be easily loaded onto
a tanker and sold into a well-developed global market that has been fine-tuned over decades. Natural gas, by
contrast, is difficult to transport except by pipeline, and LNG shipments are still a relatively small portion of the
overall market. That means the price can vary widely by region, depending on factors such as weather and
the availability of alternative fuels.
The international spot-market trade in liquefied natural gas is relatively new. Until recently, consumers got
natural gas through a collection of regional pipeline hubs, which created geographically isolated markets.
Gas-short nations such as Japan and South Korea bought natural gas under iron-clad long-term contracts.
Geography puts the U.S. at a disadvantage. Most supplies of liquefied natural gas for Europe and the U.S.
come from West Africa, the Mediterranean and the Middle East. Europe is closer, which makes delivery less
expensive. The only supplier close to the U.S. is in Trinidad.
Moreover, European buyers currently are willing to pay such a high price that a tanker from Trinidad arrived in
the U.K. Tuesday, according to Waterbourne LNG, a weekly publication of Houston energy consulting firm
Commercial Services Co. The voyage marked one of the first times liquefied natural gas from the Caribbean
had crossed the Atlantic in pursuit of higher prices.
The U.S. also faces the problem of being the newest entrant in the market and still a small purchaser. "The
larger buyers, the ones with a long history of being in the business and making consistent purchases, are
now in the position where they need more," says Rick Grant, president and chief executive at Suez LNG, a
subsidiary of French utility company Suez SA that operates an LNG terminal near Boston. "And because of
the relationships, they can compete very aggressively in the market for the cargoes that become available."
U.S. officials believe new terminals will attract significantly more imports starting in 2008, when gas from new
projects is expected to hit the market. The increased supplies are likely to allow for long-term supply
contracts, reducing dependence on the volatile spot market.
"We are 25% of the natural-gas market world-wide," says J. Mark Robinson, director of FERC's Office of
Energy Projects. "We are the big market. On an individual cargo, you might get outbid by Spain, but if you
want a long-term monetization of your reserves, I think the U.S. is the place to do that."

Sugar Futures Reach 10 1/2-Year High
Commodities Report
Sugar Futures Reach 10 1/2-Year High
By Susan Buchanan Dow Jones Newswires
496 words
17 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
World raw sugar futures raced to 10 1/2-year highs on the New York Board of Trade Friday as commodity
funds and trade houses bought, while selling from producers was limited, brokers said.
In top-grower Brazil, the nearly completed, center-south harvest and the advancing northern harvest are both
smaller than expected because of dry weather, traders said.
The New York Board of Trade March contract gained 0.33 cents to 14.20 cents a pound after reaching 14.31
cents, the highest for a front month since April 1995.
"The market's been very speculative, an unstoppable train, as funds buy," said Sergio Alvarez, vice president
at Hencorp Sugar Group in Miami, Fla. "Trade houses are buying too and the Nybot floor was a frenzy today,"
with volume heavy in sugar futures and options.
Sugar is a raw material in ethanol fuel. Brazilian cane-ethanol prices have climbed in December, with
supplies slated to tighten once the center-south crop is in the bins, traders said. Local ethanol demand is
growing as Brazilians buy more flexible-fuel cars while crude oil prices remain strong.
Traders noted that Thailand's 2005-06 cane crop, being crushed now, is at least 11% smaller than last
season because of drought.
Also buoying the New York futures market are European Union sugar reforms, announced last month, that
will slash the region's exports and help Brazil and other shippers, starting in second-half 2006, traders said.
"Sugar futures have risen by more than 50% since January, and we're in a new environment with strength in
gold and other commodities and a definite whiff of inflation in the air," said Bruce Cleary of brokerage J&F
Commodities. "Funds that already made money in the sugar market are riding the move higher" and New
York Board of Trade open interest is the highest ever at over 522,000 lots, he observed.
Open interest is the number of positions outstanding at the end of the day. Large open interest is a sign of
liquidity in a market.
London trade house ED&F Man recently predicted a world production deficit in 2005-06 for the third straight
year as expected sugar output of 147 million tons falls short of demand at 150 million.
In other commodity markets:
COPPER: Prices on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange rose amid a drop in Shanghai
Futures Exchange copper stocks and a strike by copper railway workers in top-producer Chile underpins the
tight supply situation in the market. Front-month December settled up 2.80 cents at $2.1695 per pound.
CRUDE OIL: Futures hit a two-week low in a bout of profit-taking. Petroleum inventories remain at a high
level, while temperatures are forecast to rise in the near term. The January contract on Nymex settled at
$58.06 a barrel, down $1.93.

Home -- Furniture: The Really Big Sleep --- These new beds are triple the area of a king; Will they fit through the door?
Home -- Furniture: The Really Big Sleep --- These new beds are triple the area of a king; Will they fit
through the door?
By Candace Jackson
1,021 words
17 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
P4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
A karat is a unit of measure for the fineness of
gold, while a carat is a unit of weight for precious stones. An earlier version
of this article incorrectly used carat when referring to gold.
(WSJ Jan. 30, 2016)
WHAT'S WIDER than a Hummer and longer than a freshwater crocodile? Michael Pollack's new bed.
Mr. Pollack, a Phoenix-area commercial-real-estate developer, says his 10-foot-by-9-foot super ultraking is
big enough for his family of five to stretch out on and watch TV: "I bet you could fit the whole neighborhood."
With Americans' houses getting bigger and sleep getting more precious, custom-made beds with names such
as ultraking, superultra and extreme ultra are filling a small but expensive niche. These beds can be
anywhere from 7 to more than 12 feet wide -- that's almost triple the area of a regular king-size bed. Prices
range from about $3,600 for a mattress -- or about double the cost of some regular kings -- to $16,863 for a
mattress with a 5-inch pillow top, box spring and frame.
Then there are the linens. Mr. Pollack says he paid $4,000 for three sets of sheets, a duvet and pillows. Ed
Franklin, a co-owner of Spokane, Wash.'s Specialty Linens Store, a retailer that makes custom bedding for
clients with oddly shaped or extra-large beds, says giant linens cost up to four times as much as
standard-size bedding made of comparable materials.
Oversize frames can add an additional several thousand dollars -- and that's assuming you can find a style
you like. A $74,000 12-foot-by-10-foot bed dubbed the Grande Venetian has become the best-seller at Phyllis
Morris, a West Hollywood, Calif., furniture retailer. The hand-made set has Corinthian columns and a choice
of finishes, including 22-carat gold leaf.
While some people are delighted to have more space separating them from the person they're sleeping with,
others say it can get a little lonely. Kira Wilson Gould, a former interior designer and the author of "Big Home,
Big Challenge," cautions against going overboard on bed size. "You don't want to feel like a dwarf in your own
home," she says.
There's also a practical problem with larger beds: Some really big models can't fit through regular doors,
which are common even in sprawling new houses. On its Web site, Ultra King Beds and Linens, which has
showrooms in Beverly Hills, Calif., and Las Vegas, says a "patented design allows this bed system to fit
through a standard doorway." Translation: It's shipped in two pieces and includes a custom pillow top to hold
them together.
People of Shaquille O'Neal size may buy these giant beds out of necessity; there isn't much wiggle room for a
7-footer in a California king. But lately homeowners of unexceptional size are taking an interest in
exceptionally large furniture. Some are hoping for a better night's rest. Others just want more room to spread
out. "People like the uniqueness," says Larry Wolfe, owner of Phoenix-based Sleep EZ, which sells
everything from 8-foot-long ultrakings to circular beds.
While thicker mattresses hit the market a few years ago, industry observers attribute the growth in new
oversize beds to the continuing impact of the McMansion boom. The National Association of Home Builders
in Washington says the square footage of newly constructed homes is up 13% in the past 10 years. That has
fed a taste for all kinds of oversize furniture, from block-long sectional sofas to massive dining-room tables.
Builder WCI Communities of Bonita Springs, Fla., says that master bedrooms in its new houses in Florida
now range from 900 to 1,200 square feet, up from 650 to 800 square feet seven years ago. And in New

--- Page 189 ---
Page 189 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Jersey, its average master-bedroom size is now about 1,000 square feet, twice the size of bedrooms in
homes built 10 years ago.
Beds have been getting bigger since queens were introduced in the early 1960s. According to the
International Sleep Products Association, kings -- typically about 6.5 feet square -- are one of the
fastest-growing segments of the U.S. mattress market. Kings had a market share of 9.9% in 2004, up from
8.6% in 2000. Twin and full-size beds both declined by about three percentage points, to 27% and 18%,
respectively, over the same period. The enormous custom-made ultras and ultra extremes are still a tiny part
of the overall market.
Many mass furniture and mattress makers still cap bed sizes at the California king. And sleep experts say
there's no scientific evidence that a bigger bed has any health benefits. Sealy says it has no plans under way
to make a bigger bed. Serta says it considered making a bed larger than a king, but decided against it
because the company couldn't coordinate with sheet makers.
But some manufacturers, such as Minneapolis-based Select Comfort, have rolled out larger-size beds. Select
introduced a "grand" king in 2003 that's 30% larger than a California king. "Most people's bedrooms can't fit
anything bigger," says company spokeswoman Heather Martin. As of October, grand king sales are up 9.5%
from a year ago. The company attributes the increase to a marketing campaign featuring the Minnesota
Timberwolves' 6-foot-11-inch star Kevin Garnett and to displays in the chain's new, larger showrooms.
Vanity can come into play, too. As supersize master suites have become more elaborate -- with his-and-her
bathrooms, gyms, sitting rooms and even kitchenettes -- homeowners are likelier to show them off to guests.
"Our clients always like to show people their bedrooms," says Roy Sklarin, an interior designer based in Los
Angeles who worked with Mr. Pollack. "They have super-big bedrooms, and now they have super-big beds."

Conoco Chief Aims to Stem Fallout From Burlington Bid
Conoco Chief Aims to Stem Fallout From Burlington Bid
By Russell Gold
333 words
16 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ConocoPhillips, facing criticism for offering too much for Burlington Resources Inc., is defending the deal as a
rare opportunity to acquire valuable North American natural-gas assets.
In a statement, ConocoPhillips chairman and chief executive James Mulva admitted he is paying "a full price"
for the company. The proposed $35 billion cash-and-stock acquisition is the largest energy-sector deal in
years and comes as natural-gas prices set an inflation-adjusted record this week. But Mr. Mulva also said
that "access to quality long-term resources has become much more difficult and expensive. We are in an
extremely competitive environment and a portfolio of assets of Burlington's quality cannot be replicated."
ConocoPhillips shares have been hammered since The Wall Street Journal reported Sunday it was in talks.
ConocoPhillips's stock closed yesterday at $58.77, down 6.8% since Friday. Analysts have been critical of the
deal. Houston investment bank Sanders Morris Harris and A.G. Edwards both downgraded the company to
hold from buy.
Mr. Mulva said ConocoPhillips was analyzing whether to use hedges to "either lock in prices or mitigate the
potential downside of a reduction in gas prices." This tactic is commonly employed by smaller oil and gas
producers, but bigger companies often don't hedge so that they'll get full advantage from price gains. Large
institutional investors are pressing ConocoPhillips to consider the move, said a person familiar with the
matter.
Strong demand and limited North American supply of natural gas should keep prices strong into the next
decade, Mr. Mulva said. He said he didn't believe increased imports from overseas, in the form of
super-cooled liquefied natural gas, or a pipeline from either Alaska or northern Canada, would materialize
until 2010 at the earliest.
Mr. Mulva predicted excess cash flow would allow ConocoPhillips to lower its debt-to-capital ratio to between
18% to 23% by the end of 2006.

Stocks, Bonds Ease as Economic Data Reinforce Concerns About Inflation
Thursday's Markets
Stocks, Bonds Ease as Economic Data Reinforce Concerns About Inflation
By E.S. Browning
636 words
16 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS TOOK A BREAK after two days of stock buying, turning cautious as inflation worries cropped
up again.
After rising on Tuesday and Wednesday after the Federal Reserve indicated that it might stop raising interest
rates before too long, the Dow Jones Industrial Average edged backward by 1.84 points, or 0.02%, to
10881.67. The Dow still is up 0.9% for the year.
Other stock indexes also staged mild retreats, with the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index edging back from
the 4 1/2-year high hit on Wednesday.
Investors focused on the consumer-price index, whose "core" reading worried some people. The overall index
fell heavily in November as gasoline prices plunged.
But the core index -- excluding volatile energy and food -- rose. It was up more than 2% in the past year, at
the high end of what the Fed is said to consider acceptable.
That suggested that the year's high energy prices are affecting other parts of the economy, even if they were
down in November. It fueled worries that the Fed could decide to raise interest rates more than had been
hoped, to keep the economy from overheating.
Treasury-bond prices also retreated a bit, pushing yields higher. Rising interest rates are bad for existing
bonds because they mean that bonds issued in the future will carry more attractive interest rates.
Traders said the stock and bond pullback was to be expected, especially given the day's mixed economic
news. On top of the consumer-price news, November industrial production was stronger than expected,
reinforcing the inflation worries.
A measure of manufacturing activity in New York hit a 17-month high, but a report on business activity in the
Philadelphia region was a little soft. The net effect was a mild decline in the markets.
"It has been a really good move since the October lows," with the Dow up more than 6% and the S&P 500 up
8%, said Stephen Sachs, director of trading at mutual-fund group Rydex Investments in Rockville, Md.
It is normal for the market to slow down now, he said, adding, "I do believe that this rally has some legs
between now and the end of the year."
Gold futures continued their recent retreat from a 24-year high. Crude-oil futures fell 86 cents to $59.99 a
barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.09%, or 1.96 points, to 2260.63, but it is up 3.9% this year. The S&P 500
declined 0.14%, or 1.80 points, to 1270.94 but up 4.9% for 2005.
A $10.1 billion damage judgment in Illinois against cigarette maker Altria, a component of the Dow industrials,
was quashed on appeal. Altria, formerly known as Philip Morris, jumped almost 4%, supporting the Dow.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms, including another sharp pullback in the roaring Tokyo
market, which had been hitting five-year highs. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
dropped 0.75%, or 1.48 points, to 195.30.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.61 billion shares traded, 1,236 stocks rose and
2,115 fell.

Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 6/32, or $1.88 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.470%. The 30-year bond was down 13/32 to yield 4.678%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, the currency traded at 116.32 yen, down from 117.36 yen
Wednesday, while the euro fell against the dollar to $1.1971 from $1.2002.

Dow Industrials Rise 59.79 to 10883.51 As Interest-Rate Optimism Continues
Wednesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Rise 59.79 to 10883.51 As Interest-Rate Optimism Continues
By E.S. Browning
535 words
15 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A SECOND DAY OF OPTIMISM about the interest-rate outlook sent the Dow Jones Industrial Average closer
to 11000 and pushed the Standard &Poor's 500-stock Index to its highest finish in 4 1/2 years.
The gains came a day after the Federal Reserve raised short-term interest rates for a 13th time, but signaled
in a statement that it could end its 17-month campaign of interest-rate increases sometime next year.
The Dow industrials rose 59.79 points, or 0.55%, to 10883.51. They are up 0.9% this year, again nearing the
4 1/2-year high of 10940.55, hit in March. The S&P 500 advanced 0.42%, or 5.31 points, to 1272.74, its
highest finish since before the terrorist attacks of Sept. 11, 2001. It is up 5% in 2005.
The Dow Jones Wilshire 5000 index, which includes almost all companies based in the U.S., rose 0.36% to
12755.58, a five-year high.
Financial stocks were among the leaders. They benefit from an end to interest-rate increases because higher
rates mean higher costs for them.
"The feeling is that, as we are getting toward the end of these rate hikes, the financials ought to do better,"
said Tim Heekin, director of trading at San Francisco brokerage firm Thomas Weisel Partners. More
generally, an end to interest-rate increases is good for the economy, relieving pressures on both consumers
and businesses.
Technology stocks, however, pulled back, sending the Nasdaq Composite Index down 0.11%, or 2.41 points,
to 2262.59, still up 4% this year. Downgrades of Apple Computer by two brokerage firms induced selling in a
variety of technology shares, as investors took profits after a series of strong gains.
The government announced a record trade deficit for October, but prices of imported goods fell, reflecting
declines in oil prices. That helped assuage inflation fears.
Treasury bond prices rose, helped by the interest-rate hopes.
But the dollar fell 2.3% against the yen, its sharpest percentage decline in three years, after a Japanese
report indicated strong business confidence there. Oil pulled back and gold suffered its biggest one-day
decline in a year as traders cashed in some gains after the commodity hit a 24-year high Monday.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms despite a pullback in Tokyo. The Dow Jones World Stock
Index, excluding U.S. stocks, rose 0.55%, or 1.07 points, to 196.78.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.64 billion shares traded, 2,035 stocks rose
and 1,300 fell.
Bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 19/32, or $5.94 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 4.448%. The 30-year bond was up 1 3/32 to yield 4.654%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 117.36 yen, down from 120.12, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.2002 from $1.1929.

Greenspan's Parting Shot? --- DJIA Jumps 55.95 Points as Fed Raises Rates But Hints at an End; Next Meeting Is His Last
Tuesday's Markets
Greenspan's Parting Shot? --- DJIA Jumps 55.95 Points as Fed Raises Rates But Hints at an End;
Next Meeting Is His Last
By E.S. Browning
821 words
14 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS APPLAUDED the Federal Reserve's latest announcement on interest rates, but by day's end,
the applause began to fade.
Stocks initially surged after the Fed raised its target short-term interest rate and dropped a strong hint that it
is nearing the end of its 17-month campaign of rate increases.
But as the day wore on, some analysts warned that the Fed probably still has two or more rate increases to
come next year. And some money managers took advantage of rising stock prices during the day to sell and
lock in results before the year ends, which also helped push stocks back down.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which had been up as much as 103.74 after the Fed news, finished with a
gain of 55.95 points, or 0.52%, at 10823.72. The Dow again is moving within sight of the 11000 mark, but it is
up just 0.4% this year, and remains more than 100 points off the 4 1/2-year high of 10940.55 hit in March.
The Nasdaq Composite Index and Standard &Poor's 500-stock index both neared their own recent 4 1/2-year
highs, although the late-day selling left them just short of those marks. Trading was heavy.
Even many bullish investors said they expect one or two more Fed rate increases, and that helped keep a lid
on the optimism.
"I do think the Fed has told us: Not many more," said Alfred Kugel, chief investment strategist at Chicago
investment-management firm Atlantic Trust/Stein Roe. He expects the Fed to boost its target rate twice more
next year. The Fed pushed the target, which influences the rate for overnight bank lending, to 4.25%
yesterday, and Mr. Kugel said he thought it could go as high as 4.75% next year.
The prospect of an end to rate increases is good news for the economy, and "that means that we could have
a continuation of the year-end rally. Not a big one, but some," Mr. Kugel said.
As usual, banks including J.P. Morgan Chase, Wachovia and Wells Fargo immediately raised the prime rate,
the base rate for many consumer and business loans, by a quarter point, to 7.25%. The higher prime rate is
an almost automatic response to a Fed move. It is one way that the Fed rate increases translate into
real-world slowing of the economy, by making it more expensive for consumers and businesses to borrow.
Treasury bond prices rose amid expectations that the Fed's target interest rate won't go up much further.
The dollar, which had fallen in anticipation of a Fed signal that its rate increases were coming to an end,
recovered somewhat as analysts concluded that there could be further increases next year. The talk of an
end to Fed rate increases was bad news for gold, which investors sometimes buy when they are worried
about coming trouble. Gold gave back some of its recent gains. Oil was little changed.
The Fed's statement after its interest-rate meeting yesterday caught investors' eyes because it didn't include
a reference to rates being "accommodative," which appeared in earlier statements. That was taken as a
signal that the Fed thinks rates now are in the "neutral" area, meaning that, unless the Fed suddenly sees a
problem such as inflation looming, rates are about where they can stay.
The Fed also stopped talking about raising rates at a "measured" pace, sparking speculation that it might now
raise rates intermittently, instead of bumping them up a quarter-point at each meeting. Some investors began
to speculate that the Fed might take a pause in its rate increases at its Jan. 31 or March 28 meeting.
The January meeting will be Alan Greenspan's last as Fed chairman, while the March meeting would be Ben
Bernanke's first in that role. The Fed doesn't hold a February interest-rate meeting.

--- Page 202 ---
Page 202 of 233 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But the Fed also inserted a comment that "further measured policy firming" -- meaning more rate increases --
"is likely to be needed," and that was what helped stifle the optimism at day's end.
The S&P 500 rose 0.56% yesterday, or 7.00 points, to 1267.43, up 4.6% this year. The Nasdaq composite
advanced 0.18%, or 4.05 points, to 2265.00, up 4.1% for 2005.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks and bonds rose. The 10-year Treasury note rose 9/32, or $2.81 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 4.523%.
The dollar strengthened, to 120.12 yen, up from 119.72, while the euro fell against the dollar to $1.1929 from
$1.1952.

Natural Gas Hits Another Record As Cold Settles In
Commodities Report
Natural Gas Hits Another Record As Cold Settles In
By Spencer Jakab Dow Jones Newswires
450 words
14 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Natural-gas futures jumped to an all-time high for a front-month contract, shattering the previous record set
only three sessions earlier and settling above $15 for the first time.
January futures on the New York Mercantile Exchange rose as high as $15.78 and settled at $15.378 per
million British thermal units, up 53.7 cents and about 38 cents above its previous settlement high.
Although there is no indication that gas supplies are under strain in either the short term or for the season as
a whole, traders seem to have seized on the bullish momentum created by extremely cold temperatures in
major heating markets that are expected to continue through at least Dec. 27.
Adding to the bullish sentiment in the energy trading pits was the International Energy Agency's expectation
that global oil demand growth would recover next year. In its December report released Tuesday, the
Paris-based energy watchdog group forecast a 2.2% increase in growth in 2006.
Following a November that was one of the warmest on record for the Northeast and the Upper Midwest, the
two main areas of heating-fuel demand, December has been viciously cold. Weather futures indicate that it is
likely to be at least 20% colder than normal in Chicago and New York City. Consequently, the net withdrawal
of gas from underground storage for December as a whole is likely to be roughly 550 billion cubic feet based
on various analyst forecasts.
But, according to Martin King, a natural-gas analyst at FirstEnergy Capital, the all-time record withdrawal for
December was much higher at 720 billion cubic feet. He noted that the most likely outcome for underground
storage at the end of the season is just under one trillion cubic feet, slightly on the low side but not out of the
market's traditional comfort zone.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Futures at the Comex division of the Nymex fell on profit taking ahead of the Fed's rate increase.
Spot December fell $7.40 an ounce to $521.
COPPER: Prices on Comex rose as buyers returned to the market following Monday's selloff, banking on a
strong technical chart and fundamental outlook for the market. Spot December rose 1.75 cents to $2.1730 a
pound.
WHEAT: Prices at the Kansas City Board of Trade ended firmer, initially pressured by a Nafta panel ruling
allowing in Canadian wheat imports duty free, but rising at the close as traders bought back previously sold
positions. Spot December rose two cents to $3.53 a bushel.

High Hopes for Fed Moves Fail to Lift Stocks
Monday's Markets
High Hopes for Fed Moves Fail to Lift Stocks
By E.S. Browning
450 words
13 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS HAVE turned optimistic about what the Federal Reserve will say after today's meeting on
interest rates, but you wouldn't know it from the stock market's performance.
Hopes that the Fed -- after its expected 13th interest-rate increase in this cycle -- will signal a coming end to
rate increases were stifled by fears about rising oil prices, and stocks went nowhere. Gold futures, however,
continued to soar, jumping $1.40 to $528.40 an ounce, another 24-year high. The metal is benefiting from
worries about inflation, the dollar and international political tensions.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 10.81 points, or 0.1%, to 10767.77, down 0.1% since the year began.
The broader Standard &Poor's 500-stock index edged up 0.08%, or 1.06 points, to 1260.43, up 4% for 2005.
Investors widely expect the Fed to modify the statement it issues after its meeting, to suggest that it is getting
near the end of interest-rate increases. But that didn't put to rest concerns that the Fed still could raise rates
at two or more additional meetings, including today's, before it stops. So when oil prices pushed back above
$60 a barrel -- rising $1.91 to $61.30 -- stock indexes turned listless.
"We may be closer to the end than the beginning of the rate increases, but there are probably another couple
to come," said stock trader Michael Driscoll at New York brokerage firm Bear Stearns. "The Santa Claus rally
is probably going to be in a little bit of jeopardy unless we get some backup in energy prices."
The Dow industrials also suffered from news that a judge declared a mistrial in a lawsuit over
Dow-component Merck's painkiller Vioxx.
The dollar took its hardest fall in four months against the yen, amid fears that an end to U.S. interest-rate
increases will soften demand for dollars.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.19%, or 4.22 points, to 2260.95, up 3.9% this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board, where 1.41 billion shares traded, 1,779 stocks rose and 1,565 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 6/32, or $1.88 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 4.557%. The 30-year bond was down 7/32 to yield 4.749%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 119.72 yen, down from 120.64, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.1952 from $1.1817.

Crude Price Pushes Past $61, Fueled by Concerns Over Supply
Commodities Report
Crude Price Pushes Past $61, Fueled by Concerns Over Supply
By Leah McGrath Goodman Dow Jones Newswires
347 words
13 December 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Crude-oil futures settled above $61 a barrel, up nearly $2, rebounding to early November
levels on cold weather and signs that demand is rising and supply may remain tight.
January oil futures rose $1.91 to settle at $61.30 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Heating-oil
futures leapt 4.07 cents to $1.7725 a gallon and natural-gas futures rose 52.9 cents to $14.841 a million
British thermal units, just below the record-high settlement minted last week at $14.992.
Winter forecasts played no small part in backing the gains, and indications of climbing oil demand and tight
long-term petroleum supply fed the increase.
In a preview of a monthly report due today from the International Energy Agency, the head of the Paris-based
energy watchdog group said lower prices have encouraged a rebound in energy demand since autumn's U.S.
hurricanes.
The energy outlook for 2006 from the federal Energy Information Administration was also bullish.
Yesterday began with an anticipated decision by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries to keep
its targeted oil-output ceiling unchanged. The group tabled discussions about trimming production until Jan.
31.
OPEC is already pumping an estimated two million barrels a day more than its current ceiling allows. By
meeting in January, the group hopes to thwart any chances of a supply glut during the second quarter,
historically a low-demand period.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Futures on the Comex division of the Nymex rose to 25-year highs on speculative buying, but profit
taking curbed the highs. Spot December gained $1.40 an ounce to $528.40 and most-active February gained
$1.30 to $531.50.
LIVE CATTLE: Prices at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange rose, supported by news that Japan officially
eased its ban on U.S. cattle imports. December live cattle gained 0.55 cent to 92.47 cents a pound.
